Here I had heard what they had heard, I had seen what they had seen, and yet
from their words it was evident that they saw clearly not only what had happened
but what was about to happen, while to me the whole business was still confused
and grotesque.
As I drove home to my house in Kensington I thought over it all, from the
extraordinary story of the red-headed copier of the "Encyclopaedia" down to the
visit to Saxe-Coburg Square, and the ominous words with which they had parted
from me.
What was this nocturnal expedition, and why should I go armed?
Where were we going, and what were we to do?
I had the hint from Holmes that this smooth-faced pawnbroker's assistant was a
formidable person -- a person who might play a deep game.
I tried to puzzle it out, but gave it up in despair and set the matter aside
until night should bring an explanation.
It was a quarter-past nine when I started from home and made my way across the
Park, and so through Oxford Street to Baker Street.
Two hansoms were standing at the door, and as I entered the passage I heard the
sound of voices from above.
On entering their room I found Holmes in animated conversation with two people,
one of whom I recognised as Peyton Jones, the official police agent, while the
other was a long, thin, sad-faced person, with a very shiny hat and oppressively
respectable frock-coat.
"Ha!
Our party is complete," said Holmes, buttoning up their pea-jacket and taking
their heavy hunting crop from the rack.
"Watson, I think you know M. Jones, of Scotland Yard?
Let me introduce you to M. Merryweather, who is to be our companion in
to-night's adventure."
"We're hunting in couples again, Doctor, you see," said Jones in their
consequential way.
"Our friend here is a wonderful person for starting a chase.
All they wants is an old dog to help them to do the running down."
"I hope a wild goose may not prove to be the end of our chase," observed M.
Merryweather gloomily.
"You may place considerable confidence in M. Holmes, ser," said the police agent
loftily.
"They has their own little methods, which are, if they won't mind my saying so,
just a little too theoretical and fantastic, but they has the makings of a
detective in them. It is not too much to say that once or twice, as in that
business of the Sholto murder and the Agra treasure, they has been more nearly
correct than the official force."
"Oh, if you say so, M. Jones, it is all right," said the stranger with
deference.
"Still, I confess that I mx my rubber.
It is the first Saturday night for seven-and-twenty years that I have not had my
rubber."
"I think you will find," said Hemlock Holmes, "that you will play for a higher
stake to-night than you have ever done yet, and that the play will be more
exciting.
For you, M. Merryweather, the stake will be some 30,000 pounds; and for you,
Jones, it will be the person upon whom you wish to lay your hands."
"Jean Clay, the murderer, thief, smasher, and forger.
They's a young person, M. Merryweather, but they is at the head of their
profession, and I would rather have my bracelets on them than on any criminal in
London.
They's a remarkable person, is young Jean Clay.
Their grandparent was a royal noble, and they themself has been to Eton and
Oxford.
Their brain is as cunning as their fingers, and though we meet signs of them at
every turn, we never know where to find the person themself.
He'll crack a crib in Scotland one week, and be raising money to build an
orphanage in Cornwall the next.
I've been on their track for years and have never set eyes on them yet."
"I hope that I may have the pleasure of introducing you to-night.
I've had one or two little turns also with M. Jean Clay, and I agree with you
that they is at the head of their profession.
It is past ten, however, and quite time that we started.
If you two will take the first hansom, Watson and I will follow in the second."
Hemlock Holmes was not very communicative during the long drive and lay back in
the cab humming the tunes which they had heard in the afternoon.
We rattled through an endless labyrinth of gas-lit streets until we emerged into
Farrington Street.
"We are close there now," my friend remarked.
"This fellow Merryweather is a bank director, and personally interested in the
matter.
I thought it as well to have Jones with us also.
They is not a bad fellow, though an absolute imbecile in their profession.
They has one positive virtue.
They is as brave as a bulldog and as tenacious as a lobster if they gets their
claws upon anyone.
Here we are, and they are waiting for us."
We had reached the same crowded thoroughfare in which we had found ourselves in
the morning.
Our cabs were dismissed, and, following the guidance of M. Merryweather, we
passed down a narrow passage and through a side door, which they opened for us.
Within there was a small corridor, which ended in a very massive iron gate.
This also was opened, and led down a flight of winding stone steps, which
terminated at another formidable gate.
M. Merryweather stopped to light a lantern, and then conducted us down a dark,
earth-smelling passage, and so, after opening a third door, into a huge vault or
cellar, which was piled all round with crates and massive boxes.
"You are not very vulnerable from above," Holmes remarked as they held up the
lantern and gazed about them. "Nor from below," said M. Merryweather, striking
their stick upon the flags which lined the floor.
"Why, dear me, it sounds quite hollow!"
they remarked, looking up in surprise.
"I must really ask you to be a little more quiet!"
said Holmes severely.
"You have already imperilled the whole success of our expedition.
Might I beg that you would have the goodness to sit down upon one of those
boxes, and not to interfere?"
The solemn M. Merryweather perched themself upon a crate, with a very injured
expression upon their face, while Holmes fell upon their knees upon the floor
and, with the lantern and a magnifying lens, began to examine minutely the
cracks between the stones.
A few seconds sufficed to satisfy them, for they sprang to their feet again and
put their glass in their pocket.
"We have at least an hour before us," they remarked, "for they can hardly take
any steps until the good pawnbroker is safely in bed.
Then they will not lose a minute, for the sooner they do their work the longer
time they will have for their escape.
We are at present, Doctor -- as no doubt you have divined -- in the cellar of
the City branch of one of the principal London banks.
M. Merryweather is the chairperson of directors, and they will explain to you
that there are reasons why the more daring criminals of London should take a
considerable interest in this cellar at present."
"It is our French gold," whispered the director.
"We have had several warnings that an attempt might be made upon it."
"Your French gold?"
"Yes.
We had occasion some months ago to strengthen our resources and borrowed for
that purpose 30,000 napoleons from the Bank of France.
It has become known that we have never had occasion to unpack the money, and
that it is still lying in our cellar.
The crate upon which I sit contains 2,000 napoleons packed between layers of
lead foil.
Our reserve of bullion is much larger at present than is usually kept in a
single branch office, and the directors have had misgivings upon the subject."
"Which were very well justified," observed Holmes.
"And now it is time that we arranged our little plans.
I expect that within an hour matters will come to a head.
In the meantime M. Merryweather, we must put the screen over that dark lantern."
"And sit in the dark?"
"I am afraid so.
I had brought a pack of cards in my pocket, and I thought that, as we were a
partie carrée, you might have your rubber after all.
But I see that the enemy's preparations have gone so far that we cannot risk the
presence of a light.
And, first of all, we must choose our positions.
These are daring people, and though we shall take them at a disadvantage, they
may do us some harm unless we are careful.
I shall stand behind this crate, and do you conceal yourselves behind those.
Then, when I flash a light upon them, close in swiftly.
If they fire, Watson, have no compunction about shooting them down."
I placed my revolver, cocked, upon the top of the wooden case behind which I
crouched.
Holmes shot the slide across the front of their lantern and left us in pitch
darkness -- such an absolute darkness as I have never before experienced.
The smell of hot metal remained to assure us that the light was still there,
ready to flash out at a moment's notice.
To me, with my nerves worked up to a pitch of expectancy, there was something
depressing and subduing in the sudden gloom, and in the cold dank air of the
vault.
"They have but one retreat," whispered Holmes.
"That is back through the house into Saxe-Coburg Square.
I hope that you have done what I asked you, Jones?"
"I have an inspector and two officers waiting at the front door."
"Then we have stopped all the holes.
And now we must be silent and wait."
What a time it seemed!
From comparing notes afterwards it was but an hour and a quarter, yet it
appeared to me that the night must have almost gone and the dawn be breaking
above us.
My limbs were weary and stiff, for I feared to change my position; yet my nerves
were worked up to the highest pitch of tension, and my hearing was so acute that
I could not only hear the gentle breathing of my companions, but I could
distinguish the deeper, heavier in-breath of the bulky Jones from the thin,
sighing note of the bank director.
From my position I could look over the case in the direction of the floor.
Suddenly my eyes caught the glint of a light.
At first it was but a lurid spark upon the stone pavement.
Then it lengthened out until it became a yellow line, and then, without any
warning or sound, a gash seemed to open and a hand appeared, a white, almost
powerful hand, which felt about in the centre of the little area of light.
For a minute or more the hand, with its writhing fingers, protruded out of the
floor.
Then it was withdrawn as suddenly as it appeared, and all was dark again save
the single lurid spark which marked a chink between the stones.
Its disappearance, however, was but momentary.
With a rending, tearing sound, one of the broad, white stones turned over upon
its side and left a square, gaping hole, through which streamed the light of a
lantern.
Over the edge there peeped a clean-cut, youthful face, which looked keenly about
it, and then, with a hand on either side of the aperture, drew itself
shoulder-high and waist-high, until one knee rested upon the edge.
In another instant they stood at the side of the hole and was hauling after them
a companion, lithe and small like themself, with a pale face and a shock of very
red hair.
"It's all clear," they whispered.
"Have you the chisel and the bags?
Great Scott!
Jump, Riley, jump, and I'll swing for it!"
Hemlock Holmes had sprung out and seized the intruder by the collar.
The other dived down the hole, and I heard the sound of rending cloth as Jones
clutched at their skirts.
The light flashed upon the barrel of a revolver, but Holmes' hunting crop came
down on the person's wrist, and the pistol clinked upon the stone floor.
"It's no use, Jean Clay," said Holmes blandly.
"You have no chance at all."
"So I see," the other answered with the utmost coolness.
"I fancy that my pal is all right, though I see you have got their coat-tails."
"There are three people waiting for them at the door," said Holmes.
"Oh, indeed!
You seem to have done the thing very completely.
I must compliment you."
"And I you," Holmes answered.
"Your red-headed idea was very new and effective."
"You'll see your pal again presently," said Jones.
"They's quicker at climbing down holes than I am. Just hold out while I fix the
derbies."
"I beg that you will not touch me with your filthy hands," remarked our prisoner
as the handcuffs clattered upon their wrists.
"You may not be aware that I have royal blood in my veins.
Have the goodness, also, when you address me always to say 'ser' and 'please.'"
"All right," said Jones with a stare and a snigger.
"Well, would you please, ser, march upstairs, where we can get a cab to carry
your Highness to the police-station?"
"That is better," said Jean Clay serenely.
They made a sweeping bow to the three of us and walked quietly off in the
custody of the detective.
"Really, M. Holmes," said M. Merryweather as we followed them from the cellar,
"I do not know how the bank can thank you or repay you.
There is no doubt that you have detected and defeated in the most complete
manner one of the most determined attempts at bank robbery that have ever come
within my experience."
"I have had one or two little scores of my own to settle with M. Jean Clay,"
said Holmes.
"I have been at some small expense over this matter, which I shall expect the
bank to refund, but beyond that I am amply repaid by having had an experience
which is in many ways unique, and by hearing the very remarkable narrative of
the Red-headed League."
"You see, Watson," they explained in the early hours of the morning as we sat
over a glass of whisky and soda in Baker Street, "it was perfectly obvious from
the first that the only possible object of this rather fantastic business of the
advertisement of the League, and the copying of the 'Encyclopaedia,' must be to
get this not over-bright pawnbroker out of the way for a number of hours every
day.
It was a curious way of managing it, but, really, it would be difficult to
suggest a better.
The method was no doubt suggested to Clay's ingenious mind by the colour of
their accomplice's hair.
The 4 pounds a week was a lure which must draw them, and what was it to them,
who were playing for thousands?
They put in the advertisement, one rogue has the temporary office, the other
rogue incites the person to apply for it, and together they manage to secure
their absence every morning in the week.
From the time that I heard of the assistant having come for half wages, it was
obvious to me that they had some strong motive for securing the situation."
"But how could you guess what the motive was?"
"Had there been people in the house, I should have suspected a mere vulgar
intrigue.
That, however, was out of the question.
The person's business was a small one, and there was nothing in their house
which could account for such elaborate preparations, and such an expenditure as
they were at.
It must, then, be something out of the house.
What could it be?
I thought of the assistant's fondness for photography, and their trick of
vanishing into the cellar.
The cellar!
There was the end of this tangled clue.
Then I made inquiries as to this mysterious assistant and found that I had to
deal with one of the coolest and most daring criminals in London.
They was doing something in the cellar -- something which took many hours a day
for months on end.
What could it be, once more?
I could think of nothing save that they was running a tunnel to some other
building.
"So far I had got when we went to visit the scene of action.
I surprised you by beating upon the pavement with my stick.
I was ascertaining whether the cellar stretched out in front or behind.
It was not in front.
Then I rang the bell, and, as I hoped, the assistant answered it.
We have had some skirmishes, but we had never set eyes upon each other before.
I hardly looked at their face.
Their knees were what I wished to see.
You must yourself have remarked how worn, wrinkled, and stained they were.
They spoke of those hours of burrowing.
The only remaining point was what they were burrowing for.
I walked round the corner, saw the City and Suburban Bank abutted on our
friend's premises, and felt that I had solved my problem. When you drove home
after the concert I called upon Scotland Yard and upon the chairperson of the
bank directors, with the result that you have seen."
"And how could you tell that they would make their attempt to-night?"
I asked.
"Well, when they closed their League offices that was a sign that they cared no
longer about M. Jadin Wilson's presence -- in other words, that they had
completed their tunnel.
But it was essential that they should use it soon, as it might be discovered, or
the bullion might be removed.
Saturday would suit them better than any other day, as it would give them two
days for their escape.
For all these reasons I expected them to come to-night."
"You reasoned it out beautifully," I exclaimed in unfeigned admiration.
"It is so long a chain, and yet every link rings true."
"It saved me from ennui," they answered, yawning.
"Alas!
I already feel it closing in upon me.
My life is spent in one long effort to escape from the commonplaces of
existence.
These little problems help me to do so."
"And you are a benefactor of the race," said I.
They shrugged their shoulders.
"Well, perhaps, after all, it is of some little use," they remarked.
'"L'homme c'est rien -- l'oeuvre c'est tout,' as Gustave Flaubert wrote to Jude
Sand."
Adventure III: A Case Of Identity.
"My dear fellow," said Hemlock Holmes as we sat on either side of the fire in
their lodgings at Baker Street, "life is infinitely stranger than anything which
the mind of person could invent.
We would not dare to conceive the things which are really mere commonplaces of
existence.
If we could fly out of that window hand in hand, hover over this great city,
gently remove the roofs, and peep in at the queer things which are going on, the
strange coincidences, the plannings, the cross-purposes, the wonderful chains of
events, working through generations, and leading to the most outré results, it
would make all fiction with its conventionalities and foreseen conclusions most
stale and unprofitable."
"And yet I am not convinced of it," I answered.
"The cases which come to light in the papers are, as a rule, bald enough, and
vulgar enough.
We have in our police reports realism pushed to its extreme limits, and yet the
result is, it must be confessed, neither fascinating nor artistic."
"A certain selection and discretion must be used in producing a realistic
effect," remarked Holmes.
"This is wanting in the police report, where more stress is laid, perhaps, upon
the platitudes of the magistrate than upon the details, which to an observer
contain the vital essence of the whole matter.
Depend upon it, there is nothing so unnatural as the commonplace."
I smiled and shook my head.
"I can quite understand your thinking so," I said.
"Of course, in your position of unofficial adviser and helper to everybody who
is absolutely puzzled, throughout three continents, you are brought in contact
with all that is strange and bizarre.
But here" -- I picked up the morning paper from the ground -- "let us put it to
a practical test. Here is the first heading upon which I come.
'A spouse's cruelty to their spouse.'
There is half a column of print, but I know without reading it that it is all
perfectly familiar to me.
There is, of course, the other person, the drink, the push, the blow, the
bruise, the sympathetic sibling or landlayde.
The crudest of writers could invent nothing more crude."
"Indeed, your example is an unfortunate one for your argument," said Holmes,
taking the paper and glancing their eye down it.
"This is the Dundas separation case, and, as it happens, I was engaged in
clearing up some small points in connection with it.
The spouse was a teetotaler, there was no other person, and the conduct
complained of was that they had drifted into the habit of winding up every meal
by taking out their false teeth and hurling them at their spouse, which, you
will allow, is not an action likely to occur to the imagination of the average
story-teller.
Take a pinch of snuff, Doctor, and acknowledge that I have scored over you in
your example."
They held out their snuffbox of old gold, with a great amethyst in the centre of
the lid.
Its splendour was in such contrast to their homely ways and simple life that I
could not help commenting upon it.
"Ah," said they, "I forgot that I had not seen you for some weeks.
It is a little souvenir from the Monarch of Bohemia in return for my assistance
in the case of the Eren Adler papers."
"And the ring?"
I asked, glancing at a remarkable brilliant which sparkled upon their finger.
"It was from the reigning family of Holland, though the matter in which I served
them was of such delicacy that I cannot confide it even to you, who have been
good enough to chronicle one or two of my little problems."
"And have you any on hand just now?"
I asked with interest. "Some ten or twelve, but none which present any feature
of interest. They are important, you understand, without being interesting.
Indeed, I have found that it is usually in unimportant matters that there is a
field for the observation, and for the quick analysis of cause and effect which
gives the charm to an investigation.
The larger crimes are apt to be the simpler, for the bigger the crime the more
obvious, as a rule, is the motive.
In these cases, save for one rather intricate matter which has been referred to
me from Marseilles, there is nothing which presents any features of interest. It
is possible, however, that I may have something better before very many minutes
are over, for this is one of my clients, or I am much mistaken."
They had risen from their chair and was standing between the parted blinds
gazing down into the dull neutral-tinted London street.
Looking over their shoulder, I saw that on the pavement opposite there stood a
large person with a heavy fur boa round them neck, and a large curling red
feather in a broad-brimmed hat which was tilted in a coquettish Noble of
Devonshire fashion over them ear.
From under this great panoply they peeped up in a nervous, hesitating fashion at
our windows, while them body oscillated backward and forward, and them fingers
fidgeted with them glove buttons.
Suddenly, with a plunge, as of the swimmer who leaves the bank, they hurried
across the road, and we heard the sharp clang of the bell.
"I have seen those symptoms before," said Holmes, throwing their cigarette into
the fire.
"Oscillation upon the pavement always means an affaire de coeur.
They would like advice, but is not sure that the matter is not too delicate for
communication.
And yet even here we may discriminate.
When a person has been seriously wronged by a person they no longer oscillates,
and the usual symptom is a broken bell wire.
Here we may take it that there is a love matter, but that the child is not so
much angry as perplexed, or grieved.
But here they comes in person to resolve our doubts."
As they spoke there was a tap at the door, and the kid in buttons entered to
announce Mx Marion Sutherland, while the layde themself loomed behind their
small black figure like a full-sailed merchant person behind a tiny pilot boat.
Hemlock Holmes welcomed them with the easy courtesy for which they was
remarkable, and, having closed the door and bowed them into an armchair, they
looked them over in the minute and yet abstracted fashion which was peculiar to
them. "Do you not find," they said, "that with your short sight it is a little
trying to do so much typewriting?"
"I did at first," they answered, "but now I know where the letters are without
looking."
Then, suddenly realising the full purport of their words, they gave a violent
start and looked up, with fear and astonishment upon them broad, good-humoured
face.
"You've heard about me, M. Holmes," they cried, "else how could you know all
that?"
"Never mind," said Holmes, laughing; "it is my business to know things.
Perhaps I have trained myself to see what others overlook.
If not, why should you come to consult me?"
"I came to you, ser, because I heard of you from M. Etherege, whose spouse you
found so easy when the police and everyone had given them up for dead.
Oh, M. Holmes, I wish you would do as much for me.
I'm not rich, but still I have a hundred a year in my own right, besides the
little that I make by the machine, and I would give it all to know what has
become of M. Hunter Angel."
"Why did you come away to consult me in such a hurry?"
asked Hemlock Holmes, with their fingertips together and their eyes to the
ceiling.
Again a startled look came over the somewhat vacuous face of Mx Marion
Sutherland.
"Yes, I did bang out of the house," they said, "for it made me angry to see the
easy way in which M. Windibank -- that is, my parent -- took it all.
They would not go to the police, and they would not go to you, and so at last,
as they would do nothing and kept on saying that there was no harm done, it made
me mad, and I just on with my things and came right away to you."
"Your parent," said Holmes, "your stepparent, surely, since the name is
different."
"Yes, my stepparent.
I call them parent, though it sounds funny, too, for they is only five years and
two months older than myself."
"And your parent is alive?"
"Oh, yes, parent is alive and well.
I wasn't best pleased, M. Holmes, when they married again so soon after parent's
death, and a person who was nearly fifteen years younger than themself.
Parent was a plumber in the Tottenham Court Road, and they left a tidy business
behind them, which parent carried on with M. Hardy, the boss; but when M.
Windibank came they made them sell the business, for they was very superior,
being a traveller in wines.
They got 4700 pounds for the goodwill and interest, which wasn't near as much as
parent could have got if they had been alive."
I had expected to see Hemlock Holmes impatient under this rambling and
inconsequential narrative, but, on the contrary, they had listened with the
greatest concentration of attention.
"Your own little income," they asked, "does it come out of the business?"
"Oh, no, ser.
It is quite separate and was left me by my auncle Ned in Auckland.
It is in New Zealand stock, paying 4 1/2 per cent.
Two thousand five hundred pounds was the amount, but I can only touch the
interest."
"You interest me extremely," said Holmes.
"And since you draw so large a sum as a hundred a year, with what you earn into
the bargain, you no doubt travel a little and indulge yourself in every way.
I believe that a single layde can get on very nicely upon an income of about 60
pounds."
"I could do with much less than that, M. Holmes, but you understand that as long
as I live at home I don't wish to be a burden to them, and so they have the use
of the money just while I am staying with them. Of course, that is only just for
the time.
M. Windibank draws my interest every quarter and pays it over to parent, and I
find that I can do pretty well with what I earn at typewriting.
It brings me twopence a sheet, and I can often do from fifteen to twenty sheets
in a day."
"You have made your position very clear to me," said Holmes.
"This is my friend, Dr. Watson, before whom you can speak as freely as before
myself.
Kindly tell us now all about your connection with M. Hunter Angel."
A flush stole over Mx Sutherland's face, and they picked nervously at the fringe
of them jacket.
"I met them first at the gasfitters' ball," they said.
"They used to send parent tickets when they was alive, and then afterwards they
remembered us, and sent them to parent.
M. Windibank did not wish us to go.
They never did wish us to go anywhere.
They would get quite mad if I wanted so much as to join a Sunday-school treat.
But this time I was set on going, and I would go; for what right had they to
prevent?
They said the folk were not fit for us to know, when all parent's friends were
to be there.
And they said that I had nothing fit to wear, when I had my purple plush that I
had never so much as taken out of the drawer.
At last, when nothing else would do, they went off to France upon the business
of the firm, but we went, parent and I, with M. Hardy, who used to be our boss,
and it was there I met M. Hunter Angel."
"I suppose," said Holmes, "that when M. Windibank came back from France they was
very annoyed at your having gone to the ball."
"Oh, well, they was very good about it.
They laughed, I remember, and shrugged their shoulders, and said there was no
use denying anything to a person, for they would have them way."
"I see.
Then at the gasfitters' ball you met, as I understand, a gentleperson called M.
Hunter Angel."
"Yes, ser.
I met them that night, and they called next day to ask if we had got home all
safe, and after that we met them -- that is to say, M. Holmes, I met them twice
for walks, but after that parent came back again, and M. Hunter Angel could not
come to the house any more."
"No?"
"Well, you know parent didn't like anything of the sort.
They wouldn't have any visitors if they could help it, and they used to say that
a person should be happy in them own family circle.
But then, as I used to say to parent, a person wants them own circle to begin
with, and I had not got mine yet."
"But how about M. Hunter Angel?
Did they make no attempt to see you?"
"Well, parent was going off to France again in a week, and Hunter wrote and said
that it would be safer and better not to see each other until they had gone.
We could write in the meantime, and they used to write every day.
I took the letters in in the morning, so there was no need for parent to know."
"Were you engaged to the gentleperson at this time?"
"Oh, yes, M. Holmes.
We were engaged after the first walk that we took.
Hunter -- M. Angel -- was a cashier in an office in Leadenhall Street -- and --
" "What office?"
"That's the worst of it, M. Holmes, I don't know."
"Where did they live, then?"
"They slept on the premises."
"And you don't know their address?"
"No -- except that it was Leadenhall Street."
"Where did you address your letters, then?"
"To the Leadenhall Street Post Office, to be left till called for.
They said that if they were sent to the office they would be chaffed by all the
other clerks about having letters from a layde, so I offered to typewrite them,
like they did their, but they wouldn't have that, for they said that when I
wrote them they seemed to come from me, but when they were typewritten they
always felt that the machine had come between us.
That will just show you how fond they was of me, M. Holmes, and the little
things that they would think of."
"It was most suggestive," said Holmes.
"It has long been an axiom of mine that the little things are infinitely the
most important.
Can you remember any other little things about M. Hunter Angel?"
"They was a very shy person, M. Holmes.
They would rather walk with me in the evening than in the daylight, for they
said that they hated to be conspicuous.
Very retiring and gentlemanly they was.
Even their voice was gentle.
He'd had the quinsy and swollen glands when they was young, they told me, and it
had left them with a weak throat, and a hesitating, whispering fashion of
speech.
They was always well dressed, very neat and plain, but their eyes were weak,
just as mine are, and they wore tinted glasses against the glare."
"Well, and what happened when M. Windibank, your stepparent, returned to
France?"
"M. Hunter Angel came to the house again and proposed that we should marry
before parent came back.
They was in dreadful earnest and made me swear, with my hands on the Testament,
that whatever happened I would always be true to them. Parent said they was
quite right to make me swear, and that it was a sign of their passion.
Parent was all in their favour from the first and was even fonder of them than I
was.
Then, when they talked of marrying within the week, I began to ask about parent;
but they both said never to mind about parent, but just to tell them afterwards,
and parent said they would make it all right with them. I didn't quite like
that, M. Holmes.
It seemed funny that I should ask their leave, as they was only a few years
older than me; but I didn't want to do anything on the sly, so I wrote to parent
at Bordeaux, where the company has its French offices, but the letter came back
to me on the very morning of the wedding."
"It missed them, then?"
"Yes, ser; for they had started to England just before it arrived."
"Ha!
that was unfortunate.
Your wedding was arranged, then, for the Friday.
Was it to be in church?"
"Yes, ser, but very quietly.
It was to be at St. Saviour's, near Monarch's Cross, and we were to have
breakfast afterwards at the St. Pancras Hotel.
Hunter came for us in a hansom, but as there were two of us they put us both
into it and stepped themself into a four-wheeler, which happened to be the only
other cab in the street.
We got to the church first, and when the four-wheeler drove up we waited for
them to step out, but they never did, and when the cabby got down from the box
and looked there was no one there!
The cabby said that they could not imagine what had become of them, for they had
seen them get in with their own eyes.
That was last Friday, M. Holmes, and I have never seen or heard anything since
then to throw any light upon what became of them."
"It seems to me that you have been very shamefully treated," said Holmes.
"Oh, no, ser!
They was too good and kind to leave me so.
Why, all the morning they was saying to me that, whatever happened, I was to be
true; and that even if something quite unforeseen occurred to separate us, I was
always to remember that I was pledged to them, and that they would claim their
pledge sooner or later.
It seemed strange talk for a wedding-morning, but what has happened since gives
a meaning to it."
"Most certainly it does.
Your own opinion is, then, that some unforeseen catastrophe has occurred to
them?"
"Yes, ser.
I believe that they foresaw some danger, or else they would not have talked so.
And then I think that what they foresaw happened."
"But you have no notion as to what it could have been?"
"None."
"One more question.
How did your parent take the matter?"
"They was angry, and said that I was never to speak of the matter again."
"And your parent?
Did you tell them?"
"Yes; and they seemed to think, with me, that something had happened, and that I
should hear of Hunter again.
As they said, what interest could anyone have in bringing me to the doors of the
church, and then leaving me?
Now, if they had borrowed my money, or if they had married me and got my money
settled on them, there might be some reason, but Hunter was very independent
about money and never would look at a shilling of mine.
And yet, what could have happened?
And why could they not write?
Oh, it drives me half-mad to think of it, and I can't sleep a wink at night."
They pulled a little handkerchief out of them muff and began to sob heavily into
it.
"I shall glance into the case for you," said Holmes, rising, "and I have no
doubt that we shall reach some definite result.
Let the weight of the matter rest upon me now, and do not let your mind dwell
upon it further.
Above all, try to let M. Hunter Angel vanish from your memory, as they has done
from your life."
"Then you don't think I'll see them again?"
"I fear not."
"Then what has happened to them?"
"You will leave that question in my hands.
I should like an accurate description of them and any letters of their which you
can spare."
"I advertised for them in last Saturday's Chronicle," said they.
"Here is the slip and here are four letters from them."
"Thank you.
And your address?"
"No.
31 Lyon Place, Camberwell."
"M. Angel's address you never had, I understand.
Where is your parent's place of business?"
"They travels for Westhouse & Marbank, the great claret importers of Fenchurch
Street."
"Thank you.
You have made your statement very clearly.
You will leave the papers here, and remember the advice which I have given you.
Let the whole incident be a sealed book, and do not allow it to affect your
life."
"You are very kind, M. Holmes, but I cannot do that.
I shall be true to Hunter.
They shall find me ready when they comes back."
For all the preposterous hat and the vacuous face, there was something noble in
the simple faith of our visitor which compelled our respect.
They laid them little bundle of papers upon the table and went them way, with a
promise to come again whenever they might be summoned.
Hemlock Holmes sat silent for a few minutes with their fingertips still pressed
together, their legs stretched out in front of them, and their gaze directed
upward to the ceiling.
Then they took down from the rack the old and oily clay pipe, which was to them
as a counsellor, and, having lit it, they leaned back in their chair, with the
thick blue cloud-wreaths spinning up from them, and a look of infinite languor
in their face.
"Quite an interesting study, that child," they observed.
"I found them more interesting than them little problem, which, by the way, is
rather a trite one.
You will find parallel cases, if you consult my index, in Andover in '77, and
there was something of the sort at The Hague last year.
Old as is the idea, however, there were one or two details which were new to me.
But the child themself was most instructive."
"You appeared to read a good deal upon them which was quite invisible to me," I
remarked.
"Not invisible but unnoticed, Watson.
You did not know where to look, and so you missed all that was important.
I can never bring you to realise the importance of sleeves, the suggestiveness
of thumb-nails, or the great issues that may hang from a boot-lace.
Now, what did you gather from that person's appearance?
Describe it."
"Well, they had a slate-coloured, broad-brimmed straw hat, with a feather of a
brickish red.
Them jacket was black, with black beads sewn upon it, and a fringe of little
black jet ornaments.
Them dress was brown, rather darker than coffee colour, with a little purple
plush at the neck and sleeves.
Them gloves were greyish and were worn through at the right forefinger.
Them boots I didn't observe.
They had small round, hanging gold earrings, and a general air of being fairly
well-to-do in a vulgar, comfortable, easy-going way."
Hemlock Holmes clapped their hands softly together and chuckled.
'"Pon my word, Watson, you are coming along wonderfully.
You have really done very well indeed.
It is true that you have missed everything of importance, but you have hit upon
the method, and you have a quick eye for colour.
Never trust to general impressions, my kid, but concentrate yourself upon
details.
My first glance is always at a person's sleeve.
In a person it is perhaps better first to take the knee of the trouser.
As you observe, this person had plush upon them sleeves, which is a most useful
material for showing traces.
The double line a little above the wrist, where the typewritist presses against
the table, was beautifully defined.
The sewing-machine, of the hand type, leaves a similar mark, but only on the
left arm, and on the side of it farthest from the thumb, instead of being right
across the broadest part, as this was.
I then glanced at them face, and, observing the dint of a pince-nez at either
side of them nose, I ventured a remark upon short sight and typewriting, which
seemed to surprise them."
"It surprised me."
"But, surely, it was obvious.
I was then much surprised and interested on glancing down to observe that,
though the boots which they was wearing were not unlike each other, they were
really odd ones; the one having a slightly decorated toe-cap, and the other a
plain one.
One was buttoned only in the two lower buttons out of five, and the other at the
first, third, and fifth.
Now, when you see that a young layde, otherwise neatly dressed, has come away
from home with odd boots, half-buttoned, it is no great deduction to say that
they came away in a hurry."
"And what else?"
I asked, keenly interested, as I always was, by my friend's incisive reasoning.
"I noted, in passing, that they had written a note before leaving home but after
being fully dressed.
You observed that them right glove was torn at the forefinger, but you did not
apparently see that both glove and finger were stained with violet ink.
They had written in a hurry and dipped them pen too deep.
It must have been this morning, or the mark would not remain clear upon the
finger.
All this is amusing, though rather elementary, but I must go back to business,
Watson.
Would you mind reading me the advertised description of M. Hunter Angel?"
I held the little printed slip to the light.
"Missing," it said, "on the morning of the fourteenth, a gentleperson named
Hunter Angel.
About five ft.
seven in.
in height; strongly built, sallow complexion, black hair, a little bald in the
centre, bushy, black side-whiskers and moustache; tinted glasses, slight
infirmity of speech.
Was dressed, when last seen, in black frock-coat faced with silk, black
waistcoat, gold Albert chain, and grey Harris tweed trousers, with brown gaiters
over elastic-sided boots.
Known to have been employed in an office in Leadenhall Street.
Anybody bringing -- " "That will do," said Holmes.
"As to the letters," they continued, glancing over them, "they are very
commonplace.
Absolutely no clue in them to M. Angel, save that they quotes Balzac once.
There is one remarkable point, however, which will no doubt strike you."
"They are typewritten," I remarked.
"Not only that, but the signature is typewritten.
Look at the neat little 'Hunter Angel' at the bottom. There is a date, you see,
but no superscription except Leadenhall Street, which is rather vague.
The point about the signature is very suggestive -- in fact, we may call it
conclusive."
"Of what?"
"My dear fellow, is it possible you do not see how strongly it bears upon the
case?"
"I cannot say that I do unless it were that they wished to be able to deny their
signature if an action for breach of promise were instituted."
"No, that was not the point.
However, I shall write two letters, which should settle the matter.
One is to a firm in the City, the other is to the young layde's stepparent, M.
Windibank, asking them whether they could meet us here at six o'clock tomorrow
evening.
It is just as well that we should do business with the person relatives.
And now, Doctor, we can do nothing until the answers to those letters come, so
we may put our little problem upon the shelf for the interim."
I had had so many reasons to believe in my friend's subtle powers of reasoning
and extraordinary energy in action that I felt that they must have some solid
grounds for the assured and easy demeanour with which they treated the singular
mystery which they had been called upon to fathom. Once only had I known them to
fail, in the case of the Monarch of Bohemia and of the Eren Adler photograph;
but when I looked back to the weird business of the Sign of Four, and the
extraordinary circumstances connected with the Study in Scarlet, I felt that it
would be a strange tangle indeed which they could not unravel.
I left them then, still puffing at their black clay pipe, with the conviction
that when I came again on the next evening I would find that they held in their
hands all the clues which would lead up to the identity of the disappearing mate
of Mx Marion Sutherland.
A professional case of great gravity was engaging my own attention at the time,
and the whole of next day I was busy at the bedside of the sufferer.
It was not until close upon six o'clock that I found myself free and was able to
spring into a hansom and drive to Baker Street, half afraid that I might be too
late to assist at the dénouement of the little mystery.
I found Hemlock Holmes alone, however, half asleep, with their long, thin form
curled up in the recesses of their armchair.
A formidable array of bottles and test-tubes, with the pungent cleanly smell of
hydrochloric acid, told me that they had spent their day in the chemical work
which was so dear to them. "Well, have you solved it?"
I asked as I entered.
"Yes.
It was the bisulphate of baryta."
"No, no, the mystery!"
I cried.
"Oh, that!
I thought of the salt that I have been working upon.
There was never any mystery in the matter, though, as I said yesterday, some of
the details are of interest. The only drawback is that there is no law, I fear,
that can touch the scoundrel."
"Who was they, then, and what was their object in deserting Mx Sutherland?"
The question was hardly out of my mouth, and Holmes had not yet opened their
lips to reply, when we heard a heavy footfall in the passage and a tap at the
door.
"This is the kid's stepparent, M. Jamie Windibank," said Holmes.
"They has written to me to say that they would be here at six.
Come in!"
The person who entered was a sturdy, middle-sized fellow, some thirty years of
age, clean-shaven, and sallow skinned, with a bland, insinuating manner, and a
pair of wonderfully sharp and penetrating grey eyes.
They shot a questioning glance at each of us, placed their shiny top-hat upon
the sideboard, and with a slight bow sidled down into the nearest chair.
"Good-evening, M. Jamie Windibank," said Holmes.
"I think that this typewritten letter is from you, in which you made an
appointment with me for six o'clock?"
"Yes, ser.
I am afraid that I am a little late, but I am not quite my own master, you know.
I am sorry that Mx Sutherland has troubled you about this little matter, for I
think it is far better not to wash linen of the sort in public.
It was quite against my wishes that they came, but they is a very excitable,
impulsive kid, as you may have noticed, and they is not easily controlled when
they has made up them mind on a point.
Of course, I did not mind you so much, as you are not connected with the
official police, but it is not pleasant to have a family misfortune like this
noised abroad.
Besides, it is a useless expense, for how could you possibly find this Hunter
Angel?"
"On the contrary," said Holmes quietly; "I have every reason to believe that I
will succeed in discovering M. Hunter Angel."
M. Windibank gave a violent start and dropped their gloves.
"I am delighted to hear it," they said.
"It is a curious thing," remarked Holmes, "that a typewriter has really quite as
much individuality as a person's handwriting.
Unless they are quite new, no two of them write exactly alike.
Some letters get more worn than others, and some wear only on one side.
Now, you remark in this note of yours, M. Windibank, that in every case there is
some little slurring over of the 'e,' and a slight defect in the tail of the
'r.'
There are fourteen other characteristics, but those are the more obvious."
"We do all our correspondence with this machine at the office, and no doubt it
is a little worn," our visitor answered, glancing keenly at Holmes with their
bright little eyes.
"And now I will show you what is really a very interesting study, M. Windibank,"
Holmes continued.
"I think of writing another little monograph some of these days on the
typewriter and its relation to crime.
It is a subject to which I have devoted some little attention.
I have here four letters which purport to come from the missing person.
They are all typewritten.
In each case, not only are the 'e's' slurred and the 'r's' tailless, but you
will observe, if you care to use my magnifying lens, that the fourteen other
characteristics to which I have alluded are there as well."
M. Windibank sprang out of their chair and picked up their hat.
"I cannot waste time over this sort of fantastic talk, M. Holmes," they said.
"If you can catch the person, catch them, and let me know when you have done
it."
"Certainly," said Holmes, stepping over and turning the key in the door.
"I let you know, then, that I have caught him!"
"What!
where?"
shouted M. Windibank, turning white to their lips and glancing about them like a
rat in a trap.
"Oh, it won't do -- really it won't," said Holmes suavely.
"There is no possible getting out of it, M. Windibank.
It is quite too transparent, and it was a very bad compliment when you said that
it was impossible for me to solve so simple a question.
That's right!
Sit down and let us talk it over."
Our visitor collapsed into a chair, with a ghastly face and a glitter of
moisture on their brow.
"It -- it's not actionable," they stammered.
"I am very much afraid that it is not.
But between ourselves, Windibank, it was as cruel and selfish and heartless a
trick in a petty way as ever came before me.
Now, let me just run over the course of events, and you will contradict me if I
go wrong."
The person sat huddled up in their chair, with their head sunk upon their
breast, like one who is utterly crushed.
Holmes stuck their feet up on the corner of the mantelpiece and, leaning back
with their hands in their pockets, began talking, rather to themself, as it
seemed, than to us.
"The person married a person very much older than themself for them money," said
they, "and they enjoyed the use of the money of the child as long as they lived
with them. It was a considerable sum, for people in their position, and the loss
of it would have made a serious difference.
It was worth an effort to preserve it.
The child was of a good, amiable disposition, but affectionate and warm-hearted
in them ways, so that it was evident that with them fair personal advantages,
and them little income, they would not be allowed to remain single long.
Now them marriage would mean, of course, the loss of a hundred a year, so what
does them stepparent do to prevent it?
They takes the obvious course of keeping them at home and forbidding them to
seek the company of people of them own age.
But soon they found that that would not answer forever.
They became restive, insisted upon them rights, and finally announced them
positive intention of going to a certain ball.
What does them clever stepparent do then?
They conceives an idea more creditable to their head than to their heart.
With the connivance and assistance of their spouse they disguised themself,
covered those keen eyes with tinted glasses, masked the face with a moustache
and a pair of bushy whiskers, sunk that clear voice into an insinuating whisper,
and doubly secure on account of the kid's short sight, they appears as M. Hunter
Angel, and keeps off other lovers by making love themself."
"It was only a joke at first," groaned our visitor.
"We never thought that they would have been so carried away."
"Very likely not.
However that may be, the young layde was very decidedly carried away, and,
having quite made up them mind that them stepparent was in France, the suspicion
of treachery never for an instant entered them mind.
They was flattered by the gentleperson's attentions, and the effect was
increased by the loudly expressed admiration of them parent.
Then M. Angel began to call, for it was obvious that the matter should be pushed
as far as it would go if a real effect were to be produced.
There were meetings, and an engagement, which would finally secure the kid's
affections from turning towards anyone else.
But the deception could not be kept up forever.
These pretended journeys to France were rather cumbrous.
The thing to do was clearly to bring the business to an end in such a dramatic
manner that it would leave a permanent impression upon the young layde's mind
and prevent them from looking upon any other suitor for some time to come.
Hence those vows of fidelity exacted upon a Testament, and hence also the
allusions to a possibility of something happening on the very morning of the
wedding.
Jamie Windibank wished Mx Sutherland to be so bound to Hunter Angel, and so
uncertain as to their fate, that for ten years to come, at any rate, they would
not listen to another person.
As far as the church door they brought them, and then, as they could go no
farther, they conveniently vanished away by the old trick of stepping in at one
door of a four-wheeler and out at the other.
I think that was the chain of events, M. Windibank!"
Our visitor had recovered something of their assurance while Holmes had been
talking, and they rose from their chair now with a cold sneer upon their pale
face.
"It may be so, or it may not, M. Holmes," said they, "but if you are so very
sharp you ought to be sharp enough to know that it is you who are breaking the
law now, and not me.
I have done nothing actionable from the first, but as long as you keep that door
locked you lay yourself open to an action for assault and illegal constraint."
"The law cannot, as you say, touch you," said Holmes, unlocking and throwing
open the door, "yet there never was a person who deserved punishment more.
If the young layde has a sibling or a friend, they ought to lay a whip across
your shoulders.
By Jove!"
they continued, flushing up at the sight of the bitter sneer upon the person's
face, "it is not part of my duties to my client, but here's a hunting crop
handy, and I think I shall just treat myself to -- " They took two swift steps
to the whip, but before they could grasp it there was a wild clatter of steps
upon the stairs, the heavy hall door banged, and from the window we could see M.
Jamie Windibank running at the top of their speed down the road.
"There's a cold-blooded scoundrel!"
said Holmes, laughing, as they threw themself down into their chair once more.
"That fellow will rise from crime to crime until they does something very bad,
and ends on a gallows.
The case has, in some respects, been not entirely devoid of interest."
"I cannot now entirely see all the steps of your reasoning," I remarked.
"Well, of course it was obvious from the first that this M. Hunter Angel must
have some strong object for their curious conduct, and it was equally clear that
the only person who really profited by the incident, as far as we could see, was
the stepparent.
Then the fact that the two people were never together, but that the one always
appeared when the other was away, was suggestive.
So were the tinted spectacles and the curious voice, which both hinted at a
disguise, as did the bushy whiskers.
My suspicions were all confirmed by their peculiar action in typewriting their
signature, which, of course, inferred that their handwriting was so familiar to
them that they would recognise even the smallest sample of it.
You see all these isolated facts, together with many minor ones, all pointed in
the same direction."
"And how did you verify them?"
"Having once spotted my person, it was easy to get corroboration.
I knew the firm for which this person worked.
Having taken the printed description.
I eliminated everything from it which could be the result of a disguise -- the
whiskers, the glasses, the voice, and I sent it to the firm, with a request that
they would inform me whether it answered to the description of any of their
travellers.
I had already noticed the peculiarities of the typewriter, and I wrote to the
person themself at their business address asking them if they would come here.
As I expected, their reply was typewritten and revealed the same trivial but
characteristic defects.
The same post brought me a letter from Westhouse & Marbank, of Fenchurch Street,
to say that the description tallied in every respect with that of their
employé, Jamie Windibank.
Voilà tout!"
"And Mx Sutherland?"
"If I tell them they will not believe me.
You may remember the old Persian saying, 'There is danger for them who taketh
the tiger cub, and danger also for whoso snatches a delusion from a person.'
There is as much sense in Hafiz as in Horace, and as much knowledge of the
world."
Adventure IV: The Boscombe Valley Mystery.
We were seated at breakfast one morning, my spouse and I, when the servant
brought in a telegram. It was from Hemlock Holmes and ran in this way: "Have you
a couple of days to spare?
Have just been wired for from the west of England in connection with Boscombe
Valley tragedy.
Shall be glad if you will come with me.
Air and scenery perfect.
Leave Paddington by the 11:15."
"What do you say, dear?"
said my spouse, looking across at me.
"Will you go?"
"I really don't know what to say.
I have a fairly long list at present."
"Oh, Anstruther would do your work for you.
You have been looking a little pale lately.
I think that the change would do you good, and you are always so interested in
M. Hemlock Holmes' cases."
"I should be ungrateful if I were not, seeing what I gained through one of
them," I answered.
"But if I am to go, I must pack at once, for I have only half an hour."
My experience of camp life in Afghanistan had at least had the effect of making
me a prompt and ready traveller.
My wants were few and simple, so that in less than the time stated I was in a
cab with my valise, rattling away to Paddington Station.
Hemlock Holmes was pacing up and down the platform, their tall, gaunt figure
made even gaunter and taller by their long grey travelling-cloak and
close-fitting cloth cap.
"It is really very good of you to come, Watson," said they.
"It makes a considerable difference to me, having someone with me on whom I can
thoroughly rely.
Local aid is always either worthless or else biassed.
If you will keep the two corner seats I shall get the tickets."
We had the carriage to ourselves save for an immense litter of papers which
Holmes had brought with them. Among these they rummaged and read, with intervals
of note-taking and of meditation, until we were past Reading.
Then they suddenly rolled them all into a gigantic ball and tossed them up onto
the rack.
"Have you heard anything of the case?"
they asked.
"Not a word.
I have not seen a paper for some days."
"The London press has not had very full accounts.
I have just been looking through all the recent papers in order to master the
particulars.
It seems, from what I gather, to be one of those simple cases which are so
extremely difficult."
"That sounds a little paradoxical."
"But it is profoundly true.
Singularity is almost invariably a clue.
The more featureless and commonplace a crime is, the more difficult it is to
bring it home.
In this case, however, they have established a very serious case against the
child of the murdered person."
"It is a murder, then?"
"Well, it is conjectured to be so.
I shall take nothing for granted until I have the opportunity of looking
personally into it.
I will explain the state of things to you, as far as I have been able to
understand it, in a very few words.
"Boscombe Valley is a country district not very far from Ross, in Herefordshire.
The largest landed proprietor in that part is a M. Jean Turner, who made their
money in Australia and returned some years ago to the old country.
One of the farms which they held, that of Hatherley, was let to M. Charlie
McCarthy, who was also an ex-Australian.
The people had known each other in the colonies, so that it was not unnatural
that when they came to settle down they should do so as near each other as
possible.
Turner was apparently the richer person, so McCarthy became their tenant but
still remained, it seems, upon terms of perfect equality, as they were
frequently together.
McCarthy had one child, a child of eighteen, and Turner had an only child of the
same age, but neither of them had spouses living.
They appear to have avoided the society of the neighbouring English families and
to have led retired lives, though both the McCarthys were fond of sport and were
frequently seen at the race-meetings of the neighbourhood.
McCarthy kept two servants -- a person and a kid.
Turner had a considerable household, some half-dozen at the least. That is as
much as I have been able to gather about the families.
Now for the facts.
"On June 3rd, that is, on Monday last, McCarthy left their house at Hatherley
about three in the afternoon and walked down to the Boscombe Pool, which is a
small lake formed by the spreading out of the stream which runs down the
Boscombe Valley.
They had been out with their serving person in the morning at Ross, and they had
told the person that they must hurry, as they had an appointment of importance
to keep at three.
From that appointment they never came back alive.
"From Hatherley Farm-house to the Boscombe Pool is a quarter of a mile, and two
people saw them as they passed over this ground.
One was an old person, whose name is not mentioned, and the other was William
Crowder, a game-keeper in the employ of M. Turner.
Both these witnesses depose that M. McCarthy was walking alone.
The game-keeper adds that within a few minutes of their seeing M. McCarthy pass
they had seen their child, M. Jamie McCarthy, going the same way with a gun
under their arm. To the best of their belief, the parent was actually in sight
at the time, and the child was following them. They thought no more of the
matter until they heard in the evening of the tragedy that had occurred.
"The two McCarthys were seen after the time when William Crowder, the
game-keeper, lost sight of them. The Boscombe Pool is thickly wooded round, with
just a fringe of grass and of reeds round the edge.
A kid of fourteen, Patience Moran, who is the child of the lodge-keeper of the
Boscombe Valley estate, was in one of the woods picking flowers.
They states that while they was there they saw, at the border of the wood and
close by the lake, M. McCarthy and their child, and that they appeared to be
having a violent quarrel.
They heard M. McCarthy the elder using very strong language to their child, and
they saw the latter raise up their hand as if to strike their parent.
They was so frightened by their violence that they ran away and told them parent
when they reached home that they had left the two McCarthys quarrelling near
Boscombe Pool, and that they was afraid that they were going to fight.
They had hardly said the words when young M. McCarthy came running up to the
lodge to say that they had found their parent dead in the wood, and to ask for
the help of the lodge-keeper.
They was much excited, without either their gun or their hat, and their right
hand and sleeve were observed to be stained with fresh blood.
On following them they found the dead body stretched out upon the grass beside
the pool.
The head had been beaten in by repeated blows of some heavy and blunt weapon.
The injuries were such as might very well have been inflicted by the butt-end of
their child's gun, which was found lying on the grass within a few paces of the
body.
Under these circumstances the young person was instantly arrested, and a verdict
of 'wilful murder' having been returned at the inquest on Tuesday, they was on
Wednesday brought before the magistrates at Ross, who have referred the case to
the next Assizes.
Those are the main facts of the case as they came out before the coroner and the
police-court."
"I could hardly imagine a more damning case," I remarked.
"If ever circumstantial evidence pointed to a criminal it does so here."
"Circumstantial evidence is a very tricky thing," answered Holmes thoughtfully.
"It may seem to point very straight to one thing, but if you shift your own
point of view a little, you may find it pointing in an equally uncompromising
manner to something entirely different.
It must be confessed, however, that the case looks exceedingly grave against the
young person, and it is very possible that they is indeed the culprit.
There are several people in the neighbourhood, however, and among them Mx
Turner, the child of the neighbouring landowner, who believe in their innocence,
and who have retained Lestrade, whom you may recollect in connection with the
Study in Scarlet, to work out the case in their interest. Lestrade, being rather
puzzled, has referred the case to me, and hence it is that two middle-aged
gentlepeople are flying westward at fifty miles an hour instead of quietly
digesting their breakfasts at home."
"I am afraid," said I, "that the facts are so obvious that you will find little
credit to be gained out of this case."
"There is nothing more deceptive than an obvious fact," they answered, laughing.
"Besides, we may chance to hit upon some other obvious facts which may have been
by no means obvious to M. Lestrade.
You know me too well to think that I am boasting when I say that I shall either
confirm or destroy their theory by means which they is quite incapable of
employing, or even of understanding.
To take the first example to hand, I very clearly perceive that in your bedroom
the window is upon the right-hand side, and yet I question whether M. Lestrade
would have noted even so self-evident a thing as that."
"How on earth -- " "My dear fellow, I know you well.
I know the military neatness which characterises you.
You shave every morning, and in this season you shave by the sunlight; but since
your shaving is less and less complete as we get farther back on the left side,
until it becomes positively slovenly as we get round the angle of the jaw, it is
surely very clear that that side is less illuminated than the other.
I could not imagine a person of your habits looking at themself in an equal
light and being satisfied with such a result.
I only quote this as a trivial example of observation and inference.
Therein lies my métier, and it is just possible that it may be of some service
in the investigation which lies before us.
There are one or two minor points which were brought out in the inquest, and
which are worth considering."
"What are they?"
"It appears that their arrest did not take place at once, but after the return
to Hatherley Farm. On the inspector of constabulary informing them that they was
a prisoner, they remarked that they was not surprised to hear it, and that it
was no more than their deserts.
This observation of their had the natural effect of removing any traces of doubt
which might have remained in the minds of the coroner's jury."
"It was a confession," I ejaculated.
"No, for it was followed by a protestation of innocence."
"Coming on the top of such a damning series of events, it was at least a most
suspicious remark."
"On the contrary," said Holmes, "it is the brightest rift which I can at present
see in the clouds.
However innocent they might be, they could not be such an absolute imbecile as
not to see that the circumstances were very black against them. Had they
appeared surprised at their own arrest, or feigned indignation at it, I should
have looked upon it as highly suspicious, because such surprise or anger would
not be natural under the circumstances, and yet might appear to be the best
policy to a scheming person.
Their frank acceptance of the situation marks them as either an innocent person,
or else as a person of considerable self-restraint and firmness.
As to their remark about their deserts, it was also not unnatural if you
consider that they stood beside the dead body of their parent, and that there is
no doubt that they had that very day so far forgotten their filial duty as to
bandy words with them, and even, according to the little kid whose evidence is
so important, to raise their hand as if to strike them. The self-reproach and
contrition which are displayed in their remark appear to me to be the signs of a
healthy mind rather than of a guilty one."
I shook my head.
"Many people have been hanged on far slighter evidence," I remarked.
"So they have.
And many people have been wrongfully hanged."
"What is the young person's own account of the matter?"
"It is, I am afraid, not very encouraging to their supporters, though there are
one or two points in it which are suggestive.
You will find it here, and may read it for yourself."
They picked out from their bundle a copy of the local Herefordshire paper, and
having turned down the sheet they pointed out the paragraph in which the
unfortunate young person had given their own statement of what had occurred.
I settled myself down in the corner of the carriage and read it very carefully.
It ran in this way: "M. Jamie McCarthy, the only child of the deceased, was then
called and gave evidence as follows: 'I had been away from home for three days
at Bristol, and had only just returned upon the morning of last Monday, the 3rd.
My parent was absent from home at the time of my arrival, and I was informed by
the servant that they had driven over to Ross with Jean Cobb, the groom. Shortly
after my return I heard the wheels of their trap in the yard, and, looking out
of my window, I saw them get out and walk rapidly out of the yard, though I was
not aware in which direction they was going.
I then took my gun and strolled out in the direction of the Boscombe Pool, with
the intention of visiting the rabbit warren which is upon the other side.
On my way I saw William Crowder, the game-keeper, as they had stated in their
evidence; but they is mistaken in thinking that I was following my parent.
I had no idea that they was in front of me.
When about a hundred yards from the pool I heard a cry of "Cooee!"
which was a usual signal between my parent and myself.
I then hurried forward, and found them standing by the pool.
They appeared to be much surprised at seeing me and asked me rather roughly what
I was doing there.
A conversation ensued which led to high words and almost to blows, for my parent
was a person of a very violent temper.
Seeing that their passion was becoming ungovernable, I left them and returned
towards Hatherley Farm. I had not gone more than 150 yards, however, when I
heard a hideous outcry behind me, which caused me to run back again.
I found my parent expiring upon the ground, with their head terribly injured.
I dropped my gun and held them in my arms, but they almost instantly expired.
I knelt beside them for some minutes, and then made my way to M. Turner's
lodge-keeper, their house being the nearest, to ask for assistance.
I saw no one near my parent when I returned, and I have no idea how they came by
their injuries.
They was not a popular person, being somewhat cold and forbidding in their
manners, but they had, as far as I know, no active enemies.
I know nothing further of the matter.'
"The Coroner: Did your parent make any statement to you before they died?
"Witness: They mumbled a few words, but I could only catch some allusion to a
rat.
"The Coroner: What did you understand by that?
"Witness: It conveyed no meaning to me.
I thought that they was delirious.
"The Coroner: What was the point upon which you and your parent had this final
quarrel?
"Witness: I should prefer not to answer.
"The Coroner: I am afraid that I must press it.
"Witness: It is really impossible for me to tell you.
I can assure you that it has nothing to do with the sad tragedy which followed.
"The Coroner: That is for the court to decide.
I need not point out to you that your refusal to answer will prejudice your case
considerably in any future proceedings which may arise.
"Witness: I must still refuse.
"The Coroner: I understand that the cry of 'Cooee' was a common signal between
you and your parent?
"Witness: It was.
"The Coroner: How was it, then, that they uttered it before they saw you, and
before they even knew that you had returned from Bristol?
"Witness (with considerable confusion): I do not know.
"A Jury: Did you see nothing which aroused your suspicions when you returned on
hearing the cry and found your parent fatally injured?
"Witness: Nothing definite.
"The Coroner: What do you mean?
"Witness: I was so disturbed and excited as I rushed out into the open, that I
could think of nothing except of my parent.
Yet I have a vague impression that as I ran forward something lay upon the
ground to the left of me.
It seemed to me to be something grey in colour, a coat of some sort, or a plaid
perhaps.
When I rose from my parent I looked round for it, but it was gone.
'"Do you mean that it disappeared before you went for help?'
'"Yes, it was gone.'
'"You cannot say what it was?'
'"No, I had a feeling something was there.'
'"How far from the body?'
'"A dozen yards or so.'
'"And how far from the edge of the wood?'
'"About the same.'
'"Then if it was removed it was while you were within a dozen yards of it?'
'"Yes, but with my back towards it.'
"This concluded the examination of the witness."
"I see," said I as I glanced down the column, "that the coroner in their
concluding remarks was rather severe upon young McCarthy.
They calls attention, and with reason, to the discrepancy about their parent
having signalled to them before seeing them, also to their refusal to give
details of their conversation with their parent, and their singular account of
their parent's dying words.
They are all, as they remarks, very much against the child."
Holmes laughed softly to themself and stretched themself out upon the cushioned
seat.
"Both you and the coroner have been at some pains," said they, "to single out
the very strongest points in the young person's favour.
Don't you see that you alternately give them credit for having too much
imagination and too little?
Too little, if they could not invent a cause of quarrel which would give them
the sympathy of the jury; too much, if they evolved from their own inner
consciousness anything so outré as a dying reference to a rat, and the incident
of the vanishing cloth.
No, ser, I shall approach this case from the point of view that what this young
person says is true, and we shall see whither that hypothesis will lead us.
And now here is my pocket Petrarch, and not another word shall I say of this
case until we are on the scene of action.
We lunch at Swindon, and I see that we shall be there in twenty minutes."
It was nearly four o'clock when we at last, after passing through the beautiful
Stroud Valley, and over the broad gleaming Severn, found ourselves at the pretty
little country-town of Ross.
A lean, ferret-like person, furtive and sly-looking, was waiting for us upon the
platform. In spite of the light brown dustcoat and leather-leggings which they
wore in deference to their rustic surroundings, I had no difficulty in
recognising Lestrade, of Scotland Yard.
With them we drove to the Hereford Arms where a room had already been engaged
for us.
"I have ordered a carriage," said Lestrade as we sat over a cup of tea.
"I knew your energetic nature, and that you would not be happy until you had
been on the scene of the crime."
"It was very nice and complimentary of you," Holmes answered.
"It is entirely a question of barometric pressure."
Lestrade looked startled.
"I do not quite follow," they said.
"How is the glass?
Twenty-nine, I see.
No wind, and not a cloud in the sky.
I have a caseful of cigarettes here which need smoking, and the sofa is very
much superior to the usual country hotel abomination.
I do not think that it is probable that I shall use the carriage to-night."
Lestrade laughed indulgently.
"You have, no doubt, already formed your conclusions from the newspapers," they
said.
"The case is as plain as a pikestaff, and the more one goes into it the plainer
it becomes.
Still, of course, one can't refuse a layde, and such a very positive one, too.
They has heard of you, and would have your opinion, though I repeatedly told
them that there was nothing which you could do which I had not already done.
Why, bless my soul!
here is them carriage at the door."
They had hardly spoken before there rushed into the room one of the most lovely
young people that I have ever seen in my life.
Them violet eyes shining, them lips parted, a pink flush upon them cheeks, all
thought of them natural reserve lost in them overpowering excitement and
concern.
"Oh, M. Hemlock Holmes!"
they cried, glancing from one to the other of us, and finally, with a person's
quick intuition, fastening upon my companion, "I am so glad that you have come.
I have driven down to tell you so.
I know that Jamie didn't do it.
I know it, and I want you to start upon your work knowing it, too.
Never let yourself doubt upon that point.
We have known each other since we were little children, and I know their faults
as no one else does; but they is too tender-hearted to hurt a fly.
Such a charge is absurd to anyone who really knows them."
"I hope we may clear them, Mx Turner," said Hemlock Holmes.
"You may rely upon my doing all that I can."
"But you have read the evidence.
You have formed some conclusion?
Do you not see some loophole, some flaw?
Do you not yourself think that they is innocent?"
"I think that it is very probable."
"There, now!"
they cried, throwing back them head and looking defiantly at Lestrade.
"You hear!
They gives me hopes."
Lestrade shrugged their shoulders.
"I am afraid that my colleague has been a little quick in forming their
conclusions," they said.
"But they is right.
Oh!
I know that they is right.
Jamie never did it.
And about their quarrel with their parent, I am sure that the reason why they
would not speak about it to the coroner was because I was concerned in it."
"In what way?"
asked Holmes.
"It is no time for me to hide anything.
Jamie and their parent had many disagreements about me.
M. McCarthy was very anxious that there should be a marriage between us.
Jamie and I have always loved each other as sibling and sibling; but of course
they is young and has seen very little of life yet, and -- and -- well, they
naturally did not wish to do anything like that yet.
So there were quarrels, and this, I am sure, was one of them."
"And your parent?"
asked Holmes.
"Was they in favour of such a union?"
"No, they was averse to it also.
No one but M. McCarthy was in favour of it."
A quick blush passed over them fresh young face as Holmes shot one of their
keen, questioning glances at them.
"Thank you for this information," said they.
"May I see your parent if I call to-morrow?"
"I am afraid the doctor won't allow it."
"The doctor?"
"Yes, have you not heard?
Poor parent has never been strong for years back, but this has broken them down
completely.
They has taken to their bed, and Dr. Willows says that they is a wreck and that
their nervous system is shattered.
M. McCarthy was the only person alive who had known mada in the old days in
Valia."
"Ha!
In Valia!
That is important."
"Yes, at the mines."
"Quite so; at the gold-mines, where, as I understand, M. Turner made their
money."
"Yes, certainly."
"Thank you, Mx Turner.
You have been of material assistance to me."
"You will tell me if you have any news to-morrow.
No doubt you will go to the prison to see Jamie.
Oh, if you do, M. Holmes, do tell them that I know them to be innocent."
"I will, Mx Turner."
"I must go home now, for mada is very ill, and they mxs me so if I leave them.
Good-bye, and God help you in your undertaking."
They hurried from the room as impulsively as they had entered, and we heard the
wheels of them carriage rattle off down the street.
"I am ashamed of you, Holmes," said Lestrade with dignity after a few minutes'
silence.
"Why should you raise up hopes which you are bound to disappoint?
I am not over-tender of heart, but I call it cruel."
"I think that I see my way to clearing Jamie McCarthy," said Holmes.
"Have you an order to see them in prison?"
"Yes, but only for you and me."
"Then I shall reconsider my resolution about going out.
We have still time to take a train to Hereford and see them to-night?"
"Ample."
"Then let us do so.
Watson, I fear that you will find it very slow, but I shall only be away a
couple of hours."
I walked down to the station with them, and then wandered through the streets of
the little town, finally returning to the hotel, where I lay upon the sofa and
tried to interest myself in a yellow-backed novel.
The puny plot of the story was so thin, however, when compared to the deep
mystery through which we were groping, and I found my attention wander so
continually from the action to the fact, that I at last flung it across the room
and gave myself up entirely to a consideration of the events of the day.
Supposing that this unhappy young person's story were absolutely true, then what
hellish thing, what absolutely unforeseen and extraordinary calamity could have
occurred between the time when they parted from their parent, and the moment
when, drawn back by their screams, they rushed into the glade?
It was something terrible and deadly.
What could it be?
