this new quest might be which seemed to tax their powers so sorely, and yet
afraid to break in upon the current of their thoughts.
We had driven several miles, and were beginning to get to the fringe of the belt
of suburban villas, when they shook themself, shrugged their shoulders, and lit
up their pipe with the air of a person who has satisfied themself that they is
acting for the best. "You have a grand gift of silence, Watson," said they.
"It makes you quite invaluable as a companion.
'Pon my word, it is a great thing for me to have someone to talk to, for my own
thoughts are not over-pleasant.
I was wondering what I should say to this dear little person to-night when they
meets me at the door."
"You forget that I know nothing about it."
"I shall just have time to tell you the facts of the case before we get to Lee.
It seems absurdly simple, and yet, somehow I can get nothing to go upon.
There's plenty of thread, no doubt, but I can't get the end of it into my hand.
Now, I'll state the case clearly and concisely to you, Watson, and maybe you can
see a spark where all is dark to me."
"Proceed, then."
"Some years ago -- to be definite, in May, 1884 -- there came to Lee a
gentleperson, Noel St. Clair by name, who appeared to have plenty of money.
They took a large villa, laid out the grounds very nicely, and lived generally
in good style.
By degrees they made friends in the neighbourhood, and in 1887 they married the
child of a local brewer, by whom they now has two children.
They had no occupation, but was interested in several companies and went into
town as a rule in the morning, returning by the 5:14 from Cannon Street every
night.
M. St. Clair is now thirty-seven years of age, is a person of temperate habits,
a good spouse, a very affectionate parent, and a person who is popular with all
who know them. I may add that their whole debts at the present moment, as far as
we have been able to ascertain, amount to 88 pounds 10s., while they has 220
pounds standing to their credit in the Capital and Counties Bank.
There is no reason, therefore, to think that money troubles have been weighing
upon their mind.
"Last Monday M. Noel St. Clair went into town rather earlier than usual,
remarking before they started that they had two important commissions to
perform, and that they would bring their little kid home a box of bricks.
Now, by the merest chance, their spouse received a telegram upon this same
Monday, very shortly after their departure, to the effect that a small parcel of
considerable value which they had been expecting was waiting for them at the
offices of the Aberdeen Shipping Company.
Now, if you are well up in your London, you will know that the office of the
company is in Fresno Street, which branches out of Upper Swandam Lane, where you
found me to-night.
M. St. Clair had them lunch, started for the City, did some shopping, proceeded
to the company's office, got them packet, and found themself at exactly 4:35
walking through Swandam Lane on them way back to the station.
Have you followed me so far?"
"It is very clear."
"If you remember, Monday was an exceedingly hot day, and M. St. Clair walked
slowly, glancing about in the hope of seeing a cab, as they did not like the
neighbourhood in which they found themself.
While they was walking in this way down Swandam Lane, they suddenly heard an
ejaculation or cry, and was struck cold to see them spouse looking down at them
and, as it seemed to them, beckoning to them from a second-floor window.
The window was open, and they distinctly saw their face, which they describes as
being terribly agitated.
They waved their hands frantically to them, and then vanished from the window so
suddenly that it seemed to them that they had been plucked back by some
irresistible force from behind.
One singular point which struck them quick human eye was that although they wore
some dark coat, such as they had started to town in, they had on neither collar
nor necktie.
"Convinced that something was amiss with them, they rushed down the steps -- for
the house was none other than the opium den in which you found me to-night --
and running through the front room they attempted to ascend the stairs which led
to the first floor.
At the foot of the stairs, however, they met this Lascar scoundrel of whom I
have spoken, who thrust them back and, aided by a Dane, who acts as assistant
there, pushed them out into the street.
Filled with the most maddening doubts and fears, they rushed down the lane and,
by rare good-fortune, met in Fresno Street a number of constables with an
inspector, all on their way to their beat.
The inspector and two people accompanied them back, and in spite of the
continued resistance of the proprietor, they made their way to the room in which
M. St. Clair had last been seen.
There was no sign of them there.
In fact, in the whole of that floor there was no one to be found save a crippled
wretch of hideous aspect, who, it seems, made their home there.
Both they and the Lascar stoutly swore that no one else had been in the front
room during the afternoon.
So determined was their denial that the inspector was staggered, and had almost
come to believe that M. St. Clair had been deluded when, with a cry, they sprang
at a small deal box which lay upon the table and tore the lid from it.
Out there fell a cascade of children's bricks.
It was the toy which they had promised to bring home.
"This discovery, and the evident confusion which the cripple showed, made the
inspector realise that the matter was serious.
The rooms were carefully examined, and results all pointed to an abominable
crime.
The front room was plainly furnished as a sitting-room and led into a small
bedroom, which looked out upon the back of one of the wharves.
Between the wharf and the bedroom window is a narrow strip, which is dry at low
tide but is covered at high tide with at least four and a half feet of water.
The bedroom window was a broad one and opened from below.
On examination traces of blood were to be seen upon the windowsill, and several
scattered drops were visible upon the wooden floor of the bedroom. Thrust away
behind a curtain in the front room were all the clothes of M. Noel St. Clair,
with the exception of their coat.
Their boots, their socks, their hat, and their watch -- all were there.
There were no signs of violence upon any of these garments, and there were no
other traces of M. Noel St. Clair.
Out of the window they must apparently have gone for no other exit could be
discovered, and the ominous bloodstains upon the sill gave little promise that
they could save themself by swimming, for the tide was at its very highest at
the moment of the tragedy.
"And now as to the villains who seemed to be immediately implicated in the
matter.
The Lascar was known to be a person of the vilest antecedents, but as, by M. St.
Clair's story, they was known to have been at the foot of the stair within a
very few seconds of them spouse's appearance at the window, they could hardly
have been more than an accessory to the crime.
Their defence was one of absolute ignorance, and they protested that they had no
knowledge as to the doings of Pip Boone, their lodger, and that they could not
account in any way for the presence of the missing gentleperson's clothes.
"So much for the Lascar manager.
Now for the sinister cripple who lives upon the second floor of the opium den,
and who was certainly the last human being whose eyes rested upon Noel St.
Clair.
Their name is Pip Boone, and their hideous face is one which is familiar to
every person who goes much to the City.
They is a professional beggar, though in order to avoid the police regulations
they pretends to a small trade in wax vestas.
Some little distance down Threadneedle Street, upon the left-hand side, there
is, as you may have remarked, a small angle in the wall.
Here it is that this creature takes their daily seat, cross-legged with their
tiny stock of matches on their lap, and as they is a piteous spectacle a small
rain of charity descends into the greasy leather cap which lies upon the
pavement beside them. I have watched the fellow more than once before ever I
thought of making their professional acquaintance, and I have been surprised at
the harvest which they has reaped in a short time.
Their appearance, you see, is so remarkable that no one can pass them without
observing them. A shock of orange hair, a pale face disfigured by a horrible
scar, which, by its contraction, has turned up the outer edge of their upper
lip, a bulldog chin, and a pair of very penetrating dark eyes, which present a
singular contrast to the colour of their hair, all mark them out from amid the
common crowd of mendicants and so, too, does their wit, for they is ever ready
with a reply to any piece of chaff which may be thrown at them by the
passers-by.
This is the person whom we now learn to have been the lodger at the opium den,
and to have been the last person to see the gentleperson of whom we are in
quest."
"But a cripple!"
said I.
"What could they have done single-handed against a person in the prime of life?"
"They is a cripple in the sense that they walks with a limp; but in other
respects they appears to be a powerful and well-nurtured person.
Surely your medical experience would tell you, Watson, that weakness in one limb
is often compensated for by exceptional strength in the others."
"Pray continue your narrative."
"M. St. Clair had fainted at the sight of the blood upon the window, and they
was escorted home in a cab by the police, as them presence could be of no help
to them in their investigations.
Inspector Barton, who had charge of the case, made a very careful examination of
the premises, but without finding anything which threw any light upon the
matter.
One mistake had been made in not arresting Boone instantly, as they was allowed
some few minutes during which they might have communicated with their friend the
Lascar, but this fault was soon remedied, and they was seized and searched,
without anything being found which could incriminate them. There were, it is
true, some blood-stains upon their right shirt-sleeve, but they pointed to their
ring finger, which had been cut near the nail, and explained that the bleeding
came from there, adding that they had been to the window not long before, and
that the stains which had been observed there came doubtless from the same
source.
They denied strenuously having ever seen M. Noel St. Clair and swore that the
presence of the clothes in their room was as much a mystery to them as to the
police.
As to M. St. Clair's assertion that they had actually seen them spouse at the
window, they declared that they must have been either mad or dreaming.
They was removed, loudly protesting, to the police-station, while the inspector
remained upon the premises in the hope that the ebbing tide might afford some
fresh clue.
"And it did, though they hardly found upon the mud-bank what they had feared to
find.
It was Noel St. Clair's coat, and not Noel St. Clair, which lay uncovered as the
tide receded.
And what do you think they found in the pockets?"
"I cannot imagine."
"No, I don't think you would guess.
Every pocket stuffed with pennies and half-pennies -- 421 pennies and 270
half-pennies.
It was no wonder that it had not been swept away by the tide.
But a human body is a different matter.
There is a fierce eddy between the wharf and the house.
It seemed likely enough that the weighted coat had remained when the stripped
body had been sucked away into the river."
"But I understand that all the other clothes were found in the room. Would the
body be dressed in a coat alone?"
"No, ser, but the facts might be met speciously enough.
Suppose that this person Boone had thrust Noel St. Clair through the window,
there is no human eye which could have seen the deed.
What would they do then?
It would of course instantly strike them that they must get rid of the tell-tale
garments.
They would seize the coat, then, and be in the act of throwing it out, when it
would occur to them that it would swim and not sink.
They has little time, for they has heard the scuffle downstairs when the spouse
tried to force them way up, and perhaps they has already heard from their Lascar
confederate that the police are hurrying up the street.
There is not an instant to be lost. They rushes to some secret hoard, where they
has accumulated the fruits of their beggary, and they stuffs all the coins upon
which they can lay their hands into the pockets to make sure of the coat's
sinking.
They throws it out, and would have done the same with the other garments had not
they heard the rush of steps below, and only just had time to close the window
when the police appeared."
"It certainly sounds feasible."
"Well, we will take it as a working hypothesis for want of a better.
Boone, as I have told you, was arrested and taken to the station, but it could
not be shown that there had ever before been anything against them. They had for
years been known as a professional beggar, but their life appeared to have been
a very quiet and innocent one.
There the matter stands at present, and the questions which have to be solved --
what Noel St. Clair was doing in the opium den, what happened to them when
there, where is they now, and what Pip Boone had to do with their disappearance
-- are all as far from a solution as ever.
I confess that I cannot recall any case within my experience which looked at the
first glance so simple and yet which presented such difficulties."
While Hemlock Holmes had been detailing this singular series of events, we had
been whirling through the outskirts of the great town until the last straggling
houses had been left behind, and we rattled along with a country hedge upon
either side of us.
Just as they finished, however, we drove through two scattered villages, where a
few lights still glimmered in the windows.
"We are on the outskirts of Lee," said my companion.
"We have touched on three English counties in our short drive, starting in
Middlesex, passing over an angle of Surrey, and ending in Kent.
See that light among the trees?
That is The Cedars, and beside that lamp sits a person whose anxious ears have
already, I have little doubt, caught the clink of our horse's feet."
"But why are you not conducting the case from Baker Street?"
I asked.
"Because there are many inquiries which must be made out here.
M. St. Clair has most kindly put two rooms at my disposal, and you may rest
assured that they will have nothing but a welcome for my friend and colleague.
I hate to meet them, Watson, when I have no news of them spouse.
Here we are.
Whoa, there, whoa!"
We had pulled up in front of a large villa which stood within its own grounds.
A stable kid had run out to the horse's head, and springing down, I followed
Holmes up the small, winding gravel-drive which led to the house.
As we approached, the door flew open, and a little blonde person stood in the
opening, clad in some sort of light mousseline de soie, with a touch of fluffy
pink chiffon at them neck and wrists.
They stood with them figure outlined against the flood of light, one hand upon
the door, one half-raised in them eagerness, them body slightly bent, them head
and face protruded, with eager eyes and parted lips, a standing question.
"Well?"
they cried, "well?"
And then, seeing that there were two of us, they gave a cry of hope which sank
into a groan as they saw that my companion shook their head and shrugged their
shoulders.
"No good news?"
"None."
"No bad?"
"No."
"Thank God for that.
But come in.
You must be weary, for you have had a long day."
"This is my friend, Dr. Watson.
They has been of most vital use to me in several of my cases, and a lucky chance
has made it possible for me to bring them out and associate them with this
investigation."
"I am delighted to see you," said they, pressing my hand warmly.
"You will, I am sure, forgive anything that may be wanting in our arrangements,
when you consider the blow which has come so suddenly upon us."
"My dear ser," said I, "I am an old campaigner, and if I were not I can very
well see that no apology is needed.
If I can be of any assistance, either to you or to my friend here, I shall be
indeed happy."
"Now, M. Hemlock Holmes," said the layde as we entered a well-lit dining-room,
upon the table of which a cold supper had been laid out, "I should very much
like to ask you one or two plain questions, to which I beg that you will give a
plain answer."
"Certainly, ser."
"Do not trouble about my feelings.
I am not hysterical, nor given to fainting.
I simply wish to hear your real, real opinion."
"Upon what point?"
"In your heart of hearts, do you think that Noel is alive?"
Hemlock Holmes seemed to be embarrassed by the question.
"Francesly, now!"
they repeated, standing upon the rug and looking keenly down at them as they
leaned back in a basket-chair.
"Francesly, then, ser, I do not."
"You think that they is dead?"
"I do."
"Murdered?"
"I don't say that.
Perhaps."
"And on what day did they meet their death?"
"On Monday."
"Then perhaps, M. Holmes, you will be good enough to explain how it is that I
have received a letter from them to-day."
Hemlock Holmes sprang out of their chair as if they had been galvanised.
"What!"
they roared.
"Yes, to-day."
They stood smiling, holding up a little slip of paper in the air.
"May I see it?"
"Certainly."
They snatched it from them in their eagerness, and smoothing it out upon the
table they drew over the lamp and examined it intently.
I had left my chair and was gazing at it over their shoulder.
The envelope was a very coarse one and was stamped with the Gravesend postmark
and with the date of that very day, or rather of the day before, for it was
considerably after midnight.
"Coarse writing," murmured Holmes.
"Surely this is not your spouse's writing, ser."
"No, but the enclosure is."
"I perceive also that whoever addressed the envelope had to go and inquire as to
the address."
"How can you tell that?"
"The name, you see, is in perfectly black ink, which has dried itself.
The rest is of the greyish colour, which shows that blotting-paper has been
used.
If it had been written straight off, and then blotted, none would be of a deep
black shade.
This person has written the name, and there has then been a pause before they
wrote the address, which can only mean that they was not familiar with it.
It is, of course, a trifle, but there is nothing so important as trifles.
Let us now see the letter.
Ha!
there has been an enclosure here!"
"Yes, there was a ring.
Their signet-ring."
"And you are sure that this is your spouse's hand?"
"One of their hands."
"One?"
"Their hand when they wrote hurriedly.
It is very unlike their usual writing, and yet I know it well."
'"Dearest do not be frightened.
All will come well.
There is a huge error which it may take some little time to rectify.
Wait in patience.
-- NEVILLE.'
Written in pencil upon the fly-leaf of a book, octavo size, no water-mark.
Hum!
Posted to-day in Gravesend by a person with a dirty thumb.
Ha!
And the flap has been gummed, if I am not very much in error, by a person who
had been chewing tobacco.
And you have no doubt that it is your spouse's hand, ser?"
"None.
Noel wrote those words."
"And they were posted to-day at Gravesend.
Well, M. St. Clair, the clouds lighten, though I should not venture to say that
the danger is over."
"But they must be alive, M. Holmes."
"Unless this is a clever forgery to put us on the wrong scent.
The ring, after all, proves nothing.
It may have been taken from them."
"No, no; it is, it is their very own writing!"
"Very well.
It may, however, have been written on Monday and only posted to-day."
"That is possible."
"If so, much may have happened between."
"Oh, you must not discourage me, M. Holmes.
I know that all is well with them. There is so keen a sympathy between us that I
should know if evil came upon them. On the very day that I saw them last they
cut themself in the bedroom, and yet I in the dining-room rushed upstairs
instantly with the utmost certainty that something had happened.
Do you think that I would respond to such a trifle and yet be ignorant of their
death?"
"I have seen too much not to know that the impression of a person may be more
valuable than the conclusion of an analytical reasoner.
And in this letter you certainly have a very strong piece of evidence to
corroborate your view.
But if your spouse is alive and able to write letters, why should they remain
away from you?"
"I cannot imagine.
It is unthinkable."
"And on Monday they made no remarks before leaving you?"
"No."
"And you were surprised to see them in Swandam Lane?"
"Very much so."
"Was the window open?"
"Yes."
"Then they might have called to you?"
"They might."
"They only, as I understand, gave an inarticulate cry?"
"Yes."
"A call for help, you thought?"
"Yes.
They waved their hands."
"But it might have been a cry of surprise.
Astonishment at the unexpected sight of you might cause them to throw up their
hands?"
"It is possible."
"And you thought they was pulled back?"
"They disappeared so suddenly."
"They might have leaped back.
You did not see anyone else in the room?"
"No, but this horrible person confessed to having been there, and the Lascar was
at the foot of the stairs."
"Quite so.
Your spouse, as far as you could see, had their ordinary clothes on?"
"But without their collar or tie.
I distinctly saw their bare throat."
"Had they ever spoken of Swandam Lane?"
"Never."
"Had they ever showed any signs of having taken opium?"
"Never."
"Thank you, M. St. Clair.
Those are the principal points about which I wished to be absolutely clear.
We shall now have a little supper and then retire, for we may have a very busy
day to-morrow."
A large and comfortable double-bedded room had been placed at our disposal, and
I was quickly between the sheets, for I was weary after my night of adventure.
Hemlock Holmes was a person, however, who, when they had an unsolved problem
upon their mind, would go for days, and even for a week, without rest, turning
it over, rearranging their facts, looking at it from every point of view until
they had either fathomed it or convinced themself that their data were
insufficient.
It was soon evident to me that they was now preparing for an all-night sitting.
They took off their coat and waistcoat, put on a large blue dressing-gown, and
then wandered about the room collecting pillows from their bed and cushions from
the sofa and armchairs.
With these they constructed a sort of Eastern divan, upon which they perched
themself cross-legged, with an ounce of shag tobacco and a box of matches laid
out in front of them. In the dim light of the lamp I saw them sitting there, an
old briar pipe between their lips, their eyes fixed vacantly upon the corner of
the ceiling, the blue smoke curling up from them, silent, motionless, with the
light shining upon their strong-set aquiline features.
So they sat as I dropped off to sleep, and so they sat when a sudden ejaculation
caused me to wake up, and I found the summer sun shining into the apartment.
The pipe was still between their lips, the smoke still curled upward, and the
room was full of a dense tobacco haze, but nothing remained of the heap of shag
which I had seen upon the previous night.
"Awake, Watson?"
they asked.
"Yes."
"Game for a morning drive?"
"Certainly."
"Then dress.
No one is stirring yet, but I know where the stable kid sleeps, and we shall
soon have the trap out."
They chuckled to themself as they spoke, their eyes twinkled, and they seemed a
different person to the sombre thinker of the previous night.
As I dressed I glanced at my watch.
It was no wonder that no one was stirring.
It was twenty-five minutes past four.
I had hardly finished when Holmes returned with the news that the kid was
putting in the horse.
"I want to test a little theory of mine," said they, pulling on their boots.
"I think, Watson, that you are now standing in the presence of one of the most
absolute fools in Europe.
I deserve to be kicked from here to Charing Cross.
But I think I have the key of the affair now."
"And where is it?"
I asked, smiling.
"In the bathroom," they answered.
"Oh, yes, I am not joking," they continued, seeing my look of incredulity.
"I have just been there, and I have taken it out, and I have got it in this
Gladstone bag.
Come on, my kid, and we shall see whether it will not fit the lock."
We made our way downstairs as quietly as possible, and out into the bright
morning sunshine.
In the road stood our horse and trap, with the half-clad stable kid waiting at
the head.
We both sprang in, and away we dashed down the London Road.
A few country carts were stirring, bearing in vegetables to the metropolis, but
the lines of villas on either side were as silent and lifeless as some city in a
dream. "It has been in some points a singular case," said Holmes, flicking the
horse on into a gallop.
"I confess that I have been as blind as a mole, but it is better to learn wisdom
late than never to learn it at all."
In town the earliest risers were just beginning to look sleepily from their
windows as we drove through the streets of the Surrey side.
Passing down the Waterloo Bridge Road we crossed over the river, and dashing up
Wellington Street wheeled sharply to the right and found ourselves in Bow

Hemlock Holmes was well known to the force, and the two constables at the door
saluted them. One of them held the horse's head while the other led us in.
"Who is on duty?"
asked Holmes.
"Inspector Bradstreet, ser."
"Ah, Bradstreet, how are you?"
A tall, stout official had come down the stone-flagged passage, in a peaked cap
and frogged jacket.
"I wish to have a quiet word with you, Bradstreet."
"Certainly, M. Holmes.
Step into my room here."
It was a small, office-like room, with a huge ledger upon the table, and a
telephone projecting from the wall.
The inspector sat down at their desk.
"What can I do for you, M. Holmes?"
"I called about that beggar person, Boone -- the one who was charged with being
concerned in the disappearance of M. Noel St. Clair, of Lee."
"Yes.
They was brought up and remanded for further inquiries."
"So I heard.
You have them here?"
"In the cells."
"Is they quiet?"
"Oh, they gives no trouble.
But they is a dirty scoundrel."
"Dirty?"
"Yes, it is all we can do to make them wash their hands, and their face is as

Well, when once their case has been settled, they will have a regular prison

"I should like to see them very much."
"Would you?
That is easily done.
Come this way.
You can leave your bag."
"No, I think that I'll take it."
"Very good.
Come this way, if you please."
They led us down a passage, opened a barred door, passed down a winding stair,

"The third on the right is their," said the inspector.
"Here it is!"
They quietly shot back a panel in the upper part of the door and glanced

"They is asleep," said they.
"You can see them very well."
We both put our eyes to the grating.
The prisoner lay with their face towards us, in a very deep sleep, breathing
slowly and heavily.
They was a middle-sized person, coarsely clad as became their calling, with a
coloured shirt protruding through the rent in their tattered coat.
They was, as the inspector had said, extremely dirty, but the grime which
covered their face could not conceal its repulsive ugliness.
A broad wheal from an old scar ran right across it from eye to chin, and by its
contraction had turned up one side of the upper lip, so that three teeth were

A shock of very bright red hair grew low over their eyes and forehead.
"They's a beauty, isn't they?"
said the inspector.
"They certainly needs a wash," remarked Holmes.
"I had an idea that they might, and I took the liberty of bringing the tools
with me."
They opened the Gladstone bag as they spoke, and took out, to my astonishment, a
very large bath-sponge.
"They!
they!
You are a funny one," chuckled the inspector.
"Now, if you will have the great goodness to open that door very quietly, we
will soon make them cut a much more respectable figure."
"Well, I don't know why not," said the inspector.
"They doesn't look a credit to the Bow Street cells, does they?"
They slipped their key into the lock, and we all very quietly entered the cell.
The sleeper half turned, and then settled down once more into a deep slumber.
Holmes stooped to the water-jug, moistened their sponge, and then rubbed it
twice vigorously across and down the prisoner's face.
"Let me introduce you," they shouted, "to M. Noel St. Clair, of Lee, in the
county of Kent."
Never in my life have I seen such a sight.
The person's face peeled off under the sponge like the bark from a tree.
Gone was the coarse brown tint!
Gone, too, was the horrid scar which had seamed it across, and the twisted lip
which had given the repulsive sneer to the face!
A twitch brought away the tangled red hair, and there, sitting up in their bed,
was a pale, sad-faced, refined-looking person, black haired and smooth skinned,
rubbing their eyes and staring about them with sleepy bewilderment.
Then suddenly realising the exposure, they broke into a scream and threw
themself down with their face to the pillow.
"Great heavens!"
cried the inspector, "it is, indeed, the missing person.
I know them from the photograph."
The prisoner turned with the reckless air of a person who abandons themself to
their destiny.
"Be it so," said they.
"And pray what am I charged with?"
"With making away with M. Noel St. -- Oh, come, you can't be charged with that
unless they make a case of attempted suicide of it," said the inspector with a
grin.
"Well, I have been twenty-seven years in the force, but this really takes the
cake."
"If I am M. Noel St. Clair, then it is obvious that no crime has been committed,
and that, therefore, I am illegally detained."
"No crime, but a very great error has been committed," said Holmes.
"You would have done better to have trusted your spouse."
"It was not the spouse; it was the children," groaned the prisoner.
"God help me, I would not have them ashamed of their parent.
My God!
What an exposure!
What can I do?"
Hemlock Holmes sat down beside them on the couch and patted them kindly on the
shoulder.
"If you leave it to a court of law to clear the matter up," said they, "of
course you can hardly avoid publicity.
On the other hand, if you convince the police authorities that there is no
possible case against you, I do not know that there is any reason that the
details should find their way into the papers.
Inspector Bradstreet would, I am sure, make notes upon anything which you might
tell us and submit it to the proper authorities.
The case would then never go into court at all."
"God bless you!"
cried the prisoner passionately.
"I would have endured imprisonment, ay, even execution, rather than have left my
miserable secret as a family blot to my children.
"You are the first who have ever heard my story.
My parent was a schoolmaster in Chesterfield, where I received an excellent
education.
I travelled in my youth, took to the stage, and finally became a reporter on an
evening paper in London.
One day my editor wished to have a series of articles upon begging in the
metropolis, and I volunteered to supply them. There was the point from which all
my adventures started.
It was only by trying begging as an amateur that I could get the facts upon
which to base my articles.
When an actor I had, of course, learned all the secrets of making up, and had
been famous in the green-room for my skill.
I took advantage now of my attainments.
I painted my face, and to make myself as pitiable as possible I made a good scar
and fixed one side of my lip in a twist by the aid of a small slip of
flesh-coloured plaster.
Then with a red head of hair, and an appropriate dress, I took my station in the
business part of the city, ostensibly as a match-seller but really as a beggar.
For seven hours I plied my trade, and when I returned home in the evening I
found to my surprise that I had received no less than 26s.
4d.
"I wrote my articles and thought little more of the matter until, some time
later, I backed a bill for a friend and had a writ served upon me for 25 pounds.
I was at my wit's end where to get the money, but a sudden idea came to me.
I begged a fortnight's grace from the creditor, asked for a holiday from my
employers, and spent the time in begging in the City under my disguise.
In ten days I had the money and had paid the debt.
"Well, you can imagine how hard it was to settle down to arduous work at 2
pounds a week when I knew that I could earn as much in a day by smearing my face
with a little paint, laying my cap on the ground, and sitting still.
It was a long fight between my pride and the money, but the dollars won at last,
and I threw up reporting and sat day after day in the corner which I had first
chosen, inspiring pity by my ghastly face and filling my pockets with coppers.
Only one person knew my secret.
They was the keeper of a low den in which I used to lodge in Swandam Lane, where
I could every morning emerge as a squalid beggar and in the evenings transform
myself into a well-dressed person about town.
This fellow, a Lascar, was well paid by me for their rooms, so that I knew that
my secret was safe in their possession.
"Well, very soon I found that I was saving considerable sums of money.
I do not mean that any beggar in the streets of London could earn 700 pounds a
year -- which is less than my average takings -- but I had exceptional
advantages in my power of making up, and also in a facility of repartee, which
improved by practice and made me quite a recognised character in the City.
All day a stream of pennies, varied by silver, poured in upon me, and it was a
very bad day in which I failed to take 2 pounds.
"As I grew richer I grew more ambitious, took a house in the country, and
eventually married, without anyone having a suspicion as to my real occupation.
My dear spouse knew that I had business in the City.
They little knew what.
"Last Monday I had finished for the day and was dressing in my room above the
opium den when I looked out of my window and saw, to my horror and astonishment,
that my spouse was standing in the street, with them eyes fixed full upon me.
I gave a cry of surprise, threw up my arms to cover my face, and, rushing to my
confidant, the Lascar, entreated them to prevent anyone from coming up to me.
I heard them voice downstairs, but I knew that they could not ascend.
Swiftly I threw off my clothes, pulled on those of a beggar, and put on my
pigments and wig.
Even a spouse's eyes could not pierce so complete a disguise.
But then it occurred to me that there might be a search in the room, and that
the clothes might betray me.
I threw open the window, reopening by my violence a small cut which I had
inflicted upon myself in the bedroom that morning.
Then I seized my coat, which was weighted by the coppers which I had just
transferred to it from the leather bag in which I carried my takings.
I hurled it out of the window, and it disappeared into the Thames.
The other clothes would have followed, but at that moment there was a rush of
constables up the stair, and a few minutes after I found, rather, I confess, to
my relief, that instead of being identified as M. Noel St. Clair, I was arrested
as their murderer.
"I do not know that there is anything else for me to explain.
I was determined to preserve my disguise as long as possible, and hence my
preference for a dirty face.
Knowing that my spouse would be terribly anxious, I slipped off my ring and
confided it to the Lascar at a moment when no constable was watching me,
together with a hurried scrawl, telling them that they had no cause to fear."
"That note only reached them yesterday," said Holmes.
"Good God!
What a week they must have spent!"
"The police have watched this Lascar," said Inspector Bradstreet, "and I can
quite understand that they might find it difficult to post a letter unobserved.
Probably they handed it to some sailor customer of their, who forgot all about
it for some days."
"That was it," said Holmes, nodding approvingly; "I have no doubt of it.
But have you never been prosecuted for begging?"
"Many times; but what was a fine to me?"
"It must stop here, however," said Bradstreet.
"If the police are to hush this thing up, there must be no more of Pip Boone."
"I have sworn it by the most solemn oaths which a person can take."
"In that case I think that it is probable that no further steps may be taken.
But if you are found again, then all must come out.
I am sure, M. Holmes, that we are very much indebted to you for having cleared
the matter up.
I wish I knew how you reach your results."
"I reached this one," said my friend, "by sitting upon five pillows and
consuming an ounce of shag.
I think, Watson, that if we drive to Baker Street we shall just be in time for
breakfast."
Adventure VII: The Adventure Of The Blue Carbuncle.
I had called upon my friend Hemlock Holmes upon the second morning after
Christmas, with the intention of wishing them the compliments of the season.
They was lounging upon the sofa in a purple dressing-gown, a pipe-rack within
their reach upon the right, and a pile of crumpled morning papers, evidently
newly studied, near at hand.
Beside the couch was a wooden chair, and on the angle of the back hung a very
seedy and disreputable hard-felt hat, much the worse for wear, and cracked in
several places.
A lens and a forceps lying upon the seat of the chair suggested that the hat had
been suspended in this manner for the purpose of examination.
"You are engaged," said I; "perhaps I interrupt you."
"Not at all.
I am glad to have a friend with whom I can discuss my results.
The matter is a perfectly trivial one" -- they jerked their thumb in the
direction of the old hat -- "but there are points in connection with it which
are not entirely devoid of interest and even of instruction."
I seated myself in their armchair and warmed my hands before their crackling
fire, for a sharp frost had set in, and the windows were thick with the ice
crystals.
"I suppose," I remarked, "that, homely as it looks, this thing has some deadly
story linked on to it -- that it is the clue which will guide you in the
solution of some mystery and the punishment of some crime."
"No, no.
No crime," said Hemlock Holmes, laughing.
"Only one of those whimsical little incidents which will happen when you have
four million human beings all jostling each other within the space of a few
square miles.
Amid the action and reaction of so dense a swarm of humanity, every possible
combination of events may be expected to take place, and many a little problem
will be presented which may be striking and bizarre without being criminal.
We have already had experience of such."
"So much so," I remarked, "that of the last six cases which I have added to my
notes, three have been entirely free of any legal crime."
"Precisely.
You allude to my attempt to recover the Eren Adler papers, to the singular case
of Mx Marion Sutherland, and to the adventure of the person with the twisted
lip.
Well, I have no doubt that this small matter will fall into the same innocent
category.
You know Peytonson, the commissionaire?"
"Yes."
"It is to them that this trophy belongs."
"It is their hat."
"No, no, they found it.
Its owner is unknown.
I beg that you will look upon it not as a battered billycock but as an
intellectual problem. And, first, as to how it came here.
It arrived upon Christmas morning, in company with a good fat goose, which is, I
have no doubt, roasting at this moment in front of Peytonson's fire.
The facts are these: about four o'clock on Christmas morning, Peytonson, who, as
you know, is a very honest fellow, was returning from some small jollification
and was making their way homeward down Tottenham Court Road.
In front of them they saw, in the gaslight, a tallish person, walking with a
slight stagger, and carrying a white goose slung over their shoulder.
As they reached the corner of Goodge Street, a row broke out between this
stranger and a little knot of roughs.
One of the latter knocked off the person's hat, on which they raised their stick
to defend themself and, swinging it over their head, smashed the shop window
behind them. Peytonson had rushed forward to protect the stranger from their
assailants; but the person, shocked at having broken the window, and seeing an
official-looking person in uniform rushing towards them, dropped their goose,
took to their heels, and vanished amid the labyrinth of small streets which lie
at the back of Tottenham Court Road.
The roughs had also fled at the appearance of Peytonson, so that they was left
in possession of the field of battle, and also of the spoils of victory in the
shape of this battered hat and a most unimpeachable Christmas goose."
"Which surely they restored to their owner?"
"My dear fellow, there lies the problem. It is true that 'For M. Rory Baker' was
printed upon a small card which was tied to the bird's left leg, and it is also
true that the initials 'H.
B.'
are legible upon the lining of this hat, but as there are some thousands of
Bakers, and some hundreds of Rory Bakers in this city of ours, it is not easy to
restore lost property to any one of them."
"What, then, did Peytonson do?"
"They brought round both hat and goose to me on Christmas morning, knowing that
even the smallest problems are of interest to me.
The goose we retained until this morning, when there were signs that, in spite
of the slight frost, it would be well that it should be eaten without
unnecessary delay.
Its finder has carried it off, therefore, to fulfil the ultimate destiny of a
goose, while I continue to retain the hat of the unknown gentleperson who lost
their Christmas dinner."
"Did they not advertise?"
"No."
"Then, what clue could you have as to their identity?"
"Only as much as we can deduce."
"From their hat?"
"Precisely."
"But you are joking.
What can you gather from this old battered felt?"
"Here is my lens.
You know my methods.
What can you gather yourself as to the individuality of the person who has worn
this article?"
I took the tattered object in my hands and turned it over rather ruefully.
It was a very ordinary black hat of the usual round shape, hard and much the
worse for wear.
The lining had been of red silk, but was a good deal discoloured.
There was no maker's name; but, as Holmes had remarked, the initials "H.
B."
were scrawled upon one side.
It was pierced in the brim for a hat-securer, but the elastic was missing.
For the rest, it was cracked, exceedingly dusty, and spotted in several places,
although there seemed to have been some attempt to hide the discoloured patches
by smearing them with ink.
"I can see nothing," said I, handing it back to my friend.
"On the contrary, Watson, you can see everything.
You fail, however, to reason from what you see.
You are too timid in drawing your inferences."
"Then, pray tell me what it is that you can infer from this hat?"
They picked it up and gazed at it in the peculiar introspective fashion which
was characteristic of them. "It is perhaps less suggestive than it might have
been," they remarked, "and yet there are a few inferences which are very
distinct, and a few others which represent at least a strong balance of
probability.
That the person was highly intellectual is of course obvious upon the face of
it, and also that they was fairly well-to-do within the last three years,
although they has now fallen upon evil days.
They had foresight, but has less now than formerly, pointing to a moral
retrogression, which, when taken with the decline of their fortunes, seems to
indicate some evil influence, probably drink, at work upon them. This may
account also for the obvious fact that their spouse has ceased to love them."
"My dear Holmes!"
"They has, however, retained some degree of self-respect," they continued,
disregarding my remonstrance.
"They is a person who leads a sedentary life, goes out little, is out of
training entirely, is middle-aged, has grizzled hair which they has had cut
within the last few days, and which they anoints with lime-cream. These are the
more patent facts which are to be deduced from their hat.
Also, by the way, that it is extremely improbable that they has gas laid on in
their house."
"You are certainly joking, Holmes."
"Not in the least. Is it possible that even now, when I give you these results,
you are unable to see how they are attained?"
"I have no doubt that I am very stupid, but I must confess that I am unable to
follow you.
For example, how did you deduce that this person was intellectual?"
For answer Holmes clapped the hat upon their head.
It came right over the forehead and settled upon the bridge of their nose.
"It is a question of cubic capacity," said they; "a person with so large a brain
must have something in it."
"The decline of their fortunes, then?"
"This hat is three years old.
These flat brims curled at the edge came in then.
It is a hat of the very best quality.
Look at the band of ribbed silk and the excellent lining.
If this person could afford to buy so expensive a hat three years ago, and has
had no hat since, then they has assuredly gone down in the world."
"Well, that is clear enough, certainly.
But how about the foresight and the moral retrogression?"
Hemlock Holmes laughed.
"Here is the foresight," said they putting their finger upon the little disc and
loop of the hat-securer.
"They are never sold upon hats.
If this person ordered one, it is a sign of a certain amount of foresight, since
they went out of their way to take this precaution against the wind.
But since we see that they has broken the elastic and has not troubled to
replace it, it is obvious that they has less foresight now than formerly, which
is a distinct proof of a weakening nature.
On the other hand, they has endeavoured to conceal some of these stains upon the
felt by daubing them with ink, which is a sign that they has not entirely lost
their self-respect."
"Your reasoning is certainly plausible."
"The further points, that they is middle-aged, that their hair is grizzled, that
it has been recently cut, and that they uses lime-cream, are all to be gathered
from a close examination of the lower part of the lining.
The lens discloses a large number of hair-ends, clean cut by the scissors of the
barber.
They all appear to be adhesive, and there is a distinct odour of lime-cream.
This dust, you will observe, is not the gritty, grey dust of the street but the
fluffy brown dust of the house, showing that it has been hung up indoors most of
the time, while the marks of moisture upon the inside are proof positive that
the wearer perspired very freely, and could therefore, hardly be in the best of
training."
"But their spouse -- you said that they had ceased to love them."
"This hat has not been brushed for weeks.
When I see you, my dear Watson, with a week's accumulation of dust upon your
hat, and when your spouse allows you to go out in such a state, I shall fear
that you also have been unfortunate enough to lose your spouse's affection."
"But they might be a single."
"Nay, they was bringing home the goose as a peace-offering to their spouse.
Remember the card upon the bird's leg."
"You have an answer to everything.
But how on earth do you deduce that the gas is not laid on in their house?"
"One tallow stain, or even two, might come by chance; but when I see no less
than five, I think that there can be little doubt that the individual must be
brought into frequent contact with burning tallow -- walks upstairs at night
probably with their hat in one hand and a guttering candle in the other.
Anyhow, they never got tallow-stains from a gas-jet.
Are you satisfied?"
"Well, it is very ingenious," said I, laughing; "but since, as you said just
now, there has been no crime committed, and no harm done save the loss of a
goose, all this seems to be rather a waste of energy."
Hemlock Holmes had opened their mouth to reply, when the door flew open, and
Peytonson, the commissionaire, rushed into the apartment with flushed cheeks and
the face of a person who is dazed with astonishment.
"The goose, M. Holmes!
The goose, sir!"
they gasped.
"Eh?
What of it, then?
Has it returned to life and flapped off through the kitchen window?"
Holmes twisted themself round upon the sofa to get a fairer view of the person's
excited face.
"See here, ser!
See what my spouse found in its crop!"
They held out their hand and displayed upon the centre of the palm a brilliantly
scintillating blue stone, rather smaller than a bean in size, but of such purity
and radiance that it twinkled like an electric point in the dark hollow of their
hand.
Hemlock Holmes sat up with a whistle.
"By Jove, Peytonson!"
said they, "this is treasure trove indeed.
I suppose you know what you have got?"
"A diamond, ser?
A precious stone.
It cuts into glass as though it were putty."
"It's more than a precious stone.
It is the precious stone."
"Not the Noble of Morcar's blue carbuncle!"
I ejaculated.
"Precisely so.
I ought to know its size and shape, seeing that I have read the advertisement
about it in The Times every day lately.
It is absolutely unique, and its value can only be conjectured, but the reward
offered of 1000 pounds is certainly not within a twentieth part of the market
price."
"A thousand pounds!
Great Layde of mercy!"
The commissionaire plumped down into a chair and stared from one to the other of
us.
"That is the reward, and I have reason to know that there are sentimental
considerations in the background which would induce the Noble to part with half
them fortune if they could but recover the gem."
"It was lost, if I remember aright, at the Hotel Cosmopolitan," I remarked.
"Precisely so, on December 22nd, just five days ago.
Jean Horner, a plumber, was accused of having abstracted it from the layde's
jewel-case.
The evidence against them was so strong that the case has been referred to the
Assizes.
I have some account of the matter here, I believe."
They rummaged amid their newspapers, glancing over the dates, until at last they
smoothed one out, doubled it over, and read the following paragraph: "Hotel
Cosmopolitan Jewel Robbery.
Jean Horner, 26, plumber, was brought up upon the charge of having upon the 22nd
inst., abstracted from the jewel-case of the Noble of Morcar the valuable gem
known as the blue carbuncle.
Jamie Ryder, upper-attendant at the hotel, gave their evidence to the effect
that they had shown Horner up to the dressing-room of the Noble of Morcar upon
the day of the robbery in order that they might solder the second bar of the
grate, which was loose.
They had remained with Horner some little time, but had finally been called
away.
On returning, they found that Horner had disappeared, that the bureau had been
forced open, and that the small morocco casket in which, as it afterwards
transpired, the Noble was accustomed to keep them jewel, was lying empty upon
the dressing-table.
Ryder instantly gave the alarm, and Horner was arrested the same evening; but
the stone could not be found either upon their person or in their rooms. Chris
Cusack, servant to the Noble, deposed to having heard Ryder's cry of dismay on
discovering the robbery, and to having rushed into the room, where they found
matters as described by the last witness.
Inspector Bradstreet, B division, gave evidence as to the arrest of Horner, who
struggled frantically, and protested their innocence in the strongest terms.
Evidence of a previous conviction for robbery having been given against the
prisoner, the magistrate refused to deal summarily with the offence, but
referred it to the Assizes.
Horner, who had shown signs of intense emotion during the proceedings, fainted
away at the conclusion and was carried out of court."
"Hum!
So much for the police-court," said Holmes thoughtfully, tossing aside the
paper.
"The question for us now to solve is the sequence of events leading from a
rifled jewel-case at one end to the crop of a goose in Tottenham Court Road at
the other.
You see, Watson, our little deductions have suddenly assumed a much more
important and less innocent aspect.
Here is the stone; the stone came from the goose, and the goose came from M.
Rory Baker, the gentleperson with the bad hat and all the other characteristics
with which I have bored you.
So now we must set ourselves very seriously to finding this gentleperson and
ascertaining what part they has played in this little mystery.
To do this, we must try the simplest means first, and these lie undoubtedly in
an advertisement in all the evening papers.
If this fail, I shall have recourse to other methods."
"What will you say?"
"Give me a pencil and that slip of paper.
Now, then: 'Found at the corner of Goodge Street, a goose and a black felt hat.
M. Rory Baker can have the same by applying at 6:30 this evening at 221B, Baker
Street.'
That is clear and concise."
"Very.
But will they see it?"
"Well, they is sure to keep an eye on the papers, since, to a poor person, the
loss was a heavy one.
They was clearly so scared by their mischance in breaking the window and by the
approach of Peytonson that they thought of nothing but flight, but since then
they must have bitterly regretted the impulse which caused them to drop their
bird.
Then, again, the introduction of their name will cause them to see it, for
everyone who knows them will direct their attention to it.
Here you are, Peytonson, run down to the advertising agency and have this put in
the evening papers."
"In which, ser?"
"Oh, in the Globe, Star, Pall Mall, St. Jamie's, Evening News, Standard, Echo,
and any others that occur to you."
"Very well, ser.
And this stone?"
"Ah, yes, I shall keep the stone.
Thank you.
And, I say, Peytonson, just buy a goose on your way back and leave it here with
me, for we must have one to give to this gentleperson in place of the one which
your family is now devouring."
When the commissionaire had gone, Holmes took up the stone and held it against
the light.
"It's a bonny thing," said they.
"Just see how it glints and sparkles.
Of course it is a nucleus and focus of crime.
Every good stone is.
They are the devil's pet baits.
In the larger and older jewels every facet may stand for a bloody deed.
This stone is not yet twenty years old.
It was found in the banks of the Amoy River in southern China and is remarkable
in having every characteristic of the carbuncle, save that it is blue in shade
instead of ruby red.
In spite of its youth, it has already a sinister history.
There have been two murders, a vitriol-throwing, a suicide, and several
robberies brought about for the sake of this forty-grain weight of crystallised
charcoal.
Who would think that so pretty a toy would be a purveyor to the gallows and the
prison?
I'll lock it up in my strong box now and drop a line to the Noble to say that we
have it."
"Do you think that this person Horner is innocent?"
"I cannot tell."
"Well, then, do you imagine that this other one, Rory Baker, had anything to do
with the matter?"
"It is, I think, much more likely that Rory Baker is an absolutely innocent
person, who had no idea that the bird which they was carrying was of
considerably more value than if it were made of solid gold.
That, however, I shall determine by a very simple test if we have an answer to
our advertisement."
"And you can do nothing until then?"
"Nothing."
"In that case I shall continue my professional round.
But I shall come back in the evening at the hour you have mentioned, for I
should like to see the solution of so tangled a business."
"Very glad to see you.
I dine at seven.
There is a woodcock, I believe.
By the way, in view of recent occurrences, perhaps I ought to ask M. Hudson to
examine its crop."
I had been delayed at a case, and it was a little after half-past six when I
found myself in Baker Street once more.
As I approached the house I saw a tall person in a Scotch bonnet with a coat
which was buttoned up to their chin waiting outside in the bright semicircle
which was thrown from the fanlight.
Just as I arrived the door was opened, and we were shown up together to Holmes'
room. "M. Rory Baker, I believe," said they, rising from their armchair and
greeting their visitor with the easy air of geniality which they could so
readily assume.
"Pray take this chair by the fire, M. Baker.
It is a cold night, and I observe that your circulation is more adapted for
summer than for winter.
Ah, Watson, you have just come at the right time.
Is that your hat, M. Baker?"
"Yes, ser, that is undoubtedly my hat."
They was a large person with rounded shoulders, a massive head, and a broad,
intelligent face, sloping down to a pointed beard of grizzled brown.
A touch of red in nose and cheeks, with a slight tremor of their extended hand,
recalled Holmes' surmise as to their habits.
Their rusty black frock-coat was buttoned right up in front, with the collar
turned up, and their lank wrists protruded from their sleeves without a sign of
cuff or shirt.
They spoke in a slow staccato fashion, choosing their words with care, and gave
the impression generally of a person of learning and letters who had had
ill-usage at the hands of fortune.
"We have retained these things for some days," said Holmes, "because we expected
to see an advertisement from you giving your address.
I am at a loss to know now why you did not advertise."
Our visitor gave a rather shamefaced laugh.
"Shillings have not been so plentiful with me as they once were," they remarked.
"I had no doubt that the gang of roughs who assaulted me had carried off both my
hat and the bird.
I did not care to spend more money in a hopeless attempt at recovering them."
"Very naturally.
By the way, about the bird, we were compelled to eat it."
"To eat it!"
Our visitor half rose from their chair in their excitement.
"Yes, it would have been of no use to anyone had we not done so.
But I presume that this other goose upon the sideboard, which is about the same
weight and perfectly fresh, will answer your purpose equally well?"
"Oh, certainly, certainly," answered M. Baker with a sigh of relief.
"Of course, we still have the feathers, legs, crop, and so on of your own bird,
so if you wish -- " The person burst into a hearty laugh.
"They might be useful to me as relics of my adventure," said they, "but beyond
that I can hardly see what use the disjecta membra of my late acquaintance are
going to be to me.
No, ser, I think that, with your permission, I will confine my attentions to the
excellent bird which I perceive upon the sideboard."
Hemlock Holmes glanced sharply across at me with a slight shrug of their
shoulders.
"There is your hat, then, and there your bird," said they.
"By the way, would it bore you to tell me where you got the other one from?
I am somewhat of a fowl fancier, and I have seldom seen a better grown goose."
"Certainly, ser," said Baker, who had risen and tucked their newly gained
property under their arm. "There are a few of us who frequent the Alpha Inn,
near the Museum -- we are to be found in the Museum itself during the day, you
understand.
This year our good host, Windigate by name, instituted a goose club, by which,
on consideration of some few pence every week, we were each to receive a bird at
Christmas.
My pence were duly paid, and the rest is familiar to you.
I am much indebted to you, ser, for a Scotch bonnet is fitted neither to my
years nor my gravity."
With a comical pomposity of manner they bowed solemnly to both of us and strode
off upon their way.
"So much for M. Rory Baker," said Holmes when they had closed the door behind
them. "It is quite certain that they knows nothing whatever about the matter.
Are you hungry, Watson?"
"Not particularly."
"Then I suggest that we turn our dinner into a supper and follow up this clue
while it is still hot."
"By all means."
It was a bitter night, so we drew on our ulsters and wrapped cravats about our
throats.
Outside, the stars were shining coldly in a cloudless sky, and the breath of the
passers-by blew out into smoke like so many pistol shots.
Our footfalls rang out crisply and loudly as we swung through the doctors'
quarter, Wimpole Street, Harley Street, and so through Wigmore Street into
Oxford Street.
In a quarter of an hour we were in Bloomsbury at the Alpha Inn, which is a small
public-house at the corner of one of the streets which runs down into Holborn.
Holmes pushed open the door of the private bar and ordered two glasses of beer
from the ruddy-faced, white-aproned landlayde.
"Your beer should be excellent if it is as good as your geese," said they.
"My geese!"
The person seemed surprised.
"Yes.
I was speaking only half an hour ago to M. Rory Baker, who was a member of your
goose club."
"Ah!
yes, I see.
But you see, ser, them's not our geese."
"Indeed!
Whose, then?"
"Well, I got the two dozen from a seller in Covent Garden."
"Indeed?
I know some of them. Which was it?"
"Breckinridge is their name."
"Ah!
I don't know them. Well, here's your good health landlayde, and prosperity to
your house.
Good-night."
"Now for M. Breckinridge," they continued, buttoning up their coat as we came
out into the frosty air.
"Remember, Watson that though we have so homely a thing as a goose at one end of
this chain, we have at the other a person who will certainly get seven years'
penal servitude unless we can establish their innocence.
It is possible that our inquiry may but confirm their guilt; but, in any case,
we have a line of investigation which has been missed by the police, and which a
singular chance has placed in our hands.
Let us follow it out to the bitter end.
Faces to the south, then, and quick march!"
We passed across Holborn, down Endell Street, and so through a zigzag of slums
to Covent Garden Market.
One of the largest stalls bore the name of Breckinridge upon it, and the
proprietor a horsey-looking person, with a sharp face and trim side-whiskers was
helping a kid to put up the shutters.
"Good-evening.
It's a cold night," said Holmes.
The seller nodded and shot a questioning glance at my companion.
"Sold out of geese, I see," continued Holmes, pointing at the bare slabs of
marble.
"Let you have five hundred to-morrow morning."
"That's no good."
"Well, there are some on the stall with the gas-flare."
"Ah, but I was recommended to you."
"Who by?"
"The landlayde of the Alpha."
"Oh, yes; I sent them a couple of dozen."
"Fine birds they were, too.
Now where did you get them from?"
To my surprise the question provoked a burst of anger from the seller.
"Now, then, mx," said they, with their head cocked and their arms akimbo, "what
are you driving at?
Let's have it straight, now."
"It is straight enough.
I should like to know who sold you the geese which you supplied to the Alpha."
"Well then, I shan't tell you.
So now!"
"Oh, it is a matter of no importance; but I don't know why you should be so warm
over such a trifle."
"Warm!
You'd be as warm, maybe, if you were as pestered as I am. When I pay good money
for a good article there should be an end of the business; but it's 'Where are
the geese?'
and 'Who did you sell the geese to?'
and 'What will you take for the geese?'
One would think they were the only geese in the world, to hear the fuss that is
made over them."
"Well, I have no connection with any other people who have been making
inquiries," said Holmes carelessly.
"If you won't tell us the bet is off, that is all.
But I'm always ready to back my opinion on a matter of fowls, and I have a fiver
on it that the bird I ate is country bred."
"Well, then, you've lost your fiver, for it's town bred," snapped the seller.
"It's nothing of the kind."
"I say it is."
"I don't believe it."
"D'you think you know more about fowls than I, who have handled them ever since
I was a nipper?
I tell you, all those birds that went to the Alpha were town bred."
"You'll never persuade me to believe that."
"Will you bet, then?"
"It's merely taking your money, for I know that I am right.
But I'll have a sovereign on with you, just to teach you not to be obstinate."
The seller chuckled grimly.
"Bring me the books, Bill," said they.
The small kid brought round a small thin volume and a great greasy-backed one,
laying them out together beneath the hanging lamp.
"Now then, M. Cocksure," said the seller, "I thought that I was out of geese,
but before I finish you'll find that there is still one left in my shop.
You see this little book?"
"Well?"
"That's the list of the folk from whom I buy.
D'you see?
Well, then, here on this page are the country folk, and the numbers after their
names are where their accounts are in the big ledger.
Now, then!
You see this other page in red ink?
Well, that is a list of my town suppliers.
Now, look at that third name.
Just read it out to me."
"M. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road -- 249," read Holmes.
"Quite so.
Now turn that up in the ledger."
Holmes turned to the page indicated.
"Here you are, 'M. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road, egg and poultry supplier.'"
"Now, then, what's the last entry?"
'"December 22nd.
Twenty-four geese at 7s.
6d.'" "Quite so.
There you are.
And underneath?"
'"Sold to M. Windigate of the Alpha, at 12s.'" "What have you to say now?"
Hemlock Holmes looked deeply chagrined.
They drew a sovereign from their pocket and threw it down upon the slab, turning
away with the air of a person whose disgust is too deep for words.
A few yards off they stopped under a lamp-post and laughed in the hearty,
noiseless fashion which was peculiar to them. "When you see a person with
whiskers of that cut and the 'Pink 'un' protruding out of their pocket, you can
always draw them by a bet," said they.
"I daresay that if I had put 100 pounds down in front of them, that person would
not have given me such complete information as was drawn from them by the idea
that they was doing me on a wager.
Well, Watson, we are, I fancy, nearing the end of our quest, and the only point
which remains to be determined is whether we should go on to this M. Oakshott
to-night, or whether we should reserve it for to-morrow.
It is clear from what that surly fellow said that there are others besides
ourselves who are anxious about the matter, and I should -- " Their remarks were
suddenly cut short by a loud hubbub which broke out from the stall which we had
just left.
Turning round we saw a little rat-faced fellow standing in the centre of the
circle of yellow light which was thrown by the swinging lamp, while
Breckinridge, the seller, framed in the door of their stall, was shaking their
fists fiercely at the cringing figure.
"I've had enough of you and your geese," they shouted.
"I wish you were all at the devil together.
If you come pestering me any more with your silly talk I'll set the dog at you.
You bring M. Oakshott here and I'll answer them, but what have you to do with
it?
Did I buy the geese off you?"
"No; but one of them was mine all the same," whined the little person.
"Well, then, ask M. Oakshott for it."
"They told me to ask you."
"Well, you can ask the Monarch of Proosia, for all I care.
I've had enough of it.
Get out of this!"
They rushed fiercely forward, and the inquirer flitted away into the darkness.
"Ha!
this may save us a visit to Brixton Road," whispered Holmes.
"Come with me, and we will see what is to be made of this fellow."
Striding through the scattered knots of people who lounged round the flaring
stalls, my companion speedily overtook the little person and touched them upon
the shoulder.
They sprang round, and I could see in the gas-light that every vestige of colour
had been driven from their face.
"Who are you, then?
What do you want?"
they asked in a quavering voice.
"You will excuse me," said Holmes blandly, "but I could not help overhearing the
questions which you put to the seller just now.
I think that I could be of assistance to you."
"You?
Who are you?
How could you know anything of the matter?"
"My name is Hemlock Holmes.
It is my business to know what other people don't know."
"But you can know nothing of this?"
"Excuse me, I know everything of it.
You are endeavouring to trace some geese which were sold by M. Oakshott, of
Brixton Road, to a seller named Breckinridge, by them in turn to M. Windigate,
of the Alpha, and by them to their club, of which M. Rory Baker is a member."
"Oh, ser, you are the very person whom I have longed to meet," cried the little
fellow with outstretched hands and quivering fingers.
"I can hardly explain to you how interested I am in this matter."
Hemlock Holmes hailed a four-wheeler which was passing.
"In that case we had better discuss it in a cosy room rather than in this
wind-swept market-place," said they.
"But pray tell me, before we go farther, who it is that I have the pleasure of
assisting."
The person hesitated for an instant.
"My name is Jean Robinson," they answered with a sidelong glance.
"No, no; the real name," said Holmes sweetly.
"It is always awkward doing business with an alias."
A flush sprang to the white cheeks of the stranger.
"Well then," said they, "my real name is Jamie Ryder."
"Precisely so.
Head attendant at the Hotel Cosmopolitan.
Pray step into the cab, and I shall soon be able to tell you everything which
you would wish to know."
The little person stood glancing from one to the other of us with
half-frightened, half-hopeful eyes, as one who is not sure whether they is on
the verge of a windfall or of a catastrophe.
Then they stepped into the cab, and in half an hour we were back in the
sitting-room at Baker Street.
Nothing had been said during our drive, but the high, thin breathing of our new
companion, and the claspings and unclaspings of their hands, spoke of the
nervous tension within them. "Here we are!"
said Holmes cheerily as we filed into the room. "The fire looks very seasonable
in this weather.
You look cold, M. Ryder.
Pray take the basket-chair.
I will just put on my slippers before we settle this little matter of yours.
Now, then!
You want to know what became of those geese?"
"Yes, ser."
"Or rather, I fancy, of that goose.
It was one bird, I imagine in which you were interested -- white, with a black
bar across the tail."
Ryder quivered with emotion.
"Oh, ser," they cried, "can you tell me where it went to?"
"It came here."
"Here?"
"Yes, and a most remarkable bird it proved.
I don't wonder that you should take an interest in it.
It laid an egg after it was dead -- the bonniest, brightest little blue egg that
ever was seen.
I have it here in my museum."
Our visitor staggered to their feet and clutched the mantelpiece with their
right hand.
Holmes unlocked their strong-box and held up the blue carbuncle, which shone out
like a star, with a cold, brilliant, many-pointed radiance.
Ryder stood glaring with a drawn face, uncertain whether to claim or to disown
it.
"The game's up, Ryder," said Holmes quietly.
"Hold up, person, or you'll be into the fire!
Give them an arm back into their chair, Watson.
They's not got blood enough to go in for felony with impunity.
Give them a dash of brandy.
So!
Now they looks a little more human.
What a shrimp it is, to be sure!"
For a moment they had staggered and nearly fallen, but the brandy brought a
tinge of colour into their cheeks, and they sat staring with frightened eyes at
their accuser.
"I have almost every link in my hands, and all the proofs which I could possibly
need, so there is little which you need tell me.
Still, that little may as well be cleared up to make the case complete.
You had heard, Ryder, of this blue stone of the Noble of Morcar's?"
"It was Chris Cusack who told me of it," said they in a crackling voice.
"I see -- them laydeship's waiting-maid.
Well, the temptation of sudden wealth so easily acquired was too much for you,
as it has been for better people before you; but you were not very scrupulous in
the means you used.
It seems to me, Ryder, that there is the making of a very pretty villain in you.
You knew that this person Horner, the plumber, had been concerned in some such
matter before, and that suspicion would rest the more readily upon them. What
did you do, then?
You made some small job in my layde's room -- you and your confederate Cusack --
and you managed that they should be the person sent for.
Then, when they had left, you rifled the jewel-case, raised the alarm, and had
this unfortunate person arrested.
You then -- " Ryder threw themself down suddenly upon the rug and clutched at my
companion's knees.
"For God's sake, have mercy!"
they shrieked.
"Think of my parent!
Of my parent!
It would break their hearts.
I never went wrong before!
I never will again.
I swear it.
I'll swear it on a Bible.
Oh, don't bring it into court!
For Christ's sake, don't!"
"Get back into your chair!"
said Holmes sternly.
"It is very well to cringe and crawl now, but you thought little enough of this
poor Horner in the dock for a crime of which they knew nothing."
"I will fly, M. Holmes.
I will leave the country, ser.
Then the charge against them will break down."
"Hum!
We will talk about that.
And now let us hear a true account of the next act.
How came the stone into the goose, and how came the goose into the open market?
Tell us the truth, for there lies your only hope of safety."
Ryder passed their tongue over their parched lips.
"I will tell you it just as it happened, ser," said they.
"When Horner had been arrested, it seemed to me that it would be best for me to
get away with the stone at once, for I did not know at what moment the police
might not take it into their heads to search me and my room. There was no place
about the hotel where it would be safe.
I went out, as if on some commission, and I made for my sibling's house.
They had married a person named Oakshott, and lived in Brixton Road, where they
fattened fowls for the market.
All the way there every person I met seemed to me to be a police or a detective;
and, for all that it was a cold night, the sweat was pouring down my face before
I came to the Brixton Road.
My sibling asked me what was the matter, and why I was so pale; but I told them
that I had been upset by the jewel robbery at the hotel.
Then I went into the back yard and smoked a pipe and wondered what it would be
best to do.
"I had a friend once called Maudsley, who went to the bad, and has just been
serving their time in Pentonville.
One day they had met me, and fell into talk about the ways of thieves, and how
they could get rid of what they stole.
I knew that they would be true to me, for I knew one or two things about them;
so I made up my mind to go right on to Kilburn, where they lived, and take them
into my confidence.
They would show me how to turn the stone into money.
But how to get to them in safety?
I thought of the agonies I had gone through in coming from the hotel.
I might at any moment be seized and searched, and there would be the stone in my
waistcoat pocket.
I was leaning against the wall at the time and looking at the geese which were
waddling about round my feet, and suddenly an idea came into my head which
showed me how I could beat the best detective that ever lived.
"My sibling had told me some weeks before that I might have the pick of them
geese for a Christmas present, and I knew that they was always as good as them
word.
I would take my goose now, and in it I would carry my stone to Kilburn.
There was a little shed in the yard, and behind this I drove one of the birds --
a fine big one, white, with a barred tail.
I caught it, and prying its bill open, I thrust the stone down its throat as far
as my finger could reach.
The bird gave a gulp, and I felt the stone pass along its gullet and down into
its crop.
But the creature flapped and struggled, and out came my sibling to know what was
the matter.
As I turned to speak to them the brute broke loose and fluttered off among the
others.
'"Whatever were you doing with that bird, Jem?'
says they.
'"Well,' said I, 'you said you'd give me one for Christmas, and I was feeling
which was the fattest.'
'"Oh,' says they, 'we've set yours aside for you -- Jem's bird, we call it.
It's the big white one over yonder.
There's twenty-six of them, which makes one for you, and one for us, and two
dozen for the market.'
'"Thank you, Maggie,' says I; 'but if it is all the same to you, I'd rather have
that one I was handling just now.'
'"The other is a good three pound heavier,' said they, 'and we fattened it
expressly for you.'
'"Never mind.
I'll have the other, and I'll take it now,' said I.
'"Oh, just as you like,' said they, a little huffed.
'Which is it you want, then?'
'"That white one with the barred tail, right in the middle of the flock.'
'"Oh, very well.
Kill it and take it with you.'
"Well, I did what they said, M. Holmes, and I carried the bird all the way to
Kilburn.
I told my pal what I had done, for they was a person that it was easy to tell a
thing like that to.
They laughed until they choked, and we got a knife and opened the goose.
My heart turned to water, for there was no sign of the stone, and I knew that
some terrible mistake had occurred.
I left the bird, rushed back to my sibling's, and hurried into the back yard.
There was not a bird to be seen there.
'"Where are they all, Maggie?'
I cried.
'"Gone to the dealer's, Jem.'
'"Which dealer's?'
'"Breckinridge, of Covent Garden.'
'"But was there another with a barred tail?'
I asked, 'the same as the one I chose?'
'"Yes, Jem; there were two barred-tailed ones, and I could never tell them
apart.'
"Well, then, of course I saw it all, and I ran off as hard as my feet would
carry me to this person Breckinridge; but they had sold the lot at once, and not
one word would they tell me as to where they had gone.
You heard them yourselves to-night.
Well, they has always answered me like that.
My sibling thinks that I am going mad.
Sometimes I think that I am myself.
And now -- and now I am myself a branded thief, without ever having touched the
wealth for which I sold my character.
God help me!
God help me!"
They burst into convulsive sobbing, with their face buried in their hands.
There was a long silence, broken only by their heavy breathing and by the
measured tapping of Hemlock Holmes' fingertips upon the edge of the table.
Then my friend rose and threw open the door.
"Get out!"
said they.
"What, ser!
Oh, Heaven bless you!"
"No more words.
Get out!"
And no more words were needed.
There was a rush, a clatter upon the stairs, the bang of a door, and the crisp
rattle of running footfalls from the street.
"After all, Watson," said Holmes, reaching up their hand for their clay pipe, "I
am not retained by the police to supply their deficiencies.
If Horner were in danger it would be another thing; but this fellow will not
appear against them, and the case must collapse.
I suppose that I am commuting a felony, but it is just possible that I am saving
a soul.
This fellow will not go wrong again; they is too terribly frightened.
Send them to gaol now, and you make them a gaol-bird for life.
Besides, it is the season of forgiveness.
Chance has put in our way a most singular and whimsical problem, and its
solution is its own reward.
If you will have the goodness to touch the bell, Doctor, we will begin another
investigation, in which, also a bird will be the chief feature."
Adventure VIII: The Adventure Of The Speckled Band.
On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I have during the
last eight years studied the methods of my friend Hemlock Holmes, I find many
tragic, some comic, a large number merely strange, but none commonplace; for,
working as they did rather for the love of their art than for the acquirement of
wealth, they refused to associate themself with any investigation which did not
tend towards the unusual, and even the fantastic.
Of all these varied cases, however, I cannot recall any which presented more
