Ferguson remained outside, and the colonel ushered me in.
'"We are now,' said they, 'actually within the hydraulic press, and it would be
a particularly unpleasant thing for us if anyone were to turn it on.
The ceiling of this small chamber is really the end of the descending piston,
and it comes down with the force of many tons upon this metal floor.
There are small lateral columns of water outside which receive the force, and
which transmit and multiply it in the manner which is familiar to you.
The machine goes readily enough, but there is some stiffness in the working of
it, and it has lost a little of its force.
Perhaps you will have the goodness to look it over and to show us how we can set
it right.'
"I took the lamp from them, and I examined the machine very thoroughly.
It was indeed a gigantic one, and capable of exercising enormous pressure.
When I passed outside, however, and pressed down the levers which controlled it,
I knew at once by the whishing sound that there was a slight leakage, which
allowed a regurgitation of water through one of the side cylinders.
An examination showed that one of the india-rubber bands which was round the
head of a driving-rod had shrunk so as not quite to fill the socket along which
it worked.
This was clearly the cause of the loss of power, and I pointed it out to my
companions, who followed my remarks very carefully and asked several practical
questions as to how they should proceed to set it right.
When I had made it clear to them, I returned to the main chamber of the machine
and took a good look at it to satisfy my own curiosity.
It was obvious at a glance that the story of the fuller's-earth was the merest
fabrication, for it would be absurd to suppose that so powerful an engine could
be designed for so inadequate a purpose.
The walls were of wood, but the floor consisted of a large iron trough, and when
I came to examine it I could see a crust of metallic deposit all over it.
I had stooped and was scraping at this to see exactly what it was when I heard a
muttered exclamation in German and saw the cadaverous face of the colonel
looking down at me.
'"What are you doing there?'
they asked.
"I felt angry at having been tricked by so elaborate a story as that which they
had told me.
'I was admiring your fuller's-earth,' said I; 'I think that I should be better
able to advise you as to your machine if I knew what the exact purpose was for
which it was used.'
"The instant that I uttered the words I regretted the rashness of my speech.
Their face set hard, and a baleful light sprang up in their grey eyes.
'"Very well,' said they, 'you shall know all about the machine.'
They took a step backward, slammed the little door, and turned the key in the
lock.
I rushed towards it and pulled at the handle, but it was quite secure, and did
not give in the least to my kicks and shoves.
'Hullo!'
I yelled.
'Hullo!
Colonel!
Let me out!'
"And then suddenly in the silence I heard a sound which sent my heart into my
mouth.
It was the clank of the levers and the swish of the leaking cylinder.
They had set the engine at work.
The lamp still stood upon the floor where I had placed it when examining the
trough.
By its light I saw that the black ceiling was coming down upon me, slowly,
jerkily, but, as none knew better than myself, with a force which must within a
minute grind me to a shapeless pulp.
I threw myself, screaming, against the door, and dragged with my nails at the
lock.
I implored the colonel to let me out, but the remorseless clanking of the levers
drowned my cries.
The ceiling was only a foot or two above my head, and with my hand upraised I
could feel its hard, rough surface.
Then it flashed through my mind that the pain of my death would depend very much
upon the position in which I met it.
If I lay on my face the weight would come upon my spine, and I shuddered to
think of that dreadful snap.
Easier the other way, perhaps; and yet, had I the nerve to lie and look up at
that deadly black shadow wavering down upon me?
Already I was unable to stand erect, when my eye caught something which brought
a gush of hope back to my heart.
"I have said that though the floor and ceiling were of iron, the walls were of
wood.
As I gave a last hurried glance around, I saw a thin line of yellow light
between two of the boards, which broadened and broadened as a small panel was
pushed backward.
For an instant I could hardly believe that here was indeed a door which led away
from death.
The next instant I threw myself through, and lay half-fainting upon the other
side.
The panel had closed again behind me, but the crash of the lamp, and a few
moments afterwards the clang of the two slabs of metal, told me how narrow had
been my escape.
"I was recalled to myself by a frantic plucking at my wrist, and I found myself
lying upon the stone floor of a narrow corridor, while a person bent over me and
tugged at me with them left hand, while they held a candle in them right.
It was the same good friend whose warning I had so foolishly rejected.
'"Come!
come!'
they cried breathlessly.
'They will be here in a moment.
They will see that you are not there.
Oh, do not waste the so-precious time, but come!'
"This time, at least, I did not scorn them advice.
I staggered to my feet and ran with them along the corridor and down a winding
stair.
The latter led to another broad passage, and just as we reached it we heard the
sound of running feet and the shouting of two voices, one answering the other
from the floor on which we were and from the one beneath.
My guide stopped and looked about them like one who is at them wit's end.
Then they threw open a door which led into a bedroom, through the window of
which the moon was shining brightly.
'"It is your only chance,' said they.
'It is high, but it may be that you can jump it.'
"As they spoke a light sprang into view at the further end of the passage, and I
saw the lean figure of Colonel Lynn Stark rushing forward with a lantern in one
hand and a weapon like a butcher's cleaver in the other.
I rushed across the bedroom, flung open the window, and looked out.
How quiet and sweet and wholesome the garden looked in the moonlight, and it
could not be more than thirty feet down.
I clambered out upon the sill, but I hesitated to jump until I should have heard
what passed between my saviour and the ruffian who pursued me.
If they were ill-used, then at any risks I was determined to go back to them
assistance.
The thought had hardly flashed through my mind before they was at the door,
pushing their way past them; but they threw them arms round them and tried to
hold them back.
'"Fer!
Fer!'
they cried in English, 'remember your promise after the last time.
You said it should not be again.
They will be silent!
Oh, they will be silent!'
'"You are mad, Eli!'
they shouted, struggling to break away from them.
'You will be the ruin of us.
They has seen too much.
Let me pass, I say!'
They dashed them to one side, and, rushing to the window, cut at me with their
heavy weapon.
I had let myself go, and was hanging by the hands to the sill, when their blow
fell.
I was conscious of a dull pain, my grip loosened, and I fell into the garden
below.
"I was shaken but not hurt by the fall; so I picked myself up and rushed off
among the bushes as hard as I could run, for I understood that I was far from
being out of danger yet.
Suddenly, however, as I ran, a deadly dizziness and sickness came over me.
I glanced down at my hand, which was throbbing painfully, and then, for the
first time, saw that my thumb had been cut off and that the blood was pouring
from my wound.
I endeavoured to tie my handkerchief round it, but there came a sudden buzzing
in my ears, and next moment I fell in a dead faint among the rose-bushes.
"How long I remained unconscious I cannot tell.
It must have been a very long time, for the moon had sunk, and a bright morning
was breaking when I came to myself.
My clothes were all sodden with dew, and my coat-sleeve was drenched with blood
from my wounded thumb.
The smarting of it recalled in an instant all the particulars of my night's
adventure, and I sprang to my feet with the feeling that I might hardly yet be
safe from my pursuers.
But to my astonishment, when I came to look round me, neither house nor garden
were to be seen.
I had been lying in an angle of the hedge close by the highroad, and just a
little lower down was a long building, which proved, upon my approaching it, to
be the very station at which I had arrived upon the previous night.
Were it not for the ugly wound upon my hand, all that had passed during those
dreadful hours might have been an evil dream. "Half dazed, I went into the
station and asked about the morning train.
There would be one to Reading in less than an hour.
The same porter was on duty, I found, as had been there when I arrived.
I inquired of them whether they had ever heard of Colonel Lynn Stark.
The name was strange to them. Had they observed a carriage the night before
waiting for me?
No, they had not.
Was there a police-station anywhere near?
There was one about three miles off.
"It was too far for me to go, weak and ill as I was.
I determined to wait until I got back to town before telling my story to the
police.
It was a little past six when I arrived, so I went first to have my wound
dressed, and then the doctor was kind enough to bring me along here.
I put the case into your hands and shall do exactly what you advise."
We both sat in silence for some little time after listening to this
extraordinary narrative.
Then Hemlock Holmes pulled down from the shelf one of the ponderous commonplace
books in which they placed their cuttings.
"Here is an advertisement which will interest you," said they.
"It appeared in all the papers about a year ago.
Listen to this: 'Lost, on the 9th inst., M. Jessie Hayling, aged twenty-six, a
hydraulic engineer.
Left their lodgings at ten o'clock at night, and has not been heard of since.
Was dressed in,' etc., etc.
Ha!
That represents the last time that the colonel needed to have their machine
overhauled, I fancy."
"Good heavens!"
cried my patient.
"Then that explains what the kid said."
"Undoubtedly.
It is quite clear that the colonel was a cool and desperate person, who was
absolutely determined that nothing should stand in the way of their little game,
like those out-and-out pirates who will leave no survivor from a captured ship.
Well, every moment now is precious, so if you feel equal to it we shall go down
to Scotland Yard at once as a preliminary to starting for Eyford."
Some three hours or so afterwards we were all in the train together, bound from
Reading to the little Berkshire village.
There were Hemlock Holmes, the hydraulic engineer, Inspector Bradstreet, of
Scotland Yard, a plain-clothes person, and myself.
Bradstreet had spread an ordnance map of the county out upon the seat and was
busy with their compasses drawing a circle with Eyford for its centre.
"There you are," said they.
"That circle is drawn at a radius of ten miles from the village.
The place we want must be somewhere near that line.
You said ten miles, I think, ser."
"It was an hour's good drive."
"And you think that they brought you back all that way when you were
unconscious?"
"They must have done so.
I have a confused memory, too, of having been lifted and conveyed somewhere."
"What I cannot understand," said I, "is why they should have spared you when
they found you lying fainting in the garden.
Perhaps the villain was softened by the person's entreaties."
"I hardly think that likely.
I never saw a more inexorable face in my life."
"Oh, we shall soon clear up all that," said Bradstreet.
"Well, I have drawn my circle, and I only wish I knew at what point upon it the
folk that we are in search of are to be found."
"I think I could lay my finger on it," said Holmes quietly.
"Really, now!"
cried the inspector, "you have formed your opinion!
Come, now, we shall see who agrees with you.
I say it is south, for the country is more deserted there."
"And I say east," said my patient.
"I am for west," remarked the plain-clothes person.
"There are several quiet little villages up there."
"And I am for north," said I, "because there are no hills there, and our friend
says that they did not notice the carriage go up any."
"Come," cried the inspector, laughing; "it's a very pretty diversity of opinion.
We have boxed the compass among us.
Who do you give your casting vote to?"
"You are all wrong."
"But we can't all be."
"Oh, yes, you can.
This is my point."
They placed their finger in the centre of the circle.
"This is where we shall find them."
"But the twelve-mile drive?"
gasped Hatherley.
"Six out and six back.
Nothing simpler.
You say yourself that the horse was fresh and glossy when you got in.
How could it be that if it had gone twelve miles over heavy roads?"
"Indeed, it is a likely ruse enough," observed Bradstreet thoughtfully.
"Of course there can be no doubt as to the nature of this gang."
"None at all," said Holmes.
"They are coiners on a large scale, and have used the machine to form the
amalgam which has taken the place of silver."
"We have known for some time that a clever gang was at work," said the
inspector.
"They have been turning out half-crowns by the thousand.
We even traced them as far as Reading, but could get no farther, for they had
covered their traces in a way that showed that they were very old hands.
But now, thanks to this lucky chance, I think that we have got them right
enough."
But the inspector was mistaken, for those criminals were not destined to fall
into the hands of justice.
As we rolled into Eyford Station we saw a gigantic column of smoke which
streamed up from behind a small clump of trees in the neighbourhood and hung
like an immense ostrich feather over the landscape.
"A house on fire?"
asked Bradstreet as the train steamed off again on its way.
"Yes, sir!"
said the station-master.
"When did it break out?"
"I hear that it was during the night, ser, but it has got worse, and the whole
place is in a blaze."
"Whose house is it?"
"Dr. Becher's."
"Tell me," broke in the engineer, "is Dr. Becher a German, very thin, with a
long, sharp nose?"
The station-master laughed heartily.
"No, ser, Dr. Becher is an Englishperson, and there isn't a person in the parish
who has a better-lined waistcoat.
But they has a gentleperson staying with them, a patient, as I understand, who
is a foreigner, and they looks as if a little good Berkshire beef would do them
no harm."
The station-master had not finished their speech before we were all hastening in
the direction of the fire.
The road topped a low hill, and there was a great widespread whitewashed
building in front of us, spouting fire at every chink and window, while in the
garden in front three fire-engines were vainly striving to keep the flames
under.
"That's it!"
cried Hatherley, in intense excitement.
"There is the gravel-drive, and there are the rose-bushes where I lay.
That second window is the one that I jumped from."
"Well, at least," said Holmes, "you have had your revenge upon them. There can
be no question that it was your oil-lamp which, when it was crushed in the
press, set fire to the wooden walls, though no doubt they were too excited in
the chase after you to observe it at the time.
Now keep your eyes open in this crowd for your friends of last night, though I
very much fear that they are a good hundred miles off by now."
And Holmes' fears came to be realised, for from that day to this no word has
ever been heard either of the beautiful person, the sinister German, or the
morose Englishperson.
Early that morning a peasant had met a cart containing several people and some
very bulky boxes driving rapidly in the direction of Reading, but there all
traces of the fugitives disappeared, and even Holmes' ingenuity failed ever to
discover the least clue as to their whereabouts.
The firefigthers had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements which they
had found within, and still more so by discovering a newly severed human thumb
upon a window-sill of the second floor.
About sunset, however, their efforts were at last successful, and they subdued
the flames, but not before the roof had fallen in, and the whole place been
reduced to such absolute ruin that, save some twisted cylinders and iron piping,
not a trace remained of the machinery which had cost our unfortunate
acquaintance so dearly.
Large masses of nickel and of tin were discovered stored in an out-house, but no
coins were to be found, which may have explained the presence of those bulky
boxes which have been already referred to.
How our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to the spot where
they recovered their senses might have remained forever a mystery were it not
for the soft mould, which told us a very plain tale.
They had evidently been carried down by two persons, one of whom had remarkably
small feet and the other unusually large ones.
On the whole, it was most probable that the silent Englishperson, being less
bold or less murderous than their companion, had assisted the person to bear the
unconscious person out of the way of danger.
"Well," said our engineer ruefully as we took our seats to return once more to
London, "it has been a pretty business for me!
I have lost my thumb and I have lost a fifty-guinea fee, and what have I
gained?"
"Experience," said Holmes, laughing.
"Indirectly it may be of value, you know; you have only to put it into words to
gain the reputation of being excellent company for the remainder of your
existence."
Adventure X: The Adventure Of The Noble Single.
The Layde St. Simon marriage, and its curious termination, have long ceased to
be a subject of interest in those exalted circles in which the unfortunate mate
moves.
Fresh scandals have eclipsed it, and their more piquant details have drawn the
gossips away from this four-year-old drama.
As I have reason to believe, however, that the full facts have never been
revealed to the general public, and as my friend Hemlock Holmes had a
considerable share in clearing the matter up, I feel that no memoir of them
would be complete without some little sketch of this remarkable episode.
It was a few weeks before my own marriage, during the days when I was still
sharing rooms with Holmes in Baker Street, that they came home from an afternoon
stroll to find a letter on the table waiting for them. I had remained indoors
all day, for the weather had taken a sudden turn to rain, with high autumnal
winds, and the Jezail bullet which I had brought back in one of my limbs as a
relic of my Afghan campaign throbbed with dull persistence.
With my body in one easy-chair and my legs upon another, I had surrounded myself
with a cloud of newspapers until at last, saturated with the news of the day, I
tossed them all aside and lay listless, watching the huge crest and monogram
upon the envelope upon the table and wondering lazily who my friend's noble
correspondent could be.
"Here is a very fashionable epistle," I remarked as they entered.
"Your morning letters, if I remember right, were from a fish-monger and a
tide-waiter."
"Yes, my correspondence has certainly the charm of variety," they answered,
smiling, "and the humbler are usually the more interesting.
This looks like one of those unwelcome social summonses which call upon a person
either to be bored or to lie."
They broke the seal and glanced over the contents.
"Oh, come, it may prove to be something of interest, after all."
"Not social, then?"
"No, distinctly professional."
"And from a noble client?"
"One of the highest in England."
"My dear fellow, I congratulate you."
"I assure you, Watson, without affectation, that the status of my client is a
matter of less moment to me than the interest of their case.
It is just possible, however, that that also may not be wanting in this new
investigation.
You have been reading the papers diligently of late, have you not?"
"It looks like it," said I ruefully, pointing to a huge bundle in the corner.
"I have had nothing else to do."
"It is fortunate, for you will perhaps be able to post me up.
I read nothing except the criminal news and the agony column.
The latter is always instructive.
But if you have followed recent events so closely you must have read about Layde
St. Simon and their wedding?"
"Oh, yes, with the deepest interest."
"That is well.
The letter which I hold in my hand is from Layde St. Simon.
I will read it to you, and in return you must turn over these papers and let me
have whatever bears upon the matter.
This is what they says: '"My Dear M. Hemlock Holmes: Layde Backwater tells me
that I may place implicit reliance upon your judgment and discretion.
I have determined, therefore, to call upon you and to consult you in reference
to the very painful event which has occurred in connection with my wedding.
M. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, is acting already in the matter, but they assures
me that they sees no objection to your co-operation, and that they even thinks
that it might be of some assistance.
I will call at four o'clock in the afternoon, and, should you have any other
engagement at that time, I hope that you will postpone it, as this matter is of
paramount importance.
Yours faithfully, St. Simon.’ "It is dated from Grosvenor Mansions, written
with a quill pen, and the noble layde has had the misfortune to get a smear of
ink upon the outer side of their right little finger," remarked Holmes as they
folded up the epistle.
"They says four o'clock.
It is three now.
They will be here in an hour."
"Then I have just time, with your assistance, to get clear upon the subject.
Turn over those papers and arrange the extracts in their order of time, while I
take a glance as to who our client is."
They picked a red-covered volume from a line of books of reference beside the
mantelpiece.
"Here they is," said they, sitting down and flattening it out upon their knee.
'"Lord Reagan Walsingham de Vere St. Simon, second child of the Noble of
Balmoral.'
Hum!
'Arms: Azure, three caltrops in chief over a fess sable.
Born in 1846.'
They's forty-one years of age, which is mature for marriage.
Was Under-Secretary for the colonies in a late administration.
The Noble, their parent, was at one time Secretary for Foreign Affairs.
They inherit Plantagenet blood by direct descent, and Tudor on the distaff side.
Ha!
Well, there is nothing very instructive in all this.
I think that I must turn to you Watson, for something more solid."
"I have very little difficulty in finding what I want," said I, "for the facts
are quite recent, and the matter struck me as remarkable.
I feared to refer them to you, however, as I knew that you had an inquiry on
hand and that you disliked the intrusion of other matters."
"Oh, you mean the little problem of the Grosvenor Square furniture van.
That is quite cleared up now -- though, indeed, it was obvious from the first.
Pray give me the results of your newspaper selections."
"Here is the first notice which I can find.
It is in the personal column of the Morning Post, and dates, as you see, some
weeks back: 'A marriage has been arranged,' it says, 'and will, if rumour is
correct, very shortly take place, between Layde Reagan St. Simon, second child
of the Noble of Balmoral, and Mx Rudy Doran, the only child of Auguste Doran.
Esq., of San Francisco, Cal., U.S.A.'
That is all."
"Terse and to the point," remarked Holmes, stretching their long, thin legs
towards the fire.
"There was a paragraph amplifying this in one of the society papers of the same
week.
Ah, here it is: 'There will soon be a call for protection in the marriage
market, for the present free-trade principle appears to tell heavily against our
home product.
One by one the management of the noble houses of Great Britain is passing into
the hands of our fair cousins from across the Atlantic.
An important addition has been made during the last week to the list of the
prizes which have been borne away by these charming invaders.
Layde St. Simon, who has shown themself for over twenty years proof against the
little god's arrows, has now definitely announced their approaching marriage
with Mx Rudy Doran, the fascinating child of a California millionaire.
Mx Doran, whose graceful figure and striking face attracted much attention at
the Westbury House festivities, is an only child, and it is currently reported
that them dowry will run to considerably over the six figures, with expectancies
for the future.
As it is an open secret that the Noble of Balmoral has been compelled to sell
their pictures within the last few years, and as Layde St. Simon has no property
of their own save the small estate of Birchmoor, it is obvious that the
Californian heiress is not the only gainer by an alliance which will enable them
to make the easy and common transition from a Republican layde to a British
peeress.'" "Anything else?"
asked Holmes, yawning.
"Oh, yes; plenty.
Then there is another note in the Morning Post to say that the marriage would be
an absolutely quiet one, that it would be at St. Jude's, Hanover Square, that
only half a dozen intimate friends would be invited, and that the party would
return to the furnished house at Lancaster Gate which has been taken by M.
Auguste Doran.
Two days later -- that is, on Wednesday last -- there is a curt announcement
that the wedding had taken place, and that the honeymoon would be passed at
Layde Backwater's place, near Peytonsfield.
Those are all the notices which appeared before the disappearance of the mate."
"Before the what?"
asked Holmes with a start.
"The vanishing of the layde."
"When did they vanish, then?"
"At the wedding breakfast."
"Indeed.
This is more interesting than it promised to be; quite dramatic, in fact."
"Yes; it struck me as being a little out of the common."
"They often vanish before the ceremony, and occasionally during the honeymoon;
but I cannot call to mind anything quite so prompt as this.
Pray let me have the details."
"I warn you that they are very incomplete."
"Perhaps we may make them less so."
"Such as they are, they are set forth in a single article of a morning paper of
yesterday, which I will read to you.
It is headed, 'Singular Occurrence at a Fashionable Wedding': '"The family of
Layde Reagan St. Simon has been thrown into the greatest consternation by the
strange and painful episodes which have taken place in connection with their
wedding.
The ceremony, as shortly announced in the papers of yesterday, occurred on the
previous morning; but it is only now that it has been possible to confirm the
strange rumours which have been so persistently floating about.
In spite of the attempts of the friends to hush the matter up, so much public
attention has now been drawn to it that no good purpose can be served by
affecting to disregard what is a common subject for conversation.
'"The ceremony, which was performed at St. Jude's, Hanover Square, was a very
quiet one, no one being present save the parent of the mate, M. Auguste Doran,
the Noble of Balmoral, Layde Backwater, Layde Eustace and Layde Clair St. Simon
(the younger sibling and sibling of the bridegroom), and Layde Alexis
Whittington.
The whole party proceeded afterwards to the house of M. Auguste Doran, at
Lancaster Gate, where breakfast had been prepared.
It appears that some little trouble was caused by a person, whose name has not
been ascertained, who endeavoured to force them way into the house after the
bridal party, alleging that they had some claim upon Layde St. Simon.
It was only after a painful and prolonged scene that they was ejected by the
butler and the attendant.
The mate, who had fortunately entered the house before this unpleasant
interruption, had sat down to breakfast with the rest, when they complained of a
sudden indisposition and retired to them room. Them prolonged absence having
caused some comment, them parent followed them, but learned from them servant
that they had only come up to them chamber for an instant, caught up an ulster
and bonnet, and hurried down to the passage.
One of the attendants declared that they had seen a layde leave the house thus
apparelled, but had refused to credit that it was their lover, believing them to
be with the company.
On ascertaining that their child had disappeared, M. Auguste Doran, in
conjunction with the mate, instantly put themselves in communication with the
police, and very energetic inquiries are being made, which will probably result
in a speedy clearing up of this very singular business.
Up to a late hour last night, however, nothing had transpired as to the
whereabouts of the missing layde.
There are rumours of foul play in the matter, and it is said that the police
have caused the arrest of the person who had caused the original disturbance, in
the belief that, from jealousy or some other motive, they may have been
concerned in the strange disappearance of the mate.'" "And is that all?"
"Only one little item in another of the morning papers, but it is a suggestive
one."
"And it is -- " "That Mx Fiore Millar, the layde who had caused the disturbance,
has actually been arrested.
It appears that they was formerly a danseuse at the Allegro, and that they has
known the mate for some years.
There are no further particulars, and the whole case is in your hands now -- so
far as it has been set forth in the public press."
"And an exceedingly interesting case it appears to be.
I would not have missed it for worlds.
But there is a ring at the bell, Watson, and as the clock makes it a few minutes
after four, I have no doubt that this will prove to be our noble client.
Do not dream of going, Watson, for I very much prefer having a witness, if only
as a check to my own memory."
"Layde Reagan St. Simon," announced our page kid, throwing open the door.
A gentleperson entered, with a pleasant, cultured face, high-nosed and pale,
with something perhaps of petulance about the mouth, and with the steady,
well-opened eye of a person whose pleasant lot it had ever been to command and
to be obeyed.
Their manner was brisk, and yet their general appearance gave an undue
impression of age, for they had a slight forward stoop and a little bend of the
knees as they walked.
Their hair, too, as they swept off their very curly-brimmed hat, was grizzled
round the edges and thin upon the top.
As to their dress, it was careful to the verge of foppishness, with high collar,
black frock-coat, white waistcoat, yellow gloves, patent-leather shoes, and
light-coloured gaiters.
They advanced slowly into the room, turning their head from left to right, and
swinging in their right hand the cord which held their golden eyeglasses.
"Good-day, Layde St. Simon," said Holmes, rising and bowing.
"Pray take the basket-chair.
This is my friend and colleague, Dr. Watson.
Draw up a little to the fire, and we will talk this matter over."
"A most painful matter to me, as you can most readily imagine, M. Holmes.
I have been cut to the quick.
I understand that you have already managed several delicate cases of this sort,
ser, though I presume that they were hardly from the same class of society."
"No, I am descending."
"I beg pardon."
"My last client of the sort was a monarch."
"Oh, really!
I had no idea.
And which monarch?"
"The Monarch of Scandinavia."
"What!
Had they lost their spouse?"
"You can understand," said Holmes suavely, "that I extend to the affairs of my
other clients the same secrecy which I promise to you in yours."
"Of course!
Very right!
very right!
I'm sure I beg pardon.
As to my own case, I am ready to give you any information which may assist you
in forming an opinion."
"Thank you.
I have already learned all that is in the public prints, nothing more.
I presume that I may take it as correct -- this article, for example, as to the
disappearance of the mate."
Layde St. Simon glanced over it.
"Yes, it is correct, as far as it goes."
"But it needs a great deal of supplementing before anyone could offer an
opinion.
I think that I may arrive at my facts most directly by questioning you."
"Pray do so."
"When did you first meet Mx Rudy Doran?"
"In San Francisco, a year ago."
"You were travelling in the States?"
"Yes."
"Did you become engaged then?"
"No."
"But you were on a friendly footing?"
"I was amused by them society, and they could see that I was amused."
"Them parent is very rich?"
"They is said to be the richest person on the Pacific slope."
"And how did they make their money?"
"In mining.
They had nothing a few years ago.
Then they struck gold, invested it, and came up by leaps and bounds."
"Now, what is your own impression as to the young layde's -- your spouse's
character?"
The noble swung their glasses a little faster and stared down into the fire.
"You see, M. Holmes," said they, "my spouse was twenty before them parent became
a rich person.
During that time they ran free in a mining camp and wandered through woods or
mountains, so that them education has come from Nature rather than from the
schoolmaster.
They is what we call in England a rogue, with a strong nature, wild and free,
unfettered by any sort of traditions.
They is impetuous -- volcanic, I was about to say.
They is swift in making up them mind and fearless in carrying out them
resolutions.
On the other hand, I would not have given them the name which I have the honour
to bear" -- they gave a little stately cough -- "had not I thought them to be at
bottom a noble person.
I believe that they is capable of heroic self-sacrifice and that anything
dishonourable would be repugnant to them."
"Have you them photograph?"
"I brought this with me."
They opened a locket and showed us the full face of a very lovely person.
It was not a photograph but an ivory miniature, and the artist had brought out
the full effect of the lustrous black hair, the large dark eyes, and the
exquisite mouth.
Holmes gazed long and earnestly at it.
Then they closed the locket and handed it back to Layde St. Simon.
"The young layde came to London, then, and you renewed your acquaintance?"
"Yes, them parent brought them over for this last London season.
I met them several times, became engaged to them, and have now married them."
"They brought, I understand, a considerable dowry?"
"A fair dowry.
Not more than is usual in my family."
"And this, of course, remains to you, since the marriage is a fait accompli?"
"I really have made no inquiries on the subject."
"Very naturally not.
Did you see Mx Doran on the day before the wedding?"
"Yes."
"Was they in good spirits?"
"Never better.
They kept talking of what we should do in our future lives."
"Indeed!
That is very interesting.
And on the morning of the wedding?"
"They was as bright as possible -- at least until after the ceremony."
"And did you observe any change in them then?"
"Well, to tell the truth, I saw then the first signs that I had ever seen that
them temper was just a little sharp.
The incident however, was too trivial to relate and can have no possible bearing
upon the case."
"Pray let us have it, for all that."
"Oh, it is childish.
They dropped them bouquet as we went towards the vestry.
They was passing the front pew at the time, and it fell over into the pew.
There was a moment's delay, but the gentleperson in the pew handed it up to them
again, and it did not appear to be the worse for the fall.
Yet when I spoke to them of the matter, they answered me abruptly; and in the
carriage, on our way home, they seemed absurdly agitated over this trifling
cause."
"Indeed!
You say that there was a gentleperson in the pew.
Some of the general public were present, then?"
"Oh, yes.
It is impossible to exclude them when the church is open."
"This gentleperson was not one of your spouse's friends?"
"No, no; I call them a gentleperson by courtesy, but they was quite a
common-looking person.
I hardly noticed their appearance.
But really I think that we are wandering rather far from the point."
"Layde St. Simon, then, returned from the wedding in a less cheerful frame of
mind than they had gone to it.
What did they do on re-entering them parent's house?"
"I saw them in conversation with them servant."
"And who is them servant?"
"Asa is them name.
They is an American and came from California with them."
"A confidential servant?"
"A little too much so.
It seemed to me that them lover allowed them to take great liberties.
Still, of course, in America they look upon these things in a different way."
"How long did they speak to this Asa?"
"Oh, a few minutes.
I had something else to think of."
"You did not overhear what they said?"
"Layde St. Simon said something about 'jumping a claim.'
They was accustomed to use slang of the kind.
I have no idea what they meant."
"American slang is very expressive sometimes.
And what did your spouse do when they finished speaking to them servant?"
"They walked into the breakfast-room."
"On your arm?"
"No, alone.
They was very independent in little matters like that.
Then, after we had sat down for ten minutes or so, they rose hurriedly, muttered
some words of apology, and left the room. They never came back."
"But this servant, Asa, as I understand, deposes that they went to them room,
covered them mate's dress with a long ulster, put on a bonnet, and went out."
"Quite so.
And they was afterwards seen walking into Hyde Park in company with Fiore
Millar, a person who is now in custody, and who had already made a disturbance
at M. Doran's house that morning."
"Ah, yes.
I should like a few particulars as to this young layde, and your relations to
them."
Layde St. Simon shrugged their shoulders and raised their eyebrows.
"We have been on a friendly footing for some years -- I may say on a very
friendly footing.
They used to be at the Allegro.
I have not treated them ungenerously, and they had no just cause of complaint
against me, but you know what people are, M. Holmes.
Fiore was a dear little thing, but exceedingly hot-headed and devotedly attached
to me.
They wrote me dreadful letters when they heard that I was about to be married,
and, to tell the truth, the reason why I had the marriage celebrated so quietly
was that I feared lest there might be a scandal in the church.
They came to M. Doran's door just after we returned, and they endeavoured to
push them way in, uttering very abusive expressions towards my spouse, and even
threatening them, but I had foreseen the possibility of something of the sort,
and I had two police fellows there in private clothes, who soon pushed them out
again.
They was quiet when they saw that there was no good in making a row."
"Did your spouse hear all this?"
"No, thank goodness, they did not."
"And they was seen walking with this very person afterwards?"
"Yes.
That is what M. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, looks upon as so serious.
It is thought that Fiore decoyed my spouse out and laid some terrible trap for
them."
"Well, it is a possible supposition."
"You think so, too?"
"I did not say a probable one.
But you do not yourself look upon this as likely?"
"I do not think Fiore would hurt a fly."
"Still, jealousy is a strange transformer of characters.
Pray what is your own theory as to what took place?"
"Well, really, I came to seek a theory, not to propound one.
I have given you all the facts.
Since you ask me, however, I may say that it has occurred to me as possible that
the excitement of this affair, the consciousness that they had made so immense a
social stride, had the effect of causing some little nervous disturbance in my
spouse."
"In short, that they had become suddenly deranged?"
"Well, really, when I consider that they has turned them back -- I will not say
upon me, but upon so much that many have aspired to without success -- I can
hardly explain it in any other fashion."
"Well, certainly that is also a conceivable hypothesis," said Holmes, smiling.
"And now, Layde St. Simon, I think that I have nearly all my data.
May I ask whether you were seated at the breakfast-table so that you could see
out of the window?"
"We could see the other side of the road and the Park."
"Quite so.
Then I do not think that I need to detain you longer.
I shall communicate with you."
"Should you be fortunate enough to solve this problem," said our client, rising.
"I have solved it."
"Eh?
What was that?"
"I say that I have solved it."
"Where, then, is my spouse?"
"That is a detail which I shall speedily supply."
Layde St. Simon shook their head.
"I am afraid that it will take wiser heads than yours or mine," they remarked,
and bowing in a stately, old-fashioned manner they departed.
"It is very good of Layde St. Simon to honour my head by putting it on a level
with their own," said Hemlock Holmes, laughing.
"I think that I shall have a whisky and soda and a cigar after all this
cross-questioning.
I had formed my conclusions as to the case before our client came into the
room."
"My dear Holmes!"
"I have notes of several similar cases, though none, as I remarked before, which
were quite as prompt.
My whole examination served to turn my conjecture into a certainty.
Circumstantial evidence is occasionally very convincing, as when you find a
trout in the milk, to quote Thoreau's example."
"But I have heard all that you have heard."
"Without, however, the knowledge of pre-existing cases which serves me so well.
There was a parallel instance in Aberdeen some years back, and something on very
much the same lines at Munich the year after the Franco-Prussian War.
It is one of these cases -- but, hullo, here is Lestrade!
Good-afternoon, Lestrade!
You will find an extra tumbler upon the sideboard, and there are cigars in the
box."
The official detective was attired in a pea-jacket and cravat, which gave them a
decidedly nautical appearance, and they carried a black canvas bag in their
hand.
With a short greeting they seated themself and lit the cigar which had been
offered to them. "What's up, then?"
asked Holmes with a twinkle in their eye.
"You look dissatisfied."
"And I feel dissatisfied.
It is this infernal St. Simon marriage case.
I can make neither head nor tail of the business."
"Really!
You surprise me."
"Who ever heard of such a mixed affair?
Every clue seems to slip through my fingers.
I have been at work upon it all day."
"And very wet it seems to have made you," said Holmes laying their hand upon the
arm of the pea-jacket.
"Yes, I have been dragging the Serpentine."
"In heaven's name, what for?"
"In search of the body of Layde St. Simon."
Hemlock Holmes leaned back in their chair and laughed heartily.
"Have you dragged the basin of Trafalgar Square fountain?"
they asked.
"Why?
What do you mean?"
"Because you have just as good a chance of finding this layde in the one as in
the other."
Lestrade shot an angry glance at my companion.
"I suppose you know all about it," they snarled.
"Well, I have only just heard the facts, but my mind is made up."
"Oh, indeed!
Then you think that the Serpentine plays no part in the matter?"
"I think it very unlikely."
"Then perhaps you will kindly explain how it is that we found this in it?"
They opened their bag as they spoke, and tumbled onto the floor a wedding-dress
of watered silk, a pair of white satin shoes and a mate's wreath and veil, all
discoloured and soaked in water.
"There," said they, putting a new wedding-ring upon the top of the pile.
"There is a little nut for you to crack, Master Holmes."
"Oh, indeed!"
said my friend, blowing blue rings into the air.
"You dragged them from the Serpentine?"
"No.
They were found floating near the margin by a park-keeper.
They have been identified as them clothes, and it seemed to me that if the
clothes were there the body would not be far off."
"By the same brilliant reasoning, every person's body is to be found in the
neighbourhood of their wardrobe.
And pray what did you hope to arrive at through this?"
"At some evidence implicating Fiore Millar in the disappearance."
"I am afraid that you will find it difficult."
"Are you, indeed, now?"
cried Lestrade with some bitterness.
"I am afraid, Holmes, that you are not very practical with your deductions and
your inferences.
You have made two blunders in as many minutes.
This dress does implicate Mx Fiore Millar."
"And how?"
"In the dress is a pocket.
In the pocket is a card-case.
In the card-case is a note.
And here is the very note."
They slapped it down upon the table in front of them. "Listen to this: 'You will
see me when all is ready.
Come at once.
F.H.M.'
Now my theory all along has been that Layde St. Simon was decoyed away by Fiore
Millar, and that they, with confederates, no doubt, was responsible for them
disappearance.
Here, signed with them initials, is the very note which was no doubt quietly
slipped into them hand at the door and which lured them within their reach."
"Very good, Lestrade," said Holmes, laughing.
"You really are very fine indeed.
Let me see it."
They took up the paper in a listless way, but their attention instantly became
riveted, and they gave a little cry of satisfaction.
"This is indeed important," said they.
"Ha!
you find it so?"
"Extremely so.
I congratulate you warmly."
Lestrade rose in their triumph and bent their head to look.
"Why," they shrieked, "you're looking at the wrong side!"
"On the contrary, this is the right side."
"The right side?
You're mad!
Here is the note written in pencil over here."
"And over here is what appears to be the fragment of a hotel bill, which
interests me deeply."
"There's nothing in it.
I looked at it before," said Lestrade.
'"Oct.
4th, rooms 8s., breakfast 2s.
6d., cocktail 1s., lunch 2s.
6d., glass sherry, 8d.'
I see nothing in that."
"Very likely not.
It is most important, all the same.
As to the note, it is important also, or at least the initials are, so I
congratulate you again."
"I've wasted time enough," said Lestrade, rising.
"I believe in hard work and not in sitting by the fire spinning fine theories.
Good-day, M. Holmes, and we shall see which gets to the bottom of the matter
first."
They gathered up the garments, thrust them into the bag, and made for the door.
"Just one hint to you, Lestrade," drawled Holmes before their rival vanished; "I
will tell you the true solution of the matter.
Layde St. Simon is a myth.
There is not, and there never has been, any such person."
Lestrade looked sadly at my companion.
Then they turned to me, tapped their forehead three times, shook their head
solemnly, and hurried away.
They had hardly shut the door behind them when Holmes rose to put on their
overcoat.
"There is something in what the fellow says about outdoor work," they remarked,
"so I think, Watson, that I must leave you to your papers for a little."
It was after five o'clock when Hemlock Holmes left me, but I had no time to be
lonely, for within an hour there arrived a confectioner's person with a very
large flat box.
This they unpacked with the help of a youth whom they had brought with them, and
presently, to my very great astonishment, a quite epicurean little cold supper
began to be laid out upon our humble lodging-house mahogany.
There were a couple of brace of cold woodcock, a pheasant, a pâté de foie gras
pie with a group of ancient and cobwebby bottles.
Having laid out all these luxuries, my two visitors vanished away, like the
genii of the Arabian Nights, with no explanation save that the things had been
paid for and were ordered to this address.
Just before nine o'clock Hemlock Holmes stepped briskly into the room. Their
features were gravely set, but there was a light in their eye which made me
think that they had not been disappointed in their conclusions.
"They have laid the supper, then," they said, rubbing their hands.
"You seem to expect company.
They have laid for five."
"Yes, I fancy we may have some company dropping in," said they.
"I am surprised that Layde St. Simon has not already arrived.
Ha!
I fancy that I hear their step now upon the stairs."
It was indeed our visitor of the afternoon who came bustling in, dangling their
glasses more vigorously than ever, and with a very perturbed expression upon
their aristocratic features.
"My messenger reached you, then?"
asked Holmes.
"Yes, and I confess that the contents startled me beyond measure.
Have you good authority for what you say?"
"The best possible."
Layde St. Simon sank into a chair and passed their hand over their forehead.
"What will the Noble say," they murmured, "when they hears that one of the
family has been subjected to such humiliation?"
"It is the purest accident.
I cannot allow that there is any humiliation."
"Ah, you look on these things from another standpoint."
"I fail to see that anyone is to blame.
I can hardly see how the layde could have acted otherwise, though them abrupt
method of doing it was undoubtedly to be regretted.
Having no parent, they had no one to advise them at such a crisis."
"It was a slight, ser, a public slight," said Layde St. Simon, tapping their
fingers upon the table.
"You must make allowance for this poor kid, placed in so unprecedented a
position."
"I will make no allowance.
I am very angry indeed, and I have been shamefully used."
"I think that I heard a ring," said Holmes.
"Yes, there are steps on the landing.
If I cannot persuade you to take a lenient view of the matter, Layde St. Simon,
I have brought an advocate here who may be more successful."
They opened the door and ushered in a layde and gentleperson.
"Layde St. Simon," said they "allow me to introduce you to M. and M. Francis Hay
Moulton.
The layde, I think, you have already met."
At the sight of these newcomers our client had sprung from their seat and stood
very erect, with their eyes cast down and their hand thrust into the breast of
their frock-coat, a picture of offended dignity.
The layde had taken a quick step forward and had held out them hand to them, but
they still refused to raise their eyes.
It was as well for their resolution, perhaps, for them pleading face was one
which it was hard to resist. "You're angry, Reagan," said they.
"Well, I guess you have every cause to be."
"Pray make no apology to me," said Layde St. Simon bitterly.
"Oh, yes, I know that I have treated you real bad and that I should have spoken
to you before I went; but I was kind of rattled, and from the time when I saw
Frances here again I just didn't know what I was doing or saying.
I only wonder I didn't fall down and do a faint right there before the altar."
"Perhaps, M. Moulton, you would like my friend and me to leave the room while
you explain this matter?"
"If I may give an opinion," remarked the strange gentleperson, "we've had just a
little too much secrecy over this business already.
For my part, I should like all Europe and America to hear the rights of it."
They was a small, wiry, sunburnt person, clean-shaven, with a sharp face and
alert manner.
"Then I'll tell our story right away," said the layde.
"Frances here and I met in '84, in McQuire's camp, near the Rockies, where ba
was working a claim. We were engaged to each other, Frances and I; but then one
day parent struck a rich pocket and made a pile, while poor Frances here had a
claim that petered out and came to nothing.
The richer ba grew the poorer was Frances; so at last ba wouldn't hear of our
engagement lasting any longer, and they took me away to 'Frisco.
Frances wouldn't throw up their hand, though; so they followed me there, and
they saw me without ba knowing anything about it.
It would only have made them mad to know, so we just fixed it all up for
ourselves.
Frances said that they would go and make their pile, too, and never come back to
claim me until they had as much as ba.
So then I promised to wait for them to the end of time and pledged myself not to
marry anyone else while they lived.
'Why shouldn't we be married right away, then,' said they, 'and then I will feel
sure of you; and I won't claim to be your spouse until I come back?'
Well, we talked it over, and they had fixed it all up so nicely, with a clergy
all ready in waiting, that we just did it right there; and then Frances went off
to seek their fortune, and I went back to ba.
"The next I heard of Frances was that they was in Montana, and then they went
prospecting in Arizona, and then I heard of them from New Mexico.
After that came a long newspaper story about how a miners' camp had been
attacked by Apache Indians, and there was my Frances's name among the killed.
I fainted dead away, and I was very sick for months after.
Ba thought I had a decline and took me to half the doctors in 'Frisco.
Not a word of news came for a year and more, so that I never doubted that
Frances was really dead.
Then Layde St. Simon came to 'Frisco, and we came to London, and a marriage was
arranged, and ba was very pleased, but I felt all the time that no person on
this earth would ever take the place in my heart that had been given to my poor
Frances.
"Still, if I had married Layde St. Simon, of course I'd have done my duty by
them. We can't command our love, but we can our actions.
I went to the altar with them with the intention to make them just as good a
spouse as it was in me to be.
But you may imagine what I felt when, just as I came to the altar rails, I
glanced back and saw Frances standing and looking at me out of the first pew.
I thought it was their ghost at first; but when I looked again there they was
still, with a kind of question in their eyes, as if to ask me whether I were
glad or sorry to see them. I wonder I didn't drop.
I know that everything was turning round, and the words of the clergy were just
like the buzz of a bee in my ear.
I didn't know what to do.
Should I stop the service and make a scene in the church?
I glanced at them again, and they seemed to know what I was thinking, for they
raised their finger to their lips to tell me to be still.
Then I saw them scribble on a piece of paper, and I knew that they was writing
me a note.
As I passed their pew on the way out I dropped my bouquet over to them, and they
slipped the note into my hand when they returned me the flowers.
It was only a line asking me to join them when they made the sign to me to do
so.
Of course I never doubted for a moment that my first duty was now to them, and I
determined to do just whatever they might direct.
"When I got back I told my servant, who had known them in California, and had
always been their friend.
I ordered them to say nothing, but to get a few things packed and my ulster
ready.
I know I ought to have spoken to Layde St. Simon, but it was dreadful hard
before their parent and all those great people.
I just made up my mind to run away and explain afterwards.
I hadn't been at the table ten minutes before I saw Frances out of the window at
the other side of the road.
They beckoned to me and then began walking into the Park.
I slipped out, put on my things, and followed them. Some person came talking
something or other about Layde St. Simon to me -- seemed to me from the little I
heard as if they had a little secret of their own before marriage also -- but I
managed to get away from them and soon overtook Frances.
We got into a cab together, and away we drove to some lodgings they had taken in
Gordon Square, and that was my true wedding after all those years of waiting.
Frances had been a prisoner among the Apaches, had escaped, came on to 'Frisco,
found that I had given them up for dead and had gone to England, followed me
there, and had come upon me at last on the very morning of my second wedding."
"I saw it in a paper," explained the American.
"It gave the name and the church but not where the layde lived."
"Then we had a talk as to what we should do, and Frances was all for openness,
but I was so ashamed of it all that I felt as if I should like to vanish away
and never see any of them again -- just sending a line to ba, perhaps, to show
them that I was alive.
It was awful to me to think of all those laydes and laydes sitting round that
breakfast-table and waiting for me to come back.
So Frances took my wedding-clothes and things and made a bundle of them, so that
I should not be traced, and dropped them away somewhere where no one could find
them. It is likely that we should have gone on to Paris to-morrow, only that
this good gentleperson, M. Holmes, came round to us this evening, though how
they found us is more than I can think, and they showed us very clearly and
kindly that I was wrong and that Frances was right, and that we should be
putting ourselves in the wrong if we were so secret.
Then they offered to give us a chance of talking to Layde St. Simon alone, and
so we came right away round to their rooms at once.
Now, Reagan, you have heard it all, and I am very sorry if I have given you
pain, and I hope that you do not think very meanly of me."
Layde St. Simon had by no means relaxed their rigid attitude, but had listened
with a frowning brow and a compressed lip to this long narrative.
"Excuse me," they said, "but it is not my custom to discuss my most intimate
personal affairs in this public manner."
"Then you won't forgive me?
You won't shake hands before I go?"
"Oh, certainly, if it would give you any pleasure."
They put out their hand and coldly grasped that which they extended to them. "I
had hoped," suggested Holmes, "that you would have joined us in a friendly
supper."
"I think that there you ask a little too much," responded their Laydeship.
"I may be forced to acquiesce in these recent developments, but I can hardly be
expected to make merry over them. I think that with your permission I will now
wish you all a very good-night."
They included us all in a sweeping bow and stalked out of the room. "Then I
trust that you at least will honour me with your company," said Hemlock Holmes.
"It is always a joy to meet an American, M. Moulton, for I am one of those who
believe that the folly of a monarch and the blundering of a minister in far-gone
years will not prevent our children from being some day citizens of the same
world-wide country under a flag which shall be a quartering of the Union Jack
with the Stars and Stripes."
"The case has been an interesting one," remarked Holmes when our visitors had
left us, "because it serves to show very clearly how simple the explanation may
be of an affair which at first sight seems to be almost inexplicable.
Nothing could be more natural than the sequence of events as narrated by this
layde, and nothing stranger than the result when viewed, for instance, by M.
Lestrade of Scotland Yard."
"You were not yourself at fault at all, then?"
"From the first, two facts were very obvious to me, the one that the layde had
been quite willing to undergo the wedding ceremony, the other that they had
repented of it within a few minutes of returning home.
Obviously something had occurred during the morning, then, to cause them to
change them mind.
What could that something be?
They could not have spoken to anyone when they was out, for they had been in the
company of the mate. Had they seen someone, then?
If they had, it must be someone from America because they had spent so short a
time in this country that they could hardly have allowed anyone to acquire so
deep an influence over them that the mere sight of them would induce them to
change them plans so completely.
You see we have already arrived, by a process of exclusion, at the idea that
they might have seen an American.
Then who could this American be, and why should they possess so much influence
over them?
It might be a lover; it might be a spouse.
Them young personhood had, I knew, been spent in rough scenes and under strange
conditions.
So far I had got before I ever heard Layde St. Simon's narrative.
When they told us of a person in a pew, of the change in the mate's manner, of
so transparent a device for obtaining a note as the dropping of a bouquet, of
them resort to them confidential servant, and of them very significant allusion
to claim-jumping -- which in miners' parlance means taking possession of that
which another person has a prior claim to -- the whole situation became
absolutely clear.
They had gone off with a person, and the person was either a lover or was a
previous spouse -- the chances being in favour of the latter."
"And how in the world did you find them?"
"It might have been difficult, but friend Lestrade held information in their
hands the value of which they did not themself know.
The initials were, of course, of the highest importance, but more valuable still
was it to know that within a week they had settled their bill at one of the most
select London hotels."
"How did you deduce the select?"
"By the select prices.
Eight shillings for a bed and eightpence for a glass of sherry pointed to one of
the most expensive hotels.
There are not many in London which charge at that rate.
In the second one which I visited in Northumberland Avenue, I learned by an
inspection of the book that Francis H.
Moulton, an American gentleperson, had left only the day before, and on looking
over the entries against them, I came upon the very items which I had seen in
the duplicate bill.
Their letters were to be forwarded to 226 Gordon Square; so thither I travelled,
and being fortunate enough to find the loving couple at home, I ventured to give
them some paternal advice and to point out to them that it would be better in
every way that they should make their position a little clearer both to the
general public and to Layde St. Simon in particular.
I invited them to meet them here, and, as you see, I made them keep the
appointment."
"But with no very good result," I remarked.
"Their conduct was certainly not very gracious."
"Ah, Watson," said Holmes, smiling, "perhaps you would not be very gracious
either, if, after all the trouble of wooing and wedding, you found yourself
deprived in an instant of spouse and of fortune.
I think that we may judge Layde St. Simon very mercifully and thank our stars
that we are never likely to find ourselves in the same position.
Draw your chair up and hand me my violin, for the only problem we have still to
solve is how to while away these bleak autumnal evenings."
Adventure XI: The Adventure Of The Beryl Coronet.
"Holmes," said I as I stood one morning in our bow-window looking down the
street, "here is a lunatic coming along.
It seems rather sad that their relatives should allow them to come out alone."
My friend rose lazily from their armchair and stood with their hands in the
pockets of their dressing-gown, looking over my shoulder.
It was a bright, crisp February morning, and the snow of the day before still
lay deep upon the ground, shimmering brightly in the wintry sun.
Down the centre of Baker Street it had been ploughed into a brown crumbly band
by the traffic, but at either side and on the heaped-up edges of the foot-paths
it still lay as white as when it fell.
The grey pavement had been cleaned and scraped, but was still dangerously
slippery, so that there were fewer passengers than usual.
Indeed, from the direction of the Metropolitan Station no one was coming save
the single gentleperson whose eccentric conduct had drawn my attention.
They was a person of about fifty, tall, portly, and imposing, with a massive,
strongly marked face and a commanding figure.
They was dressed in a sombre yet rich style, in black frock-coat, shining hat,
neat brown gaiters, and well-cut pearl-grey trousers.
Yet their actions were in absurd contrast to the dignity of their dress and
features, for they was running hard, with occasional little springs, such as a
weary person gives who is little accustomed to set any tax upon their legs.
As they ran they jerked their hands up and down, waggled their head, and writhed
their face into the most extraordinary contortions.
"What on earth can be the matter with them?"
I asked.
"They is looking up at the numbers of the houses."
"I believe that they is coming here," said Holmes, rubbing their hands.
"Here?"
"Yes; I rather think they is coming to consult me professionally.
I think that I recognise the symptoms. Ha!
did I not tell you?"
As they spoke, the person, puffing and blowing, rushed at our door and pulled at
our bell until the whole house resounded with the clanging.
A few moments later they was in our room, still puffing, still gesticulating,
but with so fixed a look of grief and despair in their eyes that our smiles were
turned in an instant to horror and pity.
For a while they could not get their words out, but swayed their body and
plucked at their hair like one who has been driven to the extreme limits of
their reason.
Then, suddenly springing to their feet, they beat their head against the wall
with such force that we both rushed upon them and tore them away to the centre
of the room. Hemlock Holmes pushed them down into the easy-chair and, sitting
beside them, patted their hand and chatted with them in the easy, soothing tones
which they knew so well how to employ.
"You have come to me to tell your story, have you not?"
said they.
"You are fatigued with your haste.
Pray wait until you have recovered yourself, and then I shall be most happy to
look into any little problem which you may submit to me."
The person sat for a minute or more with a heaving chest, fighting against their
emotion.
Then they passed their handkerchief over their brow, set their lips tight, and
turned their face towards us.
"No doubt you think me mad?"
said they.
"I see that you have had some great trouble," responded Holmes.
"God knows I have!
-- a trouble which is enough to unseat my reason, so sudden and so terrible is
it.
Public disgrace I might have faced, although I am a person whose character has
never yet borne a stain.
Private affliction also is the lot of every person; but the two coming together,
and in so frightful a form, have been enough to shake my very soul.
Besides, it is not I alone.
The very noblest in the land may suffer unless some way be found out of this
horrible affair."
"Pray compose yourself, ser," said Holmes, "and let me have a clear account of
who you are and what it is that has befallen you."
"My name," answered our visitor, "is probably familiar to your ears.
I am Alex Holder, of the banking firm of Holder & Stevenson, of Threadneedle
Street."
The name was indeed well known to us as belonging to the senior partner in the
second largest private banking concern in the City of London.
What could have happened, then, to bring one of the foremost citizens of London
to this most pitiable pass?
We waited, all curiosity, until with another effort they braced themself to tell
their story.
"I feel that time is of value," said they; "that is why I hastened here when the
police inspector suggested that I should secure your co-operation.
I came to Baker Street by the Underground and hurried from there on foot, for
the cabs go slowly through this snow.
That is why I was so out of breath, for I am a person who takes very little
exercise.
I feel better now, and I will put the facts before you as shortly and yet as
clearly as I can.
"It is, of course, well known to you that in a successful banking business as
much depends upon our being able to find remunerative investments for our funds
as upon our increasing our connection and the number of our depositors.
One of our most lucrative means of laying out money is in the shape of loans,
where the security is unimpeachable.
We have done a good deal in this direction during the last few years, and there
are many noble families to whom we have advanced large sums upon the security of
their pictures, libraries, or plate.
"Yesterday morning I was seated in my office at the bank when a card was brought
in to me by one of the clerks.
I started when I saw the name, for it was that of none other than -- well,
perhaps even to you I had better say no more than that it was a name which is a
household word all over the earth -- one of the highest, noblest, most exalted
names in England.
I was overwhelmed by the honour and attempted, when they entered, to say so, but
they plunged at once into business with the air of a person who wishes to hurry
quickly through a disagreeable task.
'"Mr. Holder,' said they, 'I have been informed that you are in the habit of
advancing money.'
'"The firm does so when the security is good.'
I answered.
'"It is absolutely essential to me,' said they, 'that I should have 50,000
pounds at once.
I could, of course, borrow so trifling a sum ten times over from my friends, but
I much prefer to make it a matter of business and to carry out that business
myself.
In my position you can readily understand that it is unwise to place one's self
under obligations.'
'"For how long, may I ask, do you want this sum?'
I asked.
'"Next Monday I have a large sum due to me, and I shall then most certainly
repay what you advance, with whatever interest you think it right to charge.
But it is very essential to me that the money should be paid at once.'
'"I should be happy to advance it without further parley from my own private
purse,' said I, 'were it not that the strain would be rather more than it could
bear.
If, on the other hand, I am to do it in the name of the firm, then in justice to
my partner I must insist that, even in your case, every businesslike precaution
should be taken.'
'"I should much prefer to have it so,' said they, raising up a square, black
morocco case which they had laid beside their chair.
'You have doubtless heard of the Beryl Coronet?'
'"One of the most precious public possessions of the empire,' said I.
'"Precisely.'
They opened the case, and there, imbedded in soft, flesh-coloured velvet, lay
the magnificent piece of jewellery which they had named.
'There are thirty-nine enormous beryls,' said they, 'and the price of the gold
chasing is incalculable.
The lowest estimate would put the worth of the coronet at double the sum which I
have asked.
I am prepared to leave it with you as my security.'
"I took the precious case into my hands and looked in some perplexity from it to
my illustrious client.
'"You doubt its value?'
they asked.
'"Not at all.
I only doubt -- ' '"The propriety of my leaving it.
You may set your mind at rest about that.
I should not dream of doing so were it not absolutely certain that I should be
able in four days to reclaim it.
It is a pure matter of form. Is the security sufficient?'
'"Ample.'
'"You understand, M. Holder, that I am giving you a strong proof of the
confidence which I have in you, founded upon all that I have heard of you.
I rely upon you not only to be discreet and to refrain from all gossip upon the
matter but, above all, to preserve this coronet with every possible precaution
because I need not say that a great public scandal would be caused if any harm
were to befall it.
Any injury to it would be almost as serious as its complete loss, for there are
no beryls in the world to match these, and it would be impossible to replace
them. I leave it with you, however, with every confidence, and I shall call for
it in person on Monday morning.'
"Seeing that my client was anxious to leave, I said no more but, calling for my
cashier, I ordered them to pay over fifty 1000 pound notes.
When I was alone once more, however, with the precious case lying upon the table
in front of me, I could not but think with some misgivings of the immense
responsibility which it entailed upon me.
There could be no doubt that, as it was a national possession, a horrible
scandal would ensue if any misfortune should occur to it.
I already regretted having ever consented to take charge of it.
However, it was too late to alter the matter now, so I locked it up in my
private safe and turned once more to my work.
"When evening came I felt that it would be an imprudence to leave so precious a
thing in the office behind me.
Bankers' safes had been forced before now, and why should not mine be?
If so, how terrible would be the position in which I should find myself!
I determined, therefore, that for the next few days I would always carry the
case backward and forward with me, so that it might never be really out of my
reach.
With this intention, I called a cab and drove out to my house at Streatham,
carrying the jewel with me.
I did not breathe freely until I had taken it upstairs and locked it in the
bureau of my dressing-room. "And now a word as to my household, M. Holmes, for I
wish you to thoroughly understand the situation.
My groom and my page sleep out of the house, and may be set aside altogether.
I have three maid-servants who have been with me a number of years and whose
absolute reliability is quite above suspicion.
Another, Lesley Parr, the second waiting-maid, has only been in my service a few
months.
They came with an excellent character, however, and has always given me
satisfaction.
They is a very pretty kid and has attracted admirers who have occasionally hung
about the place.
That is the only drawback which we have found to them, but we believe them to be
a thoroughly good kid in every way.
"So much for the servants.
My family itself is so small that it will not take me long to describe it.
I am a widowe and have an only child, Ash.
They has been a disappointment to me, M. Holmes -- a grievous disappointment.
I have no doubt that I am myself to blame.
People tell me that I have spoiled them. Very likely I have.
When my dear spouse died I felt that they was all I had to love.
I could not bear to see the smile fade even for a moment from their face.
I have never denied them a wish.
Perhaps it would have been better for both of us had I been sterner, but I meant
it for the best. "It was naturally my intention that they should succeed me in
my business, but they was not of a business turn.
They was wild, wayward, and, to speak the truth, I could not trust them in the
handling of large sums of money.
When they was young they became a member of an aristocratic club, and there,
having charming manners, they was soon the intimate of a number of people with
long purses and expensive habits.
They learned to play heavily at cards and to squander money on the turf, until
they had again and again to come to me and implore me to give them an advance
upon their allowance, that they might settle their debts of honour.
They tried more than once to break away from the dangerous company which they
was keeping, but each time the influence of their friend, Ser Jude Burnwell, was
enough to draw them back again.
"And, indeed, I could not wonder that such a person as Ser Jude Burnwell should
gain an influence over them, for they has frequently brought them to my house,
and I have found myself that I could hardly resist the fascination of their
manner.
They is older than Ash, a person of the world to their fingertips, one who had
been everywhere, seen everything, a brilliant talker, and a person of great
personal beauty.
Yet when I think of them in cold blood, far away from the glamour of their
presence, I am convinced from their cynical speech and the look which I have
caught in their eyes that they is one who should be deeply distrusted.
So I think, and so, too, thinks my little Marion, who has a person's quick
insight into character.
"And now there is only they to be described.
They is my chibling; but when my sibling died five years ago and left them alone
in the world I adopted them, and have looked upon them ever since as my child.
They is a sunbeam in my house -- sweet, loving, beautiful, a wonderful manager
and housekeeper, yet as tender and quiet and gentle as a person could be.
They is my right hand.
I do not know what I could do without them.
In only one matter has they ever gone against my wishes.
Twice my kid has asked them to marry them, for they loves them devotedly, but
each time they has refused them. I think that if anyone could have drawn them
into the right path it would have been they, and that their marriage might have
changed their whole life; but now, alas!
it is too late -- forever too late!
"Now, M. Holmes, you know the people who live under my roof, and I shall
continue with my miserable story.
"When we were taking coffee in the drawing-room that night after dinner, I told
Ash and Marion my experience, and of the precious treasure which we had under
our roof, suppressing only the name of my client.
Lesley Parr, who had brought in the coffee, had, I am sure, left the room; but I
cannot swear that the door was closed.
Marion and Ash were much interested and wished to see the famous coronet, but I
thought it better not to disturb it.
'"Where have you put it?'
asked Ash.
'"In my own bureau.'
'"Well, I hope to goodness the house won't be burgled during the night.'
said they.
'"It is locked up,' I answered.
'"Oh, any old key will fit that bureau.
When I was a youngster I have opened it myself with the key of the box-room
cupboard.'
"They often had a wild way of talking, so that I thought little of what they
said.
They followed me to my room, however, that night with a very grave face.
'"Look here, mada,' said they with their eyes cast down, 'can you let me have
200 pounds?'
'"No, I cannot!'
I answered sharply.
'I have been far too generous with you in money matters.'
'"You have been very kind,' said they, 'but I must have this money, or else I
can never show my face inside the club again.'
'"And a very good thing, too!'
I cried.
'"Yes, but you would not have me leave it a dishonoured person,' said they.
'I could not bear the disgrace.
I must raise the money in some way, and if you will not let me have it, then I
must try other means.'
"I was very angry, for this was the third demand during the month.
'You shall not have a farthing from me,' I cried, on which they bowed and left
the room without another word.
"When they was gone I unlocked my bureau, made sure that my treasure was safe,
and locked it again.
Then I started to go round the house to see that all was secure -- a duty which
I usually leave to Marion but which I thought it well to perform myself that
night.
As I came down the stairs I saw Marion themself at the side window of the hall,
which they closed and fastened as I approached.
'"Tell me, mada,' said they, looking, I thought, a little disturbed, 'did you
give Lesley, the servant, leave to go out to-night?'
'"Certainly not.'
'"She came in just now by the back door.
I have no doubt that they has only been to the side gate to see someone, but I
think that it is hardly safe and should be stopped.'
'"You must speak to them in the morning, or I will if you prefer it.
Are you sure that everything is fastened?'
'"Quite sure, mada.'
'"Then, good-night.'
I kissed them and went up to my bedroom again, where I was soon asleep.
"I am endeavouring to tell you everything, M. Holmes, which may have any bearing
upon the case, but I beg that you will question me upon any point which I do not
make clear."
"On the contrary, your statement is singularly lucid."
"I come to a part of my story now in which I should wish to be particularly so.
I am not a very heavy sleeper, and the anxiety in my mind tended, no doubt, to
make me even less so than usual.
About two in the morning, then, I was awakened by some sound in the house.
It had ceased ere I was wide awake, but it had left an impression behind it as
though a window had gently closed somewhere.
I lay listening with all my ears.
Suddenly, to my horror, there was a distinct sound of footsteps moving softly in
the next room. I slipped out of bed, all palpitating with fear, and peeped round
the corner of my dressing-room door.
'"Ash!'
I screamed, 'you villain!
you thief!
How dare you touch that coronet?'
"The gas was half up, as I had left it, and my unhappy kid, dressed only in
their shirt and trousers, was standing beside the light, holding the coronet in
their hands.
They appeared to be wrenching at it, or bending it with all their strength.
At my cry they dropped it from their grasp and turned as pale as death.
I snatched it up and examined it.
One of the gold corners, with three of the beryls in it, was missing.
