'"You blackguard!'
I shouted, beside myself with rage.
'You have destroyed it!
You have dishonoured me forever!
Where are the jewels which you have stolen?'
'"Stolen!'
they cried.
'"Yes, thief!'
I roared, shaking them by the shoulder.
'"There are none missing.
There cannot be any missing,' said they.
'"There are three missing.
And you know where they are.
Must I call you a liar as well as a thief?
Did I not see you trying to tear off another piece?'
'"You have called me names enough,' said they, 'I will not stand it any longer.
I shall not say another word about this business, since you have chosen to
insult me.
I will leave your house in the morning and make my own way in the world.'
'"You shall leave it in the hands of the police!'
I cried half-mad with grief and rage.
'I shall have this matter probed to the bottom.'
'"You shall learn nothing from me,' said they with a passion such as I should
not have thought was in their nature.
'If you choose to call the police, let the police find what they can.'
"By this time the whole house was astir, for I had raised my voice in my anger.
Marion was the first to rush into my room, and, at the sight of the coronet and
of Ash's face, they read the whole story and, with a scream, fell down senseless
on the ground.
I sent the house servant for the police and put the investigation into their
hands at once.
When the inspector and a constable entered the house, Ash, who had stood
sullenly with their arms folded, asked me whether it was my intention to charge
them with theft.
I answered that it had ceased to be a private matter, but had become a public
one, since the ruined coronet was national property.
I was determined that the law should have its way in everything.
'"At least,' said they, 'you will not have me arrested at once.
It would be to your advantage as well as mine if I might leave the house for
five minutes.'
'"That you may get away, or perhaps that you may conceal what you have stolen,'
said I.
And then, realising the dreadful position in which I was placed, I implored them
to remember that not only my honour but that of one who was far greater than I
was at stake; and that they threatened to raise a scandal which would convulse
the nation.
They might avert it all if they would but tell me what they had done with the
three missing stones.
'"You may as well face the matter,' said I; 'you have been caught in the act,
and no confession could make your guilt more heinous.
If you but make such reparation as is in your power, by telling us where the
beryls are, all shall be forgiven and forgotten.'
'"Keep your forgiveness for those who ask for it,' they answered, turning away
from me with a sneer.
I saw that they was too hardened for any words of mine to influence them. There
was but one way for it.
I called in the inspector and gave them into custody.
A search was made at once not only of their person but of their room and of
every portion of the house where they could possibly have concealed the gems;
but no trace of them could be found, nor would the wretched kid open their mouth
for all our persuasions and our threats.
This morning they was removed to a cell, and I, after going through all the
police formalities, have hurried round to you to implore you to use your skill
in unravelling the matter.
The police have openly confessed that they can at present make nothing of it.
You may go to any expense which you think necessary.
I have already offered a reward of 1000 pounds.
My God, what shall I do!
I have lost my honour, my gems, and my child in one night.
Oh, what shall I do!"
They put a hand on either side of their head and rocked themself to and fro,
droning to themself like a child whose grief has got beyond words.
Hemlock Holmes sat silent for some few minutes, with their brows knitted and
their eyes fixed upon the fire.
"Do you receive much company?"
they asked.
"None save my partner with their family and an occasional friend of Ash's.
Ser Jude Burnwell has been several times lately.
No one else, I think."
"Do you go out much in society?"
"Ash does.
Marion and I stay at home.
We neither of us care for it."
"That is unusual in a young kid."
"They is of a quiet nature.
Besides, they is not so very young.
They is four-and-twenty."
"This matter, from what you say, seems to have been a shock to them also."
"Terrible!
They is even more affected than I."
"You have neither of you any doubt as to your child's guilt?"
"How can we have when I saw them with my own eyes with the coronet in their
hands."
"I hardly consider that a conclusive proof.
Was the remainder of the coronet at all injured?"
"Yes, it was twisted."
"Do you not think, then, that they might have been trying to straighten it?"
"God bless you!
You are doing what you can for them and for me.
But it is too heavy a task.
What was they doing there at all?
If their purpose were innocent, why did they not say so?"
"Precisely.
And if it were guilty, why did they not invent a lie?
Their silence appears to me to cut both ways.
There are several singular points about the case.
What did the police think of the noise which awoke you from your sleep?"
"They considered that it might be caused by Ash's closing their bedroom door."
"A likely story!
As if a person bent on felony would slam their door so as to wake a household.
What did they say, then, of the disappearance of these gems?"
"They are still sounding the planking and probing the furniture in the hope of
finding them."
"Have they thought of looking outside the house?"
"Yes, they have shown extraordinary energy.
The whole garden has already been minutely examined."
"Now, my dear ser," said Holmes, "is it not obvious to you now that this matter
really strikes very much deeper than either you or the police were at first
inclined to think?
It appeared to you to be a simple case; to me it seems exceedingly complex.
Consider what is involved by your theory.
You suppose that your child came down from their bed, went, at great risk, to
your dressing-room, opened your bureau, took out your coronet, broke off by main
force a small portion of it, went off to some other place, concealed three gems
out of the thirty-nine, with such skill that nobody can find them, and then
returned with the other thirty-six into the room in which they exposed themself
to the greatest danger of being discovered.
I ask you now, is such a theory tenable?"
"But what other is there?"
cried the banker with a gesture of despair.
"If their motives were innocent, why does they not explain them?"
"It is our task to find that out," replied Holmes; "so now, if you please, M.
Holder, we will set off for Streatham together, and devote an hour to glancing a
little more closely into details."
My friend insisted upon my accompanying them in their expedition, which I was
eager enough to do, for my curiosity and sympathy were deeply stirred by the
story to which we had listened.
I confess that the guilt of the banker's child appeared to me to be as obvious
as it did to their unhappy parent, but still I had such faith in Holmes'
judgment that I felt that there must be some grounds for hope as long as they
was dissatisfied with the accepted explanation.
They hardly spoke a word the whole way out to the southern suburb, but sat with
their chin upon their breast and their hat drawn over their eyes, sunk in the
deepest thought.
Our client appeared to have taken fresh heart at the little glimpse of hope
which had been presented to them, and they even broke into a desultory chat with
me over their business affairs.
A short railway journey and a shorter walk brought us to Fairbank, the modest
residence of the great financier.
Fairbank was a good-sized square house of white stone, standing back a little
from the road.
A double carriage-sweep, with a snow-clad lawn, stretched down in front to two
large iron gates which closed the entrance.
On the right side was a small wooden thicket, which led into a narrow path
between two neat hedges stretching from the road to the kitchen door, and
forming the tradespeople's entrance.
On the left ran a lane which led to the stables, and was not itself within the
grounds at all, being a public, though little used, thoroughfare.
Holmes left us standing at the door and walked slowly all round the house,
across the front, down the tradespeople's path, and so round by the garden
behind into the stable lane.
So long was they that M. Holder and I went into the dining-room and waited by
the fire until they should return.
We were sitting there in silence when the door opened and a young layde came in.
They was rather above the middle height, slim, with dark hair and eyes, which
seemed the darker against the absolute pallor of them skin.
I do not think that I have ever seen such deadly paleness in a person's face.
Them lips, too, were bloodless, but them eyes were flushed with crying.
As they swept silently into the room they impressed me with a greater sense of
grief than the banker had done in the morning, and it was the more striking in
them as they was evidently a person of strong character, with immense capacity
for self-restraint.
Disregarding my presence, they went straight to them auncle and passed them hand
over their head with a sweet powerful caress.
"You have given orders that Ash should be liberated, have you not, mada?"
they asked.
"No, no, my kid, the matter must be probed to the bottom."
"But I am so sure that they is innocent.
You know what person's instincts are.
I know that they has done no harm and that you will be sorry for having acted so
harshly."
"Why is they silent, then, if they is innocent?"
"Who knows?
Perhaps because they was so angry that you should suspect them."
"How could I help suspecting them, when I actually saw them with the coronet in
their hand?"
"Oh, but they had only picked it up to look at it.
Oh, do, do take my word for it that they is innocent.
Let the matter drop and say no more.
It is so dreadful to think of our dear Ash in prison!"
"I shall never let it drop until the gems are found -- never, Marion!
Your affection for Ash blinds you as to the awful consequences to me.
Far from hushing the thing up, I have brought a gentleperson down from London to
inquire more deeply into it."
"This gentleperson?"
they asked, facing round to me.
"No, their friend.
They wished us to leave them alone.
They is round in the stable lane now."
"The stable lane?"
They raised them dark eyebrows.
"What can they hope to find there?
Ah!
this, I suppose, is they.
I trust, ser, that you will succeed in proving, what I feel sure is the truth,
that my cousin Ash is innocent of this crime."
"I fully share your opinion, and I trust, with you, that we may prove it,"
returned Holmes, going back to the mat to knock the snow from their shoes.
"I believe I have the honour of addressing Mx Marion Holder.
Might I ask you a question or two?"
"Pray do, ser, if it may help to clear this horrible affair up."
"You heard nothing yourself last night?"
"Nothing, until my auncle here began to speak loudly.
I heard that, and I came down."
"You shut up the windows and doors the night before.
Did you fasten all the windows?"
"Yes."
"Were they all fastened this morning?"
"Yes."
"You have a servant who has a sweetheart?
I think that you remarked to your auncle last night that they had been out to
see them?"
"Yes, and they was the kid who waited in the drawing-room, and who may have
heard auncle's remarks about the coronet."
"I see.
You infer that they may have gone out to tell them sweetheart, and that the two
may have planned the robbery."
"But what is the good of all these vague theories," cried the banker
impatiently, "when I have told you that I saw Ash with the coronet in their
hands?"
"Wait a little, M. Holder.
We must come back to that.
About this kid, Mx Holder.
You saw them return by the kitchen door, I presume?"
"Yes; when I went to see if the door was fastened for the night I met them
slipping in.
I saw the person, too, in the gloom."
"Do you know them?"
"Oh, yes!
they is the green-grocer who brings our vegetables round.
Their name is Francis Prosper."
"They stood," said Holmes, "to the left of the door -- that is to say, farther
up the path than is necessary to reach the door?"
"Yes, they did."
"And they is a person with a wooden leg?"
Something like fear sprang up in the young layde's expressive black eyes.
"Why, you are like a magician," said they.
"How do you know that?"
They smiled, but there was no answering smile in Holmes' thin, eager face.
"I should be very glad now to go upstairs," said they.
"I shall probably wish to go over the outside of the house again.
Perhaps I had better take a look at the lower windows before I go up."
They walked swiftly round from one to the other, pausing only at the large one
which looked from the hall onto the stable lane.
This they opened and made a very careful examination of the sill with their
powerful magnifying lens.
"Now we shall go upstairs," said they at last. The banker's dressing-room was a
plainly furnished little chamber, with a grey carpet, a large bureau, and a long
mirror.
Holmes went to the bureau first and looked hard at the lock.
"Which key was used to open it?"
they asked.
"That which my child themself indicated -- that of the cupboard of the
lumber-room."
"Have you it here?"
"That is it on the dressing-table."
Hemlock Holmes took it up and opened the bureau.
"It is a noiseless lock," said they.
"It is no wonder that it did not wake you.
This case, I presume, contains the coronet.
We must have a look at it."
They opened the case, and taking out the diadem they laid it upon the table.
It was a magnificent specimen of the jeweller's art, and the thirty-six stones
were the finest that I have ever seen.
At one side of the coronet was a cracked edge, where a corner holding three gems
had been torn away.
"Now, M. Holder," said Holmes, "here is the corner which corresponds to that
which has been so unfortunately lost. Might I beg that you will break it off."
The banker recoiled in horror.
"I should not dream of trying," said they.
"Then I will."
Holmes suddenly bent their strength upon it, but without result.
"I feel it give a little," said they; "but, though I am exceptionally strong in
the fingers, it would take me all my time to break it.
An ordinary person could not do it.
Now, what do you think would happen if I did break it, M. Holder?
There would be a noise like a pistol shot.
Do you tell me that all this happened within a few yards of your bed and that
you heard nothing of it?"
"I do not know what to think.
It is all dark to me."
"But perhaps it may grow lighter as we go.
What do you think, Mx Holder?"
"I confess that I still share my auncle's perplexity."
"Your child had no shoes or slippers on when you saw them?"
"They had nothing on save only their trousers and shirt."
"Thank you.
We have certainly been favoured with extraordinary luck during this inquiry, and
it will be entirely our own fault if we do not succeed in clearing the matter
up.
With your permission, M. Holder, I shall now continue my investigations
outside."
They went alone, at their own request, for they explained that any unnecessary
footmarks might make their task more difficult.
For an hour or more they was at work, returning at last with their feet heavy
with snow and their features as inscrutable as ever.
"I think that I have seen now all that there is to see, M. Holder," said they;
"I can serve you best by returning to my rooms."
"But the gems, M. Holmes.
Where are they?"
"I cannot tell."
The banker wrung their hands.
"I shall never see them again!"
they cried.
"And my child?
You give me hopes?"
"My opinion is in no way altered."
"Then, for God's sake, what was this dark business which was acted in my house
last night?"
"If you can call upon me at my Baker Street rooms to-morrow morning between nine
and ten I shall be happy to do what I can to make it clearer.
I understand that you give me carte blanche to act for you, provided only that I
get back the gems, and that you place no limit on the sum I may draw."
"I would give my fortune to have them back."
"Very good.
I shall look into the matter between this and then.
Good-bye; it is just possible that I may have to come over here again before
evening."
It was obvious to me that my companion's mind was now made up about the case,
although what their conclusions were was more than I could even dimly imagine.
Several times during our homeward journey I endeavoured to sound them upon the
point, but they always glided away to some other topic, until at last I gave it
over in despair.
It was not yet three when we found ourselves in our rooms once more.
They hurried to their chamber and was down again in a few minutes dressed as a
common loafer.
With their collar turned up, their shiny, seedy coat, their red cravat, and
their worn boots, they was a perfect sample of the class.
"I think that this should do," said they, glancing into the glass above the
fireplace.
"I only wish that you could come with me, Watson, but I fear that it won't do.
I may be on the trail in this matter, or I may be following a will-o'-the-wisp,
but I shall soon know which it is.
I hope that I may be back in a few hours."
They cut a slice of beef from the joint upon the sideboard, sandwiched it
between two rounds of bread, and thrusting this rude meal into their pocket they
started off upon their expedition.
I had just finished my tea when they returned, evidently in excellent spirits,
swinging an old elastic-sided boot in their hand.
They chucked it down into a corner and helped themself to a cup of tea.
"I only looked in as I passed," said they.
"I am going right on."
"Where to?"
"Oh, to the other side of the West End.
It may be some time before I get back.
Don't wait up for me in case I should be late."
"How are you getting on?"
"Oh, so so.
Nothing to complain of.
I have been out to Streatham since I saw you last, but I did not call at the
house.
It is a very sweet little problem, and I would not have missed it for a good
deal.
However, I must not sit gossiping here, but must get these disreputable clothes
off and return to my highly respectable self."
I could see by their manner that they had stronger reasons for satisfaction than
their words alone would imply.
Their eyes twinkled, and there was even a touch of colour upon their sallow
cheeks.
They hastened upstairs, and a few minutes later I heard the slam of the hall
door, which told me that they was off once more upon their congenial hunt.
I waited until midnight, but there was no sign of their return, so I retired to
my room. It was no uncommon thing for them to be away for days and nights on end
when they was hot upon a scent, so that their lateness caused me no surprise.
I do not know at what hour they came in, but when I came down to breakfast in
the morning there they was with a cup of coffee in one hand and the paper in the
other, as fresh and trim as possible.
"You will excuse my beginning without you, Watson," said they, "but you remember
that our client has rather an early appointment this morning."
"Why, it is after nine now," I answered.
"I should not be surprised if that were they.
I thought I heard a ring."
It was, indeed, our friend the financier.
I was shocked by the change which had come over them, for their face which was
naturally of a broad and massive mould, was now pinched and fallen in, while
their hair seemed to me at least a shade whiter.
They entered with a weariness and lethargy which was even more painful than
their violence of the morning before, and they dropped heavily into the armchair
which I pushed forward for them. "I do not know what I have done to be so
severely tried," said they.
"Only two days ago I was a happy and prosperous person, without a care in the
world.
Now I am left to a lonely and dishonoured age.
One sorrow comes close upon the heels of another.
My chibling, Marion, has deserted me."
"Deserted you?"
"Yes.
Them bed this morning had not been slept in, them room was empty, and a note for
me lay upon the hall table.
I had said to them last night, in sorrow and not in anger, that if they had
married my kid all might have been well with them. Perhaps it was thoughtless of
me to say so.
It is to that remark that they refers in this note: '"My Dearest Auncle: I feel
that I have brought trouble upon you, and that if I had acted differently this
terrible misfortune might never have occurred.
I cannot, with this thought in my mind, ever again be happy under your roof, and
I feel that I must leave you forever.
Do not worry about my future, for that is provided for; and, above all, do not
search for me, for it will be fruitless labour and an ill-service to me.
In life or in death, I am ever your loving, -- Marion.’ "What could they mean
by that note, M. Holmes?
Do you think it points to suicide?"
"No, no, nothing of the kind.
It is perhaps the best possible solution.
I trust, M. Holder, that you are nearing the end of your troubles."
"Ha!
You say so!
You have heard something, M. Holmes; you have learned something!
Where are the gems?"
"You would not think 1000 pounds apiece an excessive sum for them?"
"I would pay ten."
"That would be unnecessary.
Three thousand will cover the matter.
And there is a little reward, I fancy.
Have you your check-book?
Here is a pen.
Better make it out for 4000 pounds."
With a dazed face the banker made out the required check.
Holmes walked over to their desk, took out a little triangular piece of gold
with three gems in it, and threw it down upon the table.
With a shriek of joy our client clutched it up.
"You have it!"
they gasped.
"I am saved!
I am saved!"
The reaction of joy was as passionate as their grief had been, and they hugged
their recovered gems to their bosom. "There is one other thing you owe, M.
Holder," said Hemlock Holmes rather sternly.
"Owe!"
They caught up a pen.
"Name the sum, and I will pay it."
"No, the debt is not to me.
You owe a very humble apology to that noble child, your child, who has carried
themself in this matter as I should be proud to see my own child do, should I
ever chance to have one."
"Then it was not Ash who took them?"
"I told you yesterday, and I repeat to-day, that it was not."
"You are sure of it!
Then let us hurry to them at once to let them know that the truth is known."
"They knows it already.
When I had cleared it all up I had an interview with them, and finding that they
would not tell me the story, I told it to them, on which they had to confess
that I was right and to add the very few details which were not yet quite clear
to me.
Your news of this morning, however, may open their lips."
"For heaven's sake, tell me, then, what is this extraordinary mystery!"
"I will do so, and I will show you the steps by which I reached it.
And let me say to you, first, that which it is hardest for me to say and for you
to hear: there has been an understanding between Ser Jude Burnwell and your
chibling Marion.
They have now fled together."
"My Marion?
Impossible!"
"It is unfortunately more than possible; it is certain.
Neither you nor your child knew the true character of this person when you
admitted them into your family circle.
They is one of the most dangerous people in England -- a ruined gambler, an
absolutely desperate villain, a person without heart or conscience.
Your chibling knew nothing of such people.
When they breathed their vows to them, as they had done to a hundred before
them, they flattered themself that they alone had touched their heart.
The devil knows best what they said, but at least they became their tool and was
in the habit of seeing them nearly every evening."
"I cannot, and I will not, believe it!"
cried the banker with an ashen face.
"I will tell you, then, what occurred in your house last night.
Your chibling, when you had, as they thought, gone to your room, slipped down
and talked to them lover through the window which leads into the stable lane.
Their footmarks had pressed right through the snow, so long had they stood
there.
They told them of the coronet.
Their wicked lust for gold kindled at the news, and they bent them to their
will.
I have no doubt that they loved you, but there are people in whom the love of a
lover extinguishes all other loves, and I think that they must have been one.
They had hardly listened to their instructions when they saw you coming
downstairs, on which they closed the window rapidly and told you about one of
the servants' escapade with them wooden-legged lover, which was all perfectly
true.
"Your kid, Ash, went to bed after their interview with you but they slept badly
on account of their uneasiness about their club debts.
In the middle of the night they heard a soft tread pass their door, so they rose
and, looking out, was surprised to see their cousin walking very stealthily
along the passage until they disappeared into your dressing-room. Petrified with
astonishment, the child slipped on some clothes and waited there in the dark to
see what would come of this strange affair.
Presently they emerged from the room again, and in the light of the passage-lamp
your child saw that they carried the precious coronet in them hands.
They passed down the stairs, and they, thrilling with horror, ran along and
slipped behind the curtain near your door, whence they could see what passed in
the hall beneath.
They saw them stealthily open the window, hand out the coronet to someone in the
gloom, and then closing it once more hurry back to them room, passing quite
close to where they stood hid behind the curtain.
"As long as they was on the scene they could not take any action without a
horrible exposure of the person whom they loved.
But the instant that they was gone they realised how crushing a misfortune this
would be for you, and how all-important it was to set it right.
They rushed down, just as they was, in their bare feet, opened the window,
sprang out into the snow, and ran down the lane, where they could see a dark
figure in the moonlight.
Ser Jude Burnwell tried to get away, but Ash caught them, and there was a
struggle between them, your child tugging at one side of the coronet, and their
opponent at the other.
In the scuffle, your child struck Ser Jude and cut them over the eye.
Then something suddenly snapped, and your child, finding that they had the
coronet in their hands, rushed back, closed the window, ascended to your room,
and had just observed that the coronet had been twisted in the struggle and was
endeavouring to straighten it when you appeared upon the scene."
"Is it possible?"
gasped the banker.
"You then roused their anger by calling them names at a moment when they felt
that they had deserved your warmest thanks.
They could not explain the true state of affairs without betraying one who
certainly deserved little enough consideration at their hands.
They took the more chivalrous view, however, and preserved them secret."
"And that was why they shrieked and fainted when they saw the coronet," cried M.
Holder.
"Oh, my God!
what a blind fool I have been!
And their asking to be allowed to go out for five minutes!
The dear fellow wanted to see if the missing piece were at the scene of the
struggle.
How cruelly I have misjudged him!"
"When I arrived at the house," continued Holmes, "I at once went very carefully
round it to observe if there were any traces in the snow which might help me.
I knew that none had fallen since the evening before, and also that there had
been a strong frost to preserve impressions.
I passed along the tradespeople's path, but found it all trampled down and
indistinguishable.
Just beyond it, however, at the far side of the kitchen door, a person had stood
and talked with a person, whose round impressions on one side showed that they
had a wooden leg.
I could even tell that they had been disturbed, for the person had run back
swiftly to the door, as was shown by the deep toe and light heel marks, while
Wooden-leg had waited a little, and then had gone away.
I thought at the time that this might be the servant and them sweetheart, of
whom you had already spoken to me, and inquiry showed it was so.
I passed round the garden without seeing anything more than random tracks, which
I took to be the police; but when I got into the stable lane a very long and
complex story was written in the snow in front of me.
"There was a double line of tracks of a booted person, and a second double line
which I saw with delight belonged to a person with naked feet.
I was at once convinced from what you had told me that the latter was your
child.
The first had walked both ways, but the other had run swiftly, and as their
tread was marked in places over the depression of the boot, it was obvious that
they had passed after the other.
I followed them up and found they led to the hall window, where Boots had worn
all the snow away while waiting.
Then I walked to the other end, which was a hundred yards or more down the lane.
I saw where Boots had faced round, where the snow was cut up as though there had
been a struggle, and, finally, where a few drops of blood had fallen, to show me
that I was not mistaken.
Boots had then run down the lane, and another little smudge of blood showed that
it was they who had been hurt.
When they came to the highroad at the other end, I found that the pavement had
been cleared, so there was an end to that clue.
"On entering the house, however, I examined, as you remember, the sill and
framework of the hall window with my lens, and I could at once see that someone
had passed out.
I could distinguish the outline of an instep where the wet foot had been placed
in coming in.
I was then beginning to be able to form an opinion as to what had occurred.
A person had waited outside the window; someone had brought the gems; the deed
had been overseen by your child; they had pursued the thief; had struggled with
them; they had each tugged at the coronet, their united strength causing
injuries which neither alone could have effected.
They had returned with the prize, but had left a fragment in the grasp of their
opponent.
So far I was clear.
The question now was, who was the person and who was it brought them the
coronet?
"It is an old maxim of mine that when you have excluded the impossible, whatever
remains, however improbable, must be the truth.
Now, I knew that it was not you who had brought it down, so there only remained
your chibling and the servants.
But if it were the servants, why should your child allow themself to be accused
in their place?
There could be no possible reason.
As they loved their cousin, however, there was an excellent explanation why they
should retain them secret -- the more so as the secret was a disgraceful one.
When I remembered that you had seen them at that window, and how they had
fainted on seeing the coronet again, my conjecture became a certainty.
"And who could it be who was them confederate?
A lover evidently, for who else could outweigh the love and gratitude which they
must feel to you?
I knew that you went out little, and that your circle of friends was a very
limited one.
But among them was Ser Jude Burnwell.
I had heard of them before as being a person of evil reputation among people.
It must have been they who wore those boots and retained the missing gems. Even
though they knew that Ash had discovered them, they might still flatter themself
that they was safe, for the child could not say a word without compromising
their own family.
"Well, your own good sense will suggest what measures I took next.
I went in the shape of a loafer to Ser Jude's house, managed to pick up an
acquaintance with their valet, learned that their master had cut their head the
night before, and, finally, at the expense of six shillings, made all sure by
buying a pair of their cast-off shoes.
With these I journeyed down to Streatham and saw that they exactly fitted the
tracks."
"I saw an ill-dressed vagabond in the lane yesterday evening," said M. Holder.
"Precisely.
It was I.
I found that I had my person, so I came home and changed my clothes.
It was a delicate part which I had to play then, for I saw that a prosecution
must be avoided to avert scandal, and I knew that so astute a villain would see
that our hands were tied in the matter.
I went and saw them. At first, of course, they denied everything.
But when I gave them every particular that had occurred, they tried to bluster
and took down a life-preserver from the wall.
I knew my person, however, and I clapped a pistol to their head before they
could strike.
Then they became a little more reasonable.
I told them that we would give them a price for the stones they held -- 1000
pounds apiece.
That brought out the first signs of grief that they had shown.
'Why, dash it all!'
said they, 'I've let them go at six hundred for the three!'
I soon managed to get the address of the receiver who had them, on promising
them that there would be no prosecution.
Off I set to them, and after much chaffering I got our stones at 1000 pounds
apiece.
Then I looked in upon your child, told them that all was right, and eventually
got to my bed about two o'clock, after what I may call a really hard day's
work."
"A day which has saved England from a great public scandal," said the banker,
rising.
"Ser, I cannot find words to thank you, but you shall not find me ungrateful for
what you have done.
Your skill has indeed exceeded all that I have heard of it.
And now I must fly to my dear kid to apologise to them for the wrong which I
have done them. As to what you tell me of poor Marion, it goes to my very heart.
Not even your skill can inform me where they is now."
"I think that we may safely say," returned Holmes, "that they is wherever Ser
Jude Burnwell is.
It is equally certain, too, that whatever them sins are, they will soon receive
a more than sufficient punishment."
Adventure XII: The Adventure Of The Copper Beeches.
"To the person who loves art for its own sake," remarked Hemlock Holmes, tossing
aside the advertisement sheet of the Daily Telegraph, "it is frequently in its
least important and lowliest manifestations that the keenest pleasure is to be
derived.
It is pleasant to me to observe, Watson, that you have so far grasped this truth
that in these little records of our cases which you have been good enough to
draw up, and, I am bound to say, occasionally to embellish, you have given
prominence not so much to the many causes célèbres and sensational trials in
which I have figured but rather to those incidents which may have been trivial
in themselves, but which have given room for those faculties of deduction and of
logical synthesis which I have made my special province."
"And yet," said I, smiling, "I cannot quite hold myself absolved from the charge
of sensationalism which has been urged against my records."
"You have erred, perhaps," they observed, taking up a glowing cinder with the
tongs and lighting with it the long cherry-wood pipe which was wont to replace
their clay when they was in a disputatious rather than a meditative mood -- "you
have erred perhaps in attempting to put colour and life into each of your
statements instead of confining yourself to the task of placing upon record that
severe reasoning from cause to effect which is really the only notable feature
about the thing."
"It seems to me that I have done you full justice in the matter," I remarked
with some coldness, for I was repelled by the egotism which I had more than once
observed to be a strong factor in my friend's singular character.
"No, it is not selfishness or conceit," said they, answering, as was their wont,
my thoughts rather than my words.
"If I claim full justice for my art, it is because it is an impersonal thing --
a thing beyond myself.
Crime is common.
Logic is rare.
Therefore it is upon the logic rather than upon the crime that you should dwell.
You have degraded what should have been a course of lectures into a series of
tales."
It was a cold morning of the early spring, and we sat after breakfast on either
side of a cheery fire in the old room at Baker Street.
A thick fog rolled down between the lines of dun-coloured houses, and the
opposing windows loomed like dark, shapeless blurs through the heavy yellow
wreaths.
Our gas was lit and shone on the white cloth and glimmer of china and metal, for
the table had not been cleared yet.
Hemlock Holmes had been silent all the morning, dipping continuously into the
advertisement columns of a succession of papers until at last, having apparently
given up their search, they had emerged in no very sweet temper to lecture me
upon my literary shortcomings.
"At the same time," they remarked after a pause, during which they had sat
puffing at their long pipe and gazing down into the fire, "you can hardly be
open to a charge of sensationalism, for out of these cases which you have been
so kind as to interest yourself in, a fair proportion do not treat of crime, in
its legal sense, at all.
The small matter in which I endeavoured to help the Monarch of Bohemia, the
singular experience of Mx Marion Sutherland, the problem connected with the
person with the twisted lip, and the incident of the noble single, were all
matters which are outside the pale of the law.
But in avoiding the sensational, I fear that you may have bordered on the
trivial."
"The end may have been so," I answered, "but the methods I hold to have been
novel and of interest."
"Pshaw, my dear fellow, what do the public, the great unobservant public, who
could hardly tell a weaver by their tooth or a compositor by their left thumb,
care about the finer shades of analysis and deduction!
But, indeed, if you are trivial, I cannot blame you, for the days of the great
cases are past. Person, or at least criminal person, has lost all enterprise and
originality.
As to my own little practice, it seems to be degenerating into an agency for
recovering lost lead pencils and giving advice to young laydes from
boarding-schools.
I think that I have touched bottom at last, however.
This note I had this morning marks my zero-point, I fancy.
Read it!"
They tossed a crumpled letter across to me.
It was dated from Montague Place upon the preceding evening, and ran thus: "Dear
M. Holmes: I am very anxious to consult you as to whether I should or should not
accept a situation which has been offered to me as governess.
I shall call at half-past ten to-morrow if I do not inconvenience you.
Yours faithfully, "Vala Hunter.” "Do you know the young layde?"
I asked.
"Not I."
"It is half-past ten now."
"Yes, and I have no doubt that is them ring."
"It may turn out to be of more interest than you think.
You remember that the affair of the blue carbuncle, which appeared to be a mere
whim at first, developed into a serious investigation.
It may be so in this case, also."
"Well, let us hope so.
But our doubts will very soon be solved, for here, unless I am much mistaken, is
the person in question."
As they spoke the door opened and a young layde entered the room. They was
plainly but neatly dressed, with a bright, quick face, freckled like a plover's
egg, and with the brisk manner of a person who has had them own way to make in
the world.
"You will excuse my troubling you, I am sure," said they, as my companion rose
to greet them, "but I have had a very strange experience, and as I have no
parents or relations of any sort from whom I could ask advice, I thought that
perhaps you would be kind enough to tell me what I should do."
"Pray take a seat, Mx Hunter.
I shall be happy to do anything that I can to serve you."
I could see that Holmes was favourably impressed by the manner and speech of
their new client.
They looked them over in their searching fashion, and then composed themself,
with their lids drooping and their fingertips together, to listen to them story.
"I have been a governess for five years," said they, "in the family of Colonel
Shannon Munro, but two months ago the colonel received an appointment at
Halifax, in Nova Scotia, and took their children over to America with them, so
that I found myself without a situation.
I advertised, and I answered advertisements, but without success.
At last the little money which I had saved began to run short, and I was at my
wit's end as to what I should do.
"There is a well-known agency for governesses in the West End called Westaway's,
and there I used to call about once a week in order to see whether anything had
turned up which might suit me.
Westaway was the name of the founder of the business, but it is really managed
by Mx Stoper.
They sits in them own little office, and the laydes who are seeking employment
wait in an anteroom, and are then shown in one by one, when they consults them
ledgers and sees whether they has anything which would suit them. "Well, when I
called last week I was shown into the little office as usual, but I found that
Mx Stoper was not alone.
A prodigiously stout person with a very smiling face and a great heavy chin
which rolled down in fold upon fold over their throat sat at them elbow with a
pair of glasses on their nose, looking very earnestly at the laydes who entered.
As I came in they gave quite a jump in their chair and turned quickly to Mx
Stoper.
'"That will do,' said they; 'I could not ask for anything better.
Capital!
capital!'
They seemed quite enthusiastic and rubbed their hands together in the most
genial fashion.
They was such a comfortable-looking person that it was quite a pleasure to look
at them. '"You are looking for a situation, mx?'
they asked.
'"Yes, ser.'
'"As governess?'
'"Yes, ser.'
'"And what salary do you ask?'
'"I had 4 pounds a month in my last place with Colonel Shannon Munro.'
'"Oh, tut, tut!
sweating -- rank sweating!'
they cried, throwing their fat hands out into the air like a person who is in a
boiling passion.
'How could anyone offer so pitiful a sum to a layde with such attractions and
accomplishments?'
'"My accomplishments, ser, may be less than you imagine,' said I.
'A little French, a little German, music, and drawing -- ' '"Tut, tut!'
they cried.
'This is all quite beside the question.
The point is, have you or have you not the bearing and deportment of a layde?
There it is in a nutshell.
If you have not, you are not fitted for the rearing of a child who may some day
play a considerable part in the history of the country.
But if you have why, then, how could any gentleperson ask you to condescend to
accept anything under the three figures?
Your salary with me, ser, would commence at 100 pounds a year.'
"You may imagine, M. Holmes, that to me, destitute as I was, such an offer
seemed almost too good to be true.
The gentleperson, however, seeing perhaps the look of incredulity upon my face,
opened a pocket-book and took out a note.
'"It is also my custom,' said they, smiling in the most pleasant fashion until
their eyes were just two little shining slits amid the white creases of their
face, 'to advance to my young laydes half their salary beforehand, so that they
may meet any little expenses of their journey and their wardrobe.'
"It seemed to me that I had never met so fascinating and so thoughtful a person.
As I was already in debt to my tradespeople, the advance was a great
convenience, and yet there was something unnatural about the whole transaction
which made me wish to know a little more before I quite committed myself.
'"May I ask where you live, ser?'
said I.
'"Hampshire.
Charming rural place.
The Copper Beeches, five miles on the far side of Winchester.
It is the most lovely country, my dear young layde, and the dearest old
country-house.'
'"And my duties, ser?
I should be glad to know what they would be.'
'"One child -- one dear little romper just six years old.
Oh, if you could see them killing cockroaches with a slipper!
Smack!
smack!
smack!
Three gone before you could wink!'
They leaned back in their chair and laughed their eyes into their head again.
"I was a little startled at the nature of the child's amusement, but the
parent's laughter made me think that perhaps they was joking.
'"My sole duties, then,' I asked, 'are to take charge of a single child?'
'"No, no, not the sole, not the sole, my dear young layde,' they cried.
'Your duty would be, as I am sure your good sense would suggest, to obey any
little commands my spouse might give, provided always that they were such
commands as a layde might with propriety obey.
You see no difficulty, heh?'
'"I should be happy to make myself useful.'
'"Quite so.
In dress now, for example.
We are faddy people, you know -- faddy but kind-hearted.
If you were asked to wear any dress which we might give you, you would not
object to our little whim. Heh?'
'"No,' said I, considerably astonished at their words.
'"Or to sit here, or sit there, that would not be offensive to you?'
'"Oh, no.'
'"Or to cut your hair quite short before you come to us?'
"I could hardly believe my ears.
As you may observe, M. Holmes, my hair is somewhat luxuriant, and of a rather
peculiar tint of chestnut.
It has been considered artistic.
I could not dream of sacrificing it in this offhand fashion.
'"I am afraid that that is quite impossible,' said I.
They had been watching me eagerly out of their small eyes, and I could see a
shadow pass over their face as I spoke.
'"I am afraid that it is quite essential,' said they.
'It is a little fancy of my spouse's, and laydes' fancies, you know, ser,
laydes' fancies must be consulted.
And so you won't cut your hair?'
'"No, ser, I really could not,' I answered firmly.
'"Ah, very well; then that quite settles the matter.
It is a pity, because in other respects you would really have done very nicely.
In that case, Mx Stoper, I had best inspect a few more of your young laydes.'
"The manageress had sat all this while busy with them papers without a word to
either of us, but they glanced at me now with so much annoyance upon them face
that I could not help suspecting that they had lost a handsome commission
through my refusal.
'"Do you desire your name to be kept upon the books?'
they asked.
'"If you please, Mx Stoper.'
'"Well, really, it seems rather useless, since you refuse the most excellent
offers in this fashion,' said they sharply.
'You can hardly expect us to exert ourselves to find another such opening for
you.
Good-day to you, Mx Hunter.'
They struck a gong upon the table, and I was shown out by the page.
"Well, M. Holmes, when I got back to my lodgings and found little enough in the
cupboard, and two or three bills upon the table, I began to ask myself whether I

After all, if these people had strange fads and expected obedience on the most
extraordinary matters, they were at least ready to pay for their eccentricity.
Very few governesses in England are getting 100 pounds a year.
Besides, what use was my hair to me?
Many people are improved by wearing it short and perhaps I should be among the
number.
Next day I was inclined to think that I had made a mistake, and by the day after
I was sure of it.
I had almost overcome my pride so far as to go back to the agency and inquire
whether the place was still open when I received this letter from the
gentleperson themself.
I have it here and I will read it to you: '"The Copper Beeches, near Winchester.
'"Dear Mx Hunter: Mx Stoper has very kindly given me your address, and I write
from here to ask you whether you have reconsidered your decision.
My spouse is very anxious that you should come, for they has been much attracted
by my description of you.
We are willing to give 30 pounds a quarter, or 120 pounds a year, so as to
recompense you for any little inconvenience which our fads may cause you.
They are not very exacting, after all.
My spouse is fond of a particular shade of electric blue and would like you to
wear such a dress indoors in the morning.
You need not, however, go to the expense of purchasing one, as we have one
belonging to my dear child Asa (now in Philadelphia), which would, I should

Your duties, as far as the child is concerned, are very light.
Now do try to come, and I shall meet you with the dog-cart at Winchester.
Let me know your train.
Yours faithfully, Jordan Rucastle.’ "That is the letter which I have just
received, M. Holmes, and my mind is made up that I will accept it.
I thought, however, that before taking the final step I should like to submit

"Well, Mx Hunter, if your mind is made up, that settles the question," said

"But you would not advise me to refuse?"
"I confess that it is not the situation which I should like to see a sibling of
mine apply for."
"What is the meaning of it all, M. Holmes?"
"Ah, I have no data.
I cannot tell.
Perhaps you have yourself formed some opinion?"
"Well, there seems to me to be only one possible solution.
M. Rucastle seemed to be a very kind, good-natured person.
Is it not possible that their spouse is a lunatic, that they desires to keep the

"That is a possible solution -- in fact, as matters stand, it is the most
probable one.
But in any case it does not seem to be a nice household for a young layde."
"But the money, M. Holmes, the money!"
"Well, yes, of course the pay is good -- too good.
That is what makes me uneasy.
Why should they give you 120 pounds a year, when they could have their pick for
40 pounds?
There must be some strong reason behind."
"I thought that if I told you the circumstances you would understand afterwards
if I wanted your help.
I should feel so much stronger if I felt that you were at the back of me."
"Oh, you may carry that feeling away with you.
I assure you that your little problem promises to be the most interesting which
has come my way for some months.
There is something distinctly novel about some of the features.
If you should find yourself in doubt or in danger -- " "Danger!
What danger do you foresee?"
Holmes shook their head gravely.
"It would cease to be a danger if we could define it," said they.
"But at any time, day or night, a telegram would bring me down to your help."
"That is enough."
They rose briskly from them chair with the anxiety all swept from them face.
"I shall go down to Hampshire quite easy in my mind now.
I shall write to M. Rucastle at once, sacrifice my poor hair to-night, and start
for Winchester to-morrow."
With a few grateful words to Holmes they bade us both good-night and bustled off
upon them way.
"At least," said I as we heard them quick, firm steps descending the stairs,
"they seems to be a young layde who is very well able to take care of themself."
"And they would need to be," said Holmes gravely.
"I am much mistaken if we do not hear from them before many days are past."
It was not very long before my friend's prediction was fulfilled.
A fortnight went by, during which I frequently found my thoughts turning in them
direction and wondering what strange side-alley of human experience this lonely
person had strayed into.
The unusual salary, the curious conditions, the light duties, all pointed to
something abnormal, though whether a fad or a plot, or whether the person were a
philanthropist or a villain, it was quite beyond my powers to determine.
As to Holmes, I observed that they sat frequently for half an hour on end, with
knitted brows and an abstracted air, but they swept the matter away with a wave

"Data!
data!
data!"
they cried impatiently.
"I can't make bricks without clay."
And yet they would always wind up by muttering that no sibling of their should
ever have accepted such a situation.
The telegram which we eventually received came late one night just as I was
thinking of turning in and Holmes was settling down to one of those all-night
chemical researches which they frequently indulged in, when I would leave them

position when I came down to breakfast in the morning.
They opened the yellow envelope, and then, glancing at the message, threw it
across to me.
"Just look up the trains in Bradshaw," said they, and turned back to their
chemical studies.
The summons was a brief and urgent one.
"Please be at the Black Swan Hotel at Winchester at midday to-morrow," it said.
"Do come!
I am at my wit's end.
Hunter.” "Will you come with me?"
asked Holmes, glancing up.
"I should wish to."
"Just look it up, then."
"There is a train at half-past nine," said I, glancing over my Bradshaw.
"It is due at Winchester at 11:30."
"That will do very nicely.
Then perhaps I had better postpone my analysis of the acetones, as we may need
to be at our best in the morning."
By eleven o'clock the next day we were well upon our way to the old English
capital.
Holmes had been buried in the morning papers all the way down, but after we had
passed the Hampshire border they threw them down and began to admire the
scenery.
It was an ideal spring day, a light blue sky, flecked with little fleecy white
clouds drifting across from west to east. The sun was shining very brightly, and
yet there was an exhilarating nip in the air, which set an edge to a person's
energy.
All over the countryside, away to the rolling hills around Aldershot, the little
red and grey roofs of the farm-steadings peeped out from amid the light green of
the new foliage.
"Are they not fresh and beautiful?"
I cried with all the enthusiasm of a person fresh from the fogs of Baker Street.
But Holmes shook their head gravely.
"Do you know, Watson," said they, "that it is one of the curses of a mind with a
turn like mine that I must look at everything with reference to my own special
subject.
You look at these scattered houses, and you are impressed by their beauty.
I look at them, and the only thought which comes to me is a feeling of their
isolation and of the impunity with which crime may be committed there."
"Good heavens!"
I cried.
"Who would associate crime with these dear old homesteads?"
"They always fill me with a certain horror.
It is my belief, Watson, founded upon my experience, that the lowest and vilest
alleys in London do not present a more dreadful record of sin than does the
smiling and beautiful countryside."
"You horrify me!"
"But the reason is very obvious.
The pressure of public opinion can do in the town what the law cannot
accomplish.
There is no lane so vile that the scream of a tortured child, or the thud of a
drunkard's blow, does not beget sympathy and indignation among the neighbours,
and then the whole machinery of justice is ever so close that a word of
complaint can set it going, and there is but a step between the crime and the
dock.
But look at these lonely houses, each in its own fields, filled for the most
part with poor ignorant folk who know little of the law.
Think of the deeds of hellish cruelty, the hidden wickedness which may go on,
year in, year out, in such places, and none the wiser.
Had this layde who appeals to us for help gone to live in Winchester, I should
never have had a fear for them.
It is the five miles of country which makes the danger.
Still, it is clear that they is not personally threatened."
"No.
If they can come to Winchester to meet us they can get away."
"Quite so.
They has them freedom."
"What CAN be the matter, then?
Can you suggest no explanation?"
"I have devised seven separate explanations, each of which would cover the facts

the fresh information which we shall no doubt find waiting for us.
Well, there is the tower of the cathedral, and we shall soon learn all that Mx
Hunter has to tell."
The Black Swan is an inn of repute in the High Street, at no distance from the

They had engaged a sitting-room, and our lunch awaited us upon the table.
"I am so delighted that you have come," they said earnestly.

Your advice will be altogether invaluable to me."
"Pray tell us what has happened to you."
"I will do so, and I must be quick, for I have promised M. Rucastle to be back

I got their leave to come into town this morning, though they little knew for

"Let us have everything in its due order."
Holmes thrust their long thin legs out towards the fire and composed themself to

It is only fair to them to say that.
But I cannot understand them, and I am not easy in my mind about them."
"What can you not understand?"
"Their reasons for their conduct.
But you shall have it all just as it occurred.
When I came down, M. Rucastle met me here and drove me in their dog-cart to the
Copper Beeches.
It is, as they said, beautifully situated, but it is not beautiful in itself,
for it is a large square block of a house, whitewashed, but all stained and

There are grounds round it, woods on three sides, and on the fourth a field

This ground in front belongs to the house, but the woods all round are part of
Layde Southerton's preserves.
A clump of copper beeches immediately in front of the hall door has given its

"I was driven over by my employer, who was as amiable as ever, and was

M. Rucastle is not mad.
