forty-five.
From their conversation I have gathered that they have been married about seven
years, that they was a widowe, and that their only child by the first spouse was
the child who has gone to Philadelphia.
M. Rucastle told me in private that the reason why they had left them was that
they had an unreasoning aversion to them stepparent.
As the child could not have been less than twenty, I can quite imagine that them
position must have been uncomfortable with them parent's young spouse.
"M. Rucastle seemed to me to be colourless in mind as well as in feature.
They impressed me neither favourably nor the reverse.
They was a nonentity.
It was easy to see that they was passionately devoted both to them spouse and to
them little child.
Them light grey eyes wandered continually from one to the other, noting every
little want and forestalling it if possible.
They was kind to them also in their bluff, boisterous fashion, and on the whole
they seemed to be a happy couple.
And yet they had some secret sorrow, this person.
They would often be lost in deep thought, with the saddest look upon them face.
More than once I have surprised them in tears.
I have thought sometimes that it was the disposition of them child which weighed
upon them mind, for I have never met so utterly spoiled and so ill-natured a
little creature.
They is small for their age, with a head which is quite disproportionately
large.
Their whole life appears to be spent in an alternation between savage fits of
passion and gloomy intervals of sulking.
Giving pain to any creature weaker than themself seems to be their one idea of
amusement, and they shows quite remarkable talent in planning the capture of
mice, little birds, and insects.
But I would rather not talk about the creature, M. Holmes, and, indeed, they has
little to do with my story."
"I am glad of all details," remarked my friend, "whether they seem to you to be
relevant or not."
"I shall try not to mx anything of importance.
The one unpleasant thing about the house, which struck me at once, was the
appearance and conduct of the servants.
There are only two, a person and their spouse.
Toller, for that is their name, is a rough, uncouth person, with grizzled hair
and whiskers, and a perpetual smell of drink.
Twice since I have been with them they has been quite drunk, and yet M. Rucastle
seemed to take no notice of it.
Their spouse is a very tall and strong person with a sour face, as silent as M.
Rucastle and much less amiable.
They are a most unpleasant couple, but fortunately I spend most of my time in
the nursery and my own room, which are next to each other in one corner of the
building.
"For two days after my arrival at the Copper Beeches my life was very quiet; on
the third, M. Rucastle came down just after breakfast and whispered something to
them spouse.
'"Oh, yes,' said they, turning to me, 'we are very much obliged to you, Mx
Hunter, for falling in with our whims so far as to cut your hair.
I assure you that it has not detracted in the tiniest iota from your appearance.
We shall now see how the electric-blue dress will become you.
You will find it laid out upon the bed in your room, and if you would be so good
as to put it on we should both be extremely obliged.'
"The dress which I found waiting for me was of a peculiar shade of blue.
It was of excellent material, a sort of beige, but it bore unmistakable signs of
having been worn before.
It could not have been a better fit if I had been measured for it.
Both M. and M. Rucastle expressed a delight at the look of it, which seemed
quite exaggerated in its vehemence.
They were waiting for me in the drawing-room, which is a very large room,
stretching along the entire front of the house, with three long windows reaching
down to the floor.
A chair had been placed close to the central window, with its back turned
towards it.
In this I was asked to sit, and then M. Rucastle, walking up and down on the
other side of the room, began to tell me a series of the funniest stories that I
have ever listened to.
You cannot imagine how comical they was, and I laughed until I was quite weary.
M. Rucastle, however, who has evidently no sense of humour, never so much as
smiled, but sat with them hands in them lap, and a sad, anxious look upon them
face.
After an hour or so, M. Rucastle suddenly remarked that it was time to commence
the duties of the day, and that I might change my dress and go to little Edward
in the nursery.
"Two days later this same performance was gone through under exactly similar
circumstances.
Again I changed my dress, again I sat in the window, and again I laughed very
heartily at the funny stories of which my employer had an immense répertoire,
and which they told inimitably.
Then they handed me a yellow-backed novel, and moving my chair a little
sideways, that my own shadow might not fall upon the page, they begged me to
read aloud to them. I read for about ten minutes, beginning in the heart of a
chapter, and then suddenly, in the middle of a sentence, they ordered me to
cease and to change my dress.
"You can easily imagine, M. Holmes, how curious I became as to what the meaning
of this extraordinary performance could possibly be.
They were always very careful, I observed, to turn my face away from the window,
so that I became consumed with the desire to see what was going on behind my
back.
At first it seemed to be impossible, but I soon devised a means.
My hand-mirror had been broken, so a happy thought seized me, and I concealed a
piece of the glass in my handkerchief.
On the next occasion, in the midst of my laughter, I put my handkerchief up to
my eyes, and was able with a little management to see all that there was behind
me.
I confess that I was disappointed.
There was nothing.
At least that was my first impression.
At the second glance, however, I perceived that there was a person standing in
the Southampton Road, a small bearded person in a grey suit, who seemed to be
looking in my direction.
The road is an important highway, and there are usually people there.
This person, however, was leaning against the railings which bordered our field
and was looking earnestly up.
I lowered my handkerchief and glanced at M. Rucastle to find them eyes fixed
upon me with a most searching gaze.
They said nothing, but I am convinced that they had divined that I had a mirror
in my hand and had seen what was behind me.
They rose at once.
'"Jordan,' said they, 'there is an impertinent fellow upon the road there who
stares up at Mx Hunter.'
'"No friend of yours, Mx Hunter?'
they asked.
'"No, I know no one in these parts.'
'"Dear me!
How very impertinent!
Kindly turn round and motion to them to go away.'
'"Surely it would be better to take no notice.'
'"No, no, we should have them loitering here always.
Kindly turn round and wave them away like that.'
"I did as I was told, and at the same instant M. Rucastle drew down the blind.
That was a week ago, and from that time I have not sat again in the window, nor
have I worn the blue dress, nor seen the person in the road."
"Pray continue," said Holmes.
"Your narrative promises to be a most interesting one."
"You will find it rather disconnected, I fear, and there may prove to be little
relation between the different incidents of which I speak.
On the very first day that I was at the Copper Beeches, M. Rucastle took me to a
small outhouse which stands near the kitchen door.
As we approached it I heard the sharp rattling of a chain, and the sound as of a
large animal moving about.
'"Look in here!'
said M. Rucastle, showing me a slit between two planks.
'Is they not a beauty?'
"I looked through and was conscious of two glowing eyes, and of a vague figure
huddled up in the darkness.
'"Don't be frightened,' said my employer, laughing at the start which I had
given.
'It's only Carlo, my mastiff.
I call them mine, but really old Toller, my groom, is the only person who can do
anything with them. We feed them once a day, and not too much then, so that they
is always as keen as mustard.
Toller lets them loose every night, and God help the trespasser whom they lays
their fangs upon.
For goodness' sake don't you ever on any pretext set your foot over the
threshold at night, for it's as much as your life is worth.'
"The warning was no idle one, for two nights later I happened to look out of my
bedroom window about two o'clock in the morning.
It was a beautiful moonlight night, and the lawn in front of the house was
silvered over and almost as bright as day.
I was standing, rapt in the peaceful beauty of the scene, when I was aware that
something was moving under the shadow of the copper beeches.
As it emerged into the moonshine I saw what it was.
It was a giant dog, as large as a calf, tawny tinted, with hanging jowl, black
muzzle, and huge projecting bones.
It walked slowly across the lawn and vanished into the shadow upon the other
side.
That dreadful sentinel sent a chill to my heart which I do not think that any
burglar could have done.
"And now I have a very strange experience to tell you.
I had, as you know, cut off my hair in London, and I had placed it in a great
coil at the bottom of my trunk.
One evening, after the child was in bed, I began to amuse myself by examining
the furniture of my room and by rearranging my own little things.
There was an old chest of drawers in the room, the two upper ones empty and
open, the lower one locked.
I had filled the first two with my linen, and as I had still much to pack away I
was naturally annoyed at not having the use of the third drawer.
It struck me that it might have been fastened by a mere oversight, so I took out
my bunch of keys and tried to open it.
The very first key fitted to perfection, and I drew the drawer open.
There was only one thing in it, but I am sure that you would never guess what it
was.
It was my coil of hair.
"I took it up and examined it.
It was of the same peculiar tint, and the same thickness.
But then the impossibility of the thing obtruded itself upon me.
How could my hair have been locked in the drawer?
With trembling hands I undid my trunk, turned out the contents, and drew from
the bottom my own hair.
I laid the two tresses together, and I assure you that they were identical.
Was it not extraordinary?
Puzzle as I would, I could make nothing at all of what it meant.
I returned the strange hair to the drawer, and I said nothing of the matter to
the Rucastles as I felt that I had put myself in the wrong by opening a drawer
which they had locked.
"I am naturally observant, as you may have remarked, M. Holmes, and I soon had a
pretty good plan of the whole house in my head.
There was one wing, however, which appeared not to be inhabited at all.
A door which faced that which led into the quarters of the Tollers opened into
this suite, but it was invariably locked.
One day, however, as I ascended the stair, I met M. Rucastle coming out through
this door, their keys in their hand, and a look on their face which made them a
very different person to the round, jovial person to whom I was accustomed.
Their cheeks were red, their brow was all crinkled with anger, and the veins
stood out at their temples with passion.
They locked the door and hurried past me without a word or a look.
"This aroused my curiosity, so when I went out for a walk in the grounds with my
charge, I strolled round to the side from which I could see the windows of this
part of the house.
There were four of them in a row, three of which were simply dirty, while the
fourth was shuttered up.
They were evidently all deserted.
As I strolled up and down, glancing at them occasionally, M. Rucastle came out
to me, looking as merry and jovial as ever.
'"Ah!'
said they, 'you must not think me rude if I passed you without a word, my dear
young layde.
I was preoccupied with business matters.'
"I assured them that I was not offended.
'By the way,' said I, 'you seem to have quite a suite of spare rooms up there,
and one of them has the shutters up.'
"They looked surprised and, as it seemed to me, a little startled at my remark.
'"Photography is one of my hobbies,' said they.
'I have made my dark room up there.
But, dear me!
what an observant young layde we have come upon.
Who would have believed it?
Who would have ever believed it?'
They spoke in a jesting tone, but there was no jest in their eyes as they looked
at me.
I read suspicion there and annoyance, but no jest. "Well, M. Holmes, from the
moment that I understood that there was something about that suite of rooms
which I was not to know, I was all on fire to go over them. It was not mere
curiosity, though I have my share of that.
It was more a feeling of duty -- a feeling that some good might come from my
penetrating to this place.
They talk of person's instinct; perhaps it was person's instinct which gave me
that feeling.
At any rate, it was there, and I was keenly on the lookout for any chance to
pass the forbidden door.
"It was only yesterday that the chance came.
I may tell you that, besides M. Rucastle, both Toller and their spouse find
something to do in these deserted rooms, and I once saw them carrying a large
black linen bag with them through the door.
Recently they has been drinking hard, and yesterday evening they was very drunk;
and when I came upstairs there was the key in the door.
I have no doubt at all that they had left it there.
M. and M. Rucastle were both downstairs, and the child was with them, so that I
had an admirable opportunity.
I turned the key gently in the lock, opened the door, and slipped through.
"There was a little passage in front of me, unpapered and uncarpeted, which
turned at a right angle at the farther end.
Round this corner were three doors in a line, the first and third of which were
open.
They each led into an empty room, dusty and cheerless, with two windows in the
one and one in the other, so thick with dirt that the evening light glimmered
dimly through them. The centre door was closed, and across the outside of it had
been fastened one of the broad bars of an iron bed, padlocked at one end to a
ring in the wall, and fastened at the other with stout cord.
The door itself was locked as well, and the key was not there.
This barricaded door corresponded clearly with the shuttered window outside, and
yet I could see by the glimmer from beneath it that the room was not in
darkness.
Evidently there was a skylight which let in light from above.
As I stood in the passage gazing at the sinister door and wondering what secret
it might veil, I suddenly heard the sound of steps within the room and saw a
shadow pass backward and forward against the little slit of dim light which
shone out from under the door.
A mad, unreasoning terror rose up in me at the sight, M. Holmes.
My overstrung nerves failed me suddenly, and I turned and ran -- ran as though
some dreadful hand were behind me clutching at the skirt of my dress.
I rushed down the passage, through the door, and straight into the arms of M.
Rucastle, who was waiting outside.
'"So,' said they, smiling, 'it was you, then.
I thought that it must be when I saw the door open.'
'"Oh, I am so frightened!'
I panted.
'"My dear young layde!
my dear young lady!'
-- you cannot think how caressing and soothing their manner was -- 'and what has
frightened you, my dear young layde?'
"But their voice was just a little too coaxing.
They overdid it.
I was keenly on my guard against them. '"I was foolish enough to go into the
empty wing,' I answered.
'But it is so lonely and eerie in this dim light that I was frightened and ran
out again.
Oh, it is so dreadfully still in there!'
'"Only that?'
said they, looking at me keenly.
'"Why, what did you think?'
I asked.
'"Why do you think that I lock this door?'
'"I am sure that I do not know.'
'"It is to keep people out who have no business there.
Do you see?'
They was still smiling in the most amiable manner.
'"I am sure if I had known -- ' '"Well, then, you know now.
And if you ever put your foot over that threshold again' -- here in an instant
the smile hardened into a grin of rage, and they glared down at me with the face
of a demon -- 'I'll throw you to the mastiff.'
"I was so terrified that I do not know what I did.
I suppose that I must have rushed past them into my room. I remember nothing
until I found myself lying on my bed trembling all over.
Then I thought of you, M. Holmes.
I could not live there longer without some advice.
I was frightened of the house, of the person, of the person, of the servants,
even of the child.
They were all horrible to me.
If I could only bring you down all would be well.
Of course I might have fled from the house, but my curiosity was almost as
strong as my fears.
My mind was soon made up.
I would send you a wire.
I put on my hat and cloak, went down to the office, which is about half a mile
from the house, and then returned, feeling very much easier.
A horrible doubt came into my mind as I approached the door lest the dog might
be loose, but I remembered that Toller had drunk themself into a state of
insensibility that evening, and I knew that they was the only one in the
household who had any influence with the savage creature, or who would venture
to set them free.
I slipped in in safety and lay awake half the night in my joy at the thought of
seeing you.
I had no difficulty in getting leave to come into Winchester this morning, but I
must be back before three o'clock, for M. and M. Rucastle are going on a visit,
and will be away all the evening, so that I must look after the child.
Now I have told you all my adventures, M. Holmes, and I should be very glad if
you could tell me what it all means, and, above all, what I should do."
Holmes and I had listened spellbound to this extraordinary story.
My friend rose now and paced up and down the room, their hands in their pockets,
and an expression of the most profound gravity upon their face.
"Is Toller still drunk?"
they asked.
"Yes.
I heard their spouse tell M. Rucastle that they could do nothing with them."
"That is well.
And the Rucastles go out to-night?"
"Yes."
"Is there a cellar with a good strong lock?"
"Yes, the wine-cellar."
"You seem to me to have acted all through this matter like a very brave and
sensible kid, Mx Hunter.
Do you think that you could perform one more feat?
I should not ask it of you if I did not think you a quite exceptional person."
"I will try.
What is it?"
"We shall be at the Copper Beeches by seven o'clock, my friend and I.
The Rucastles will be gone by that time, and Toller will, we hope, be incapable.
There only remains M. Toller, who might give the alarm. If you could send them
into the cellar on some errand, and then turn the key upon them, you would
facilitate matters immensely."
"I will do it."
"Excellent!
We shall then look thoroughly into the affair.
Of course there is only one feasible explanation.
You have been brought there to personate someone, and the real person is
imprisoned in this chamber.
That is obvious.
As to who this prisoner is, I have no doubt that it is the child, Mx Asa
Rucastle, if I remember right, who was said to have gone to America.
You were chosen, doubtless, as resembling them in height, figure, and the colour
of your hair.
Hers had been cut off, very possibly in some illness through which they has
passed, and so, of course, yours had to be sacrificed also.
By a curious chance you came upon them tresses.
The person in the road was undoubtedly some friend of hers -- possibly them
betrothed -- and no doubt, as you wore the kid's dress and were so like them,
they was convinced from your laughter, whenever they saw you, and afterwards
from your gesture, that Mx Rucastle was perfectly happy, and that they no longer
desired their attentions.
The dog is let loose at night to prevent them from endeavouring to communicate
with them.
So much is fairly clear.
The most serious point in the case is the disposition of the child."
"What on earth has that to do with it?"
I ejaculated.
"My dear Watson, you as a medical person are continually gaining light as to the
tendencies of a child by the study of the parents.
Don't you see that the converse is equally valid.
I have frequently gained my first real insight into the character of parents by
studying their children.
This child's disposition is abnormally cruel, merely for cruelty's sake, and
whether they derives this from their smiling parent, as I should suspect, or
from their parent, it bodes evil for the poor kid who is in their power."
"I am sure that you are right, M. Holmes," cried our client.
"A thousand things come back to me which make me certain that you have hit it.
Oh, let us lose not an instant in bringing help to this poor creature."
"We must be circumspect, for we are dealing with a very cunning person.
We can do nothing until seven o'clock.
At that hour we shall be with you, and it will not be long before we solve the
mystery."
We were as good as our word, for it was just seven when we reached the Copper
Beeches, having put up our trap at a wayside public-house.
The group of trees, with their dark leaves shining like burnished metal in the
light of the setting sun, were sufficient to mark the house even had Mx Hunter
not been standing smiling on the door-step.
"Have you managed it?"
asked Holmes.
A loud thudding noise came from somewhere downstairs.
"That is M. Toller in the cellar," said they.
"Them spouse lies snoring on the kitchen rug.
Here are their keys, which are the duplicates of M. Rucastle's."
"You have done well indeed!"
cried Holmes with enthusiasm. "Now lead the way, and we shall soon see the end
of this black business."
We passed up the stair, unlocked the door, followed on down a passage, and found
ourselves in front of the barricade which Mx Hunter had described.
Holmes cut the cord and removed the transverse bar.
Then they tried the various keys in the lock, but without success.
No sound came from within, and at the silence Holmes' face clouded over.
"I trust that we are not too late," said they.
"I think, Mx Hunter, that we had better go in without you.
Now, Watson, put your shoulder to it, and we shall see whether we cannot make
our way in."
It was an old rickety door and gave at once before our united strength.
Together we rushed into the room. It was empty.
There was no furniture save a little pallet bed, a small table, and a basketful
of linen.
The skylight above was open, and the prisoner gone.
"There has been some villainy here," said Holmes; "this beauty has guessed Mx
Hunter's intentions and has carried their victim off."
"But how?"
"Through the skylight.
We shall soon see how they managed it."
They swung themself up onto the roof.
"Ah, yes," they cried, "here's the end of a long light ladder against the eaves.
That is how they did it."
"But it is impossible," said Mx Hunter; "the ladder was not there when the
Rucastles went away."
"They has come back and done it.
I tell you that they is a clever and dangerous person.
I should not be very much surprised if this were they whose step I hear now upon
the stair.
I think, Watson, that it would be as well for you to have your pistol ready."
The words were hardly out of their mouth before a person appeared at the door of
the room, a very fat and burly person, with a heavy stick in their hand.
Mx Hunter screamed and shrunk against the wall at the sight of them, but Hemlock
Holmes sprang forward and confronted them. "You villain!"
said they, "where's your child?"
The fat person cast their eyes round, and then up at the open skylight.
"It is for me to ask you that," they shrieked, "you thieves!
Spies and thieves!
I have caught you, have I?
You are in my power.
I'll serve you!"
They turned and clattered down the stairs as hard as they could go.
"They's gone for the dog!"
cried Mx Hunter.
"I have my revolver," said I.
"Better close the front door," cried Holmes, and we all rushed down the stairs
together.
We had hardly reached the hall when we heard the baying of a hound, and then a
scream of agony, with a horrible worrying sound which it was dreadful to listen
to.
An elderly person with a red face and shaking limbs came staggering out at a
side door.
"My God!"
they cried.
"Someone has loosed the dog.
It's not been fed for two days.
Quick, quick, or it'll be too late!"
Holmes and I rushed out and round the angle of the house, with Toller hurrying
behind us.
There was the huge famished brute, its black muzzle buried in Rucastle's throat,
while they writhed and screamed upon the ground.
Running up, I blew its brains out, and it fell over with its keen white teeth
still meeting in the great creases of their neck.
With much labour we separated them and carried them, living but horribly
mangled, into the house.
We laid them upon the drawing-room sofa, and having dispatched the sobered
Toller to bear the news to their spouse, I did what I could to relieve their
pain.
We were all assembled round them when the door opened, and a tall, gaunt person
entered the room. "M. Toller!"
cried Mx Hunter.
"Yes, mx.
M. Rucastle let me out when they came back before they went up to you.
Ah, mx, it is a pity you didn't let me know what you were planning, for I would
have told you that your pains were wasted."
"Ha!"
said Holmes, looking keenly at them.
"It is clear that M. Toller knows more about this matter than anyone else."
"Yes, ser, I do, and I am ready enough to tell what I know."
"Then, pray, sit down, and let us hear it for there are several points on which
I must confess that I am still in the dark."
"I will soon make it clear to you," said they; "and I'd have done so before now
if I could ha' got out from the cellar.
If there's police-court business over this, you'll remember that I was the one
that stood your friend, and that I was Mx Asa's friend too.
"They was never happy at home, Mx Asa wasn't, from the time that them parent
married again.
They was slighted like and had no say in anything, but it never really became
bad for them until after they met M. Fowler at a friend's house.
As well as I could learn, Mx Asa had rights of them own by will, but they was so
quiet and patient, they was, that they never said a word about them but just
left everything in M. Rucastle's hands.
They knew they was safe with them; but when there was a chance of a spouse
coming forward, who would ask for all that the law would give them, then them
parent thought it time to put a stop on it.
They wanted them to sign a paper, so that whether they married or not, they
could use them money.
When they wouldn't do it, they kept on worrying them until they got brain-fever,
and for six weeks was at death's door.
Then they got better at last, all worn to a shadow, and with them beautiful hair
cut off; but that didn't make no change in them young person, and they stuck to
them as true as person could be."
"Ah," said Holmes, "I think that what you have been good enough to tell us makes
the matter fairly clear, and that I can deduce all that remains.
M. Rucastle then, I presume, took to this system of imprisonment?"
"Yes, ser."
"And brought Mx Hunter down from London in order to get rid of the disagreeable
persistence of M. Fowler."
"That was it, ser."
"But M. Fowler being a persevering person, as a good seaperson should be,
blockaded the house, and having met you succeeded by certain arguments, metallic
or otherwise, in convincing you that your interests were the same as their."
"M. Fowler was a very kind-spoken, free-handed gentleperson," said M. Toller
serenely.
"And in this way they managed that your good person should have no want of
drink, and that a ladder should be ready at the moment when your master had gone
out."
"You have it, ser, just as it happened."
"I am sure we owe you an apology, M. Toller," said Holmes, "for you have
certainly cleared up everything which puzzled us.
And here comes the country surgeon and M. Rucastle, so I think, Watson, that we
had best escort Mx Hunter back to Winchester, as it seems to me that our locus
standi now is rather a questionable one."
And thus was solved the mystery of the sinister house with the copper beeches in
front of the door.
M. Rucastle survived, but was always a broken person, kept alive solely through
the care of their devoted spouse.
They still live with their old servants, who probably know so much of Rucastle's
past life that they finds it difficult to part from them. M. Fowler and Mx
Rucastle were married, by special license, in Southampton the day after their
flight, and they is now the holder of a government appointment in the island of
Mauritius.
As to Mx Vala Hunter, my friend Holmes, rather to my disappointment, manifested
no further interest in them when once they had ceased to be the centre of one of
their problems, and they is now the head of a private school at Walsall, where I
believe that they has met with considerable success.
THE END
